THE RED PAGE
The Tin Wreath
Henry
Lawson.
I claim to know “something good” for
The Bulletin’s Galvanised-iron Halo Stakes
for Australian Bards. If the race
goes by clever riming, I’m in-
clined to have a win and a place
on “Kodak,” and a saver on
O. C. Cabot. But if it is run to suit the
patriotic drum-bang rimer, then Hervey
and Bedford must be reckoned with. If the
nominations and acceptances show that
Dorothy McCrae is a trier, she may be with
them at the finish. If the value of the
stakes comprises only a tin head-bangle that
has been dust-catching in The Bulletin’s
office for ages, I’d give it to Hop’s Under-
study, and let the youngster amuse himself
with it as a hoop. If, as I think, the laurel
wreath is to go to the bard who is worthily
keeping up the reputation of Gordon, Ken-
dall, Paterson and co., who are Australian
bards, the tin ornament ought to be posted
right away to H. Lawson.
ANON.
The Bard of
the Bush.
When that wreath is burnished and made
fit for encircling the noble brow of the
chosen of Australia s bards,
I want to trot forward good
old Harry Lawson. Gordon,
Kendall and Daley were
poets, but not Australian—the scent of the
gum-leaves wasn’t pungent enough. Quinn,
Brunton Stephens and Essex Evaus wrote
poetry, but it wasn’t Australian. Ogilvie
wrote well and truly of the bush, but the
sentimentalism necessarily clinging to a
scion of the “ Bonnie Hoose o’ Airlie ” took
a fall out of him at intervals, much too fre-
quent for the yarran and spinifex. Pater-
son did well while he lasted—but his Pegasus
was a four-furlong weed. Scattered among
the others, as “ Gilrooney,” “ Milky White,”
etc., much could be gleaned; but gleaning
is slow work, only fit for non-unionists.
Grant Hervey is solid and stirring, and
Goodge jingles well. But Harry started
with, kept with us stirrup to stirrup, and
romps in a clear furlong in advance as the
Bard of the Bush. When he sang of the
time “ When the World was Wide,” he
showed that he was of the bush, for the bush
and with the bush ; and up to date he has
left not a line of verse to which the most
hypercritical could point and say : “That is
not of the bush ; that is not Australian.”
And Australian bushmen swear by Harry.
And when it comes to the truly pathetic,
where is all your poetry ? Not on the same
selection with that plaint of the bushman
whose happiest moments are embittered
with the suspicion that his “ Pants begin to
go.” You of the purple and fine linen can
form no idea of the eerie feeling that bailiffs
the bushman when he realises that his moles
have weakened in their bosom—when he
pays his best girl the homage that royalty
exacts, backing out of her smiling presence.
And when “ unmerciful disaster followed
fast and followed faster,” it was cheery old
Harry who slapped us on the back and said :
Do not whine.
Keep your smile in working order for the brighter
days to come;
If there is no corn in Egypt, surely Africa has
some.
When the bush is known and respected—
when its strong battlers’ set-backs and com-
pensations are writ large as a spur to those
who keep on leaning against posts—when a
liberty-loving united Australia looks back
with pride on the men who “ blazed the
track ” —then Henry Lawson will take his
rightful place as the Chief of Australia’s
CABER.
A Word for
“Gilrooney”
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” fits the tin millinery
on to the head of “Gilrooney. He “ has
the gifts of exquisite melody,
a fine imagination, a happy,
wholesome humor, a pas-
sionate love of humanity,
the broad, tolerant spirit of optimism, strik-
ing susceptibility to beauty in any shape, a
great capacity for taking pains—in short, all
the emotional-intellectual equipment of the
Celt, born and bred in the lonely but lovely
bush of Anstralia.”
Why not a
Tin Leaf Each?
I\e the final disposal of The Bulletin's
laurel wreath, I rise to say that it can’t be
done— intact. Justice can
only be dispensed by
handing the gewgaw
round in pieces. There-
fore, unsolder the wattle-daubs and pass ’em
on to Roderic Quinn, H. McCrae, Arthur
Adams, Dora Wilcox, Henry Lawson, “ Fur-
ness Born,” Mary Gilmore, C. G. A. Colies,
H. Church, AN ill Lawson, Essex Evans,
E. Dyson, and others — to a limited
number. Pass a leaf each to W. T. Goodge,
Frank Henty, “Kodak,” O. C. Cabot, E. S.
Emerson, Edmund Fisher, “Veldt,” O’Hara,
M. Forrest, Frank Bellman, and so on, as
far as the leaves will suffice. Randolph Bed-
ford and Grant Hervey are entitled to the
solder. “ Stargazer ” gets the paint. The
nail and odor of sanctity go to Berny
O’Dowd. Norman Lilley may claim the
void created—if included in the spoils.
To choose any Australasian bard for the
sole distinction of wearing The Bulletin’s
nebulous hat is an utter impossibility where
fairness is the essence of the distinction ; for
the styles of the various bards entitled to
some distinction vary so. Take the Lawsons,
for instance. Take Frank Henty’s nervy
style, and compare it with Grant Hervey’s high
faiutin, or Randolph Bedford’s more labored
efforts. Take Colles’ graceful gamut of
ink impressions, or W. T. Goodge's catchy
jingles—and where are you ? Is the Aus-
tralasian song-writer and Muse-wooer to be
judged worthy of being even the Red Page
laureate because he (or she) adopts one style,
say like Bernard O’Dowd, Hebblethvvaite,
and co. ? Certainly not—and more so, because
most Bulletin bards affect the style that
seems most likely of editorial acceptance in
the bean-raking sense. The Bulletin laureate
w r ould not be the chosen one of, say, The
Australasian’s readers, and vice versa.
Truth to say, Australia has no national
versifier worthy the sole distinction of
Laureate. Essex Evans’ “ Awake, Arisq ! ”
ode brought him into a lucky prominence
more through good fortune than remember-
some work. Grant Hervey’s stuff has a
melodious ring—likewise metallic, as though
prompted by the nibblings of the money-
bag. But enough ! Parcel the gewgaw,
and let contentment reign till some bard
buds forth from the core of some great
calamity—say Australia’s invasion by the
Japs. Most of the pi’esent claimants will
have been nipped off by the pinch of Time
and forgotten ere then. And since no par-
ticipant in the present scramble can claim
sole perpetual title to his (or her) tinny
cx-umb, at the editorial trump all pieces must
be returned. Then, when a Laureate does
reign o’er the bardlets of our Great Loan
Land, he may be crowned with a wreath
intact.
PERI WINKLE.
A Novel of the Sea
There is a satisfying epic largeness o:
treatment in Edward Noble’s novel,
The Grain Carriers (Blackwood). It is a
novel with a purpose—and a good purpose,
too —but Noble is too subtle a craftsman tc
subordinate his purpose to his yarn. The
story is of two grain carriers, which leave
’Frisco for London, and the voyages of these
rival ships is made the theme for a damning
indictment of the recent clumsy effort oi
England to fight the bounty-fed trade of hei
rivals by lowei’ing the load line—and en-
dangering the safety—of her own ships. Of
these grain carriers one of Noble’s characters
thus speaks:
“ There’s nothing particularly inspiring
about them, nothing to make your poets
squirm; they aren’t lofty and trim anc
dainty, as were the old-time ships ; theyTe
innocent of any attempt at skittishness—no
poles, no gilt work, no scroll or fancy
figures on the prow, just lead-colored tanks
with steel rigging and iron ratlines; all hard,
dividend-earning iron and steel. That’s
what I figure them at—iron tanks, bare to
the point of indecency. .. . Waal, who put
them up ? ”
“ My owners.”
“ Wrong there. It’s your nation that put
’em up. It’s your nation that is responsible
for their darned sloppiness—the nation that
has killed its farmers and turned its country
into a park ; that has forced its farm hands
to emigrate or get into the cities—a nation
that would turn them into Hell if it
thought it could get grain cheaper by
hustling them there. . . . The British nation
wants cheap bread. It’s nothing to her
how it comes. Gutter-tanks from the Black
Sea; gutter-tanks round the Horn. She
has killed her farmers. She wants grain,
and the Germans have got together a lot of
ships to carry it. Germany has subsidised
her ships; so has France ; so, too, will
America. . . . It’s a losing game for you men
of the sea. It stands to reason you’ll get
whipped. The cheap ships you’re building
won’t help you. The cheap crews you’re
carrying won't help you. Increase of load-
line won’t help you ; but it’s the dying kick
of your nation and you’d better come in out
of the rain. . . . And when you run things
fine at sea, you are playing a game in which
men’s lives are risked for gold.”
So these iron warehouses—these biscuit-
tins with rounded ends—stagger toward the
Horn in their losing fight with the other
nations to feed the country that can’t feed
herself. And the yarn is an interesting and
exciting one; and though, perfoi’ce, the
note is sometimes a little "forced, the book
remains one of the best sea-yarns since The
Nigger of the Narcissus. F2.
The Portrait of Christ
rnHE curious and interesting question
whether we possess a portrait of Christ
is considered by James Burns in The
Christ Face in Art (Duckworth, per Dymoek’s
Library, Sydney). Despite the extraordi-
nary variety of treatment by artists of all
ages of the Christ face, it is impossible
to overlook the fact that one distinct
type of feature recurs again and again.
Is this type of face an actual portrait or
merely a conventionalised ideal ? Burns, by
means of over sixty admirable pictures of
the face of Christ in art, provides the
material for an unbiassed judgment. First,
as to the historical probability, there is no
reason to postulate that no portrait of Christ
was ever drawn during the life of Jesus upon
earth ; though the likeness attributed to
St. Luke by that most unreliable of
witnesses, tradition, does not Hatter Christ,
nor show much skill on the part of the
artist. Tradition also acknowledges two
early photographs of Christ, produced, in one
instance, by the subject washing his face
and wiping it on a cloth, which thereupon
retained a perfect portrait of his features,
while in the well-known Veronica legend
that lady, seeing Jesus passing on his way to
Calvary, handed him her veil to wipe the
perspiration from his face, The veil
photographed the features, and, judging
from the illustration given of the im-
pression at St. Silvestro, Rome, it was a
remarkably vivid portrait of an intellectual
type. Unfortunately for these evidences of
miraculous photography, all these alleged
portraits have been dismissed by the higher
criticism of both Art and Theology as be-
longing to a much later date. But a general
resemblance of feature persists in most
of the paintings, dating from a type
found in the catacombs of Rome. The
question is whether this is a copy of an
original portrait made while Christ was alive
or from the recollection of Christ’s appear-
ance, or whether gradually an ideal type
of feature grew up and was accepted as
the conventional Christ. None of the
Evangelists mention Christ’s appearance.
And, following Jewish law, the Early
Church regarded it as impious to make any
image of Christ in his purely human aspect.
It was not till the fourth century that artists
began to pass from the classic, conventional
conception of Christ as the ideal or symbol
(as found in the catacombs) to what they
imagined to be his actual appearance.
“They first introduced the beard, then the
hair parted in the middle, and so gradually
there was created a conventional likeness,
which became stereotyped in the hands of
the Byzantine workers, and which has been
handed down, and more or less followed,
until the present day.”
So Burns comes to the conclusion that it
is highly improbable that any authentic like-
ness of Christ exists. Art is without direct
knowledge of his actual appearance.,
And a comparison of the many types of
face presented us by artists in this book
proves that there is little main agreement
upon the essential features. In fact, Christ
was painted as a Spaniard in Spain, as a
Venetian in Venice, as a Tuscan in Tuscany,
as a Fleming in Flanders, as a German in
Germany, as a Dutchman in Holland, as an
Englishman in England. Had a Chinaman
painted him he would have certainly showed
us a Chinaman. With a rarity remarkable
he is presented as a Jew.
Indeed, these portraits show, in the main,
little of that devotional feeling that presum-
ably inspired their makers. There is an
assortment of horror and brutality, of course
—blood and piercing thorns, which in
Quentin Matsys borders on the ludicrous.
Botticelli shows an affected Christ, Luini
a girlish Christ, Baroccio and Andrea del
Sarto a silly Christ, Maratti a merely hand-
some Christ, Rossetti a modern English-
man. Michael Angelo portrays Christ as
a muscular prize-fighter, Velasquez as
a commonplace man, H. van Eyck as
Buddha, J. van Eyck as Shakespeare, Mem-
ling as a Tartar, Rubens as a very
dead criminal. Madox Brown gives us a
Christ washing his Disciples’ feet, garbed
and looking like a servant girl ; Holman
Hunt—but everyone has seen Hunt’s bar-
baric conception. Probably the most in-
spired attempts at ideal portraiture are
da Vinci’s delicate “ Head of Christ,”
Raphael’s robust “Transfiguration,” and
Guido Reni’s wonderful “ Ecce Homo.”
The Simple Life
Trucking
Stock
I am awakened at 4 a.ra. by the sun, glaring
angrily over the horizon. Already the haze is
shimmering and the heat is unbearable. The
comparatively cool breath of night air has been
rudely dispelled by the dawn. The
ghastly, sickening heat is working
up to that stage of awfulness when
the despair of never-never seizes
you. A drought is on Out Back, and starving stock
is being feverishly hurried to the railway to be
trucked away to the mountains at “starving
stock’’ rates.
A mob of cattle from the Queensland border
and a big consignment of sheep on their last legs
have travelled in during the night, and 60 trucks
of them have to be ready by 5 in the afternoon.
Already I can hear the others stirring. I put on
a pair of dungarees, and hustle into the kitchen,
and, with a gibe at the yellow heathen who has
charge of the culinary department, I seize a hunk
of bread, and pour into a mug black tea which has
been stewing all night. I fill it up with water in
a futile endeavor to drown the tannic acid. The
dry Dread sticks in my throat, and I have a nasty
taste in ray mouth, but I wash 'em down with the
Chinaman’s horrible concoction.
The boss is now in the trap, yelling loudly and
impatientfy. I bolt the last crust and jump j n
We drive off, and at the corner of the street nic
up half a score of hungry-looking urchins ear-
armed with a substantial nulla. We allow’ then
about five seconds to climb aboard, and therethev
are hanging in festoons to the buckboard—am
tailboard—until our rattling vehicle is quite oh
soured. We are followed by seven lean and savage
mongrels, but good with stock. Their tongues
hang out, and they pant, for the temperature
is now close to 110°, and the dust rises from the
road in a yellow cloud. In half-an-hour we reach
the trucking yard, and all hands begin to push the
trucks into position. I am a sort of working
ganger. I strip off my shirt and tighten my belt
then I fasten-to the gangways and wrestle with
truck doors and revile the stiff fastenings. Thereis
no shade, and I am soon steaming with perspira-
tion. Then the boys begin to hustle up the stock"
The lean dogs fairly scream at them and bite tin-
boys yell, and the wild-eyed bullocks rush hi’thei
and thither. I rush round furiously here, there
and everywhere, waving a stick to turn refractory
brutes. The dust is raised in clouds, and the mil
now high in the heavens shrieks at us through the
haze. I hustle the bullocks on the gangways and
then pandemonium ! The boys come to’ the
attack like young demons in Hell, and belt and
prod with the waddies. A dog which has been
kicked on the side of the jaw howls dismally
The bullocks rush blindly, with slavering mouths'
and slip and kick the dust and muck all over us’
One goes down and madly kicks, and breaks his
leg. How we strain to extricate the brute— and
by H ! the heat is indescribable ! My scanty
clothes are wringing wet and my face black with
dirt. I have yelled so much that I can yell no
more. My tongue is dry as leather and my throat
raw.
Sixty trucks by five o’clock, and the tempera-
ture now 112°. A baresark fury seizes me. I
work like a devil doomed to eternal labor in the
turmoil of dust and smell, until we knock off for
breakfast. Surely I drink a gallon of tea.
The greasy crows sit on the rails and watch for
the slaughter. There is no time to smoke. I get
my shoulder against the buffer of a truck and
shove and shove. Then we start on the sheep.
The temperature is 115°, and the sun through the
dust is like a ball of fire. The inferno starts
again. Stupid, bleating sheep are urged up to the
races. The boys are more like imps of the devil
than ever as they wrestle with distressed mothers
bleating piteously for their lambs born overnight,
which I have knocked on the head as fast as I
could sort ’em out. They are too weak to travel,
so the crows—the devils! how they gloat!
Hour after hour I toil. At 4 o’clock the tempera-
ture has climbed up to 117°—high water mark.
The awfulness of that heat ! Imagine a score of
dim figures cursing, shouting, sweating and
hustling—the dog with the broken jaw in the
thickest of the fray—all mixed up in a cloud of
yellow dust. Five more trucks to go! How I
urge these boys ! even with vulgar threats of
violence.
Sharp at 5 the engine whistles along. The last
sheep is trucked, and the long day’s toil is over.
Dead to the world we mount the old buckboard,
and you wouldn’t know the torn and bedraggled
crowd and the exhausted dogs. We are silent till
we reach old Billy’s. The boss, without a word,
pulls up and we each drink one of the battered
pewters full of beer. There are occasions when a
beer is worth a guinea a time, and this is one of
them.
I go inside and have a shower and change my
shirt. You city coves don’t know the luxury of
a clean shirt ! As soon as the bullock-bell rings I
spar up to the festive board. Old Billy cuts off a
tremendous hunk of corned beef and piles on the
vegetables. “'Ere yer are, mate,’’ he says;
“there’s no backin’ yer cart ’ere.” After tea we
play crib and smoke.
At midnight, old Billy the publican roars out,
“ Bed O ! ” and shambles round to put out the
lights. We all leave the bar, and I creep into the
alley-way and there sweat like a ’possum in a
hollow log until the sun glares at me again in the
morning.
JOE ANDERSON.
With the Banks at her Mercy , by A.
Dennison (The Lockely Library, Sydney),
may have some interest for bank managers.
It is the story of a fraud worked on Aus-
tralian banks, but is a little too technical for
the general reader. All that need be said
of the book is fortunately said by the author.
“ The author claims that this is an ‘original’
story. It is quite different from ordinary
stories in subject, conception, plot, character
and treatment. It is terse and dramatic-
limited to the doings of two characters. . .
Sydney B. .T. Skertchlv, in The Story of the
Noble Opal (Flavelle, Brisbane), has witten an
elaborate and interesting account of this typical
Australian stone.
The thirtieth part of that useful work, The
Forest Flora of N.S. W., by J. H. Maiden, contains
a group of excellent photographs of our native
trees.

There can be no doubt that all Colonial residents greatly appreciated the recent
opportunity provided them for a week’s free “ Hair Drill,” for a most significant
reference was made to the very frank offer lately made in the columns of their paper.
This was only to be expected, for it is indeed a deplorable fact that residence m
foreign climes has a very detrimental effect upon the beauty, health, and conditions
of the hair. / . J
Men and women alike who dwell in the Colonies and Dependencies have long grieved
over the fact that the climate and circumstances act very adversely upon the hair, and
that, as a consequence, many scalp and hair defects become quickly apparent and very
pronounced. Unfortunately the use of most of the prevalent specifics is entirely
without benefit, for they are based generally upon vague knowledge of hair requirements
and are generally compounded of ingredients lacking in nutrition and culturative value.
These, whether taken internally or applied externally, have always proved dis-
appointing, and at times injurious both to the head, hair, and health. Still there is
one undoubted application of approved merit and reputation, and it cannot be gainsayed
that Edwards’ “ Harlene” is a real hair restorer, a real fault corrector, a real stimulant,
tonic, and preserver for the hair; whether it be affected with dryness, brittleness,
scurfiness, or greasiness; whether it shows signs of decay or evidenced by lack of bistre,
colour, or healthy and beautiful appearance, or whether its deterioration is marked by
greyness, patchiness, vacuum, or baldness. For many years has Edwards’ ' Harlene
held an envied and unrivalled reputation as a true specific for all untoward conditions ot
the hair, and the volume of praise accorded to this notable preparation confirm very
striking testimony from the highest quarter.
Royalties, dignitaries of the State, social leaders, ladies and gentlemen occupying
high positions, as well as the great general public, have used it, and always with the
most gratifying results, for the use of “ Harlene ” overcomes any tendency or condition
of hair weakness and disease. .
It actually drills and disciplines the hair into healthy condition, and the faith of the
proprietors in their preparation is abundantly shown by the very open offer they recently
made and which now, by popular request, they are repeating. Jf

PILLS
A CURED WOMAN
Relates her Intense Sufferings—how Doctors
Failed to do her any Good. She Finally Tried
Clements Tonic
Which Makes Permanent Cure.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.) ;
Here is a narrative which will assuredly put many a reader on the right track—supplied to us by
Mrs Annie Young of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt. It was at Mrs. Young’s present address, where
she has been living for the past eleven years, that our special correspondent was granted the inter-
view which appears hereunder
“It is thirteen years since I had the illness you refer to. It doesn t seem half so long ago, tor
everything is as fresh in my memory as though it was only yesterday.”
“ Were you experiencing any physical pain ? ” T , ,
“oh 1 don’t talk about it. Have you ever had neuralgia in the head ? I hope not, tor it was
something dreadful. Right across the forehead, through from one temple to the other, and up to the
top of my head, the most acute pain imaginable harassed me. The agony was so great that often I
imagined something was pressing on my brain, and I really used to think I would go mad with it.
It had such an effect on me, indeed, that even my sight went bad, and, as for the feeling in my eyes
—well they felt as if they were double the size they should have been. Ah! what desolate, melan-
choly days they were ! I’m sure I felt myself to be about the most unhappy woman in the world,
and 1 think I had occasion to be, in view of the fact that the outlook was so black,
“Do you mean that your prospects of getting strong again seemed remote? ” .
“ They did, indeed, for I had been attended to by several doctors, and the most they could do for
me was to give just a little relief now and then ; but there was nothing lasting about it, and: if I felt a
trifle better one day, I was sure to feel ten times worse the next, and the medicine, besides being so
ineffective was always disagreeable to take. A strange thing was that I felt always so worn-out,
drowsy and exhausted—as if I was only fit to go somewhere and lie down and die—yet, when I made
an effort to sleep, do you think I could ? Not a bit of it. I’d twist and turn nearly the whole night
through, and when at last I dosed off the dreams I had were fearful.” .
“ And you couldn’t get permanent relief from these symptoms through the medicine prescribed
for un f ortunate i y . But finally I got something to do me good. Clements Tonic ft was, and
the best’remedy I ever had in my life, too. Goodness, what a show it made of everything
else I had taken, for instead of only easing me a little occasionally, it went right to the base of my
infirmities, and lifted them out by the roots ! In addition to what I have already told you, I must
state that I was highly nervous—nobody could be more so, for I was actually afraid to stay in my own
house by myself, and was continually worrying myself about things that I imagined were going to
happen Trouble was always hanging over me, and I couldn’t get the idea out of my head+-not until
I had been taking Clements Tonic for a space of time, when, quite mysteriously, I ceased brooding
over such silly things, and my mind became as happy as the day. Oh ! it was wonderful what an
altered woman Clements Tonic made me. Instead of being anxious and timid all day, I got as happy
and cheerful as possible—nothing frightened me, and my nerves were steady. It is because I know
so many nervous women that I deem it my dpty to say what a splendid remedy Clements Tonic is for
that complaint, though, for that matter, it wap equally beneficial to me in othfijs-ways. I used to get
pains down the small of my back and about my shoulders, also palpitation arid pains in the region of
my heart, which made me think that something very serious was wrong with me there; but Clements
Tonic showed itself to be such a pain-removing medium that I can faithfully recommeqd it to all
sufferers. Its regulating influences on the heart were extraordinary, and for making one’s sleep sound
and peaceful the same remedy was the only one to meet with my requirements. I wish I had used it
months before, for plenty of people told me what a grand medicine Clements Tonic was. The least I
can do is to assist my neighbors to throw off their afflictions, so you may publish my experiences in
any manner you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I ANNIE YOUNG, ol Myrtle-street, Leichhardt, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a ’true and faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any w y my statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act 1900 (Sgd.) ANNIE YOUNG.
Subscribed and declared at Petersham this fifth day of September, one thousand nine hundred and four,
before me, H - ETANS * J.P.
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Lost Fairies.
They come no more with the dancing feet,
Where the daffodil chorus rang sweet, so sweet,
Fairies o’ mine, have ye fled for ever ?
Shall we meet no more as we used to meet ?
They come no more and the wheels run slow,
And the laughter is hushed that I used to know ;
The white owl cries in the twilit meadows,
Where our revels rang in the long ago.
O ! a fairy came knocking one day, one day,
At the meadow-sweet gates where we used to
play—
I heard him knock, but my heart was weary,
And I sent him weeping away, away.
And ever since then, though my heart be sore
With waiting and watching, they come no more ;
And the lilies have stolen their golden sandals,
And the poppies are flaunting the gowns they
wore.
Ah ! Ever since then i’ the noon o’ the flowers,
When the lights are soft in the fairy bowers,
I sigh and sigh for the banished laughter,
For the singing soul of the wasted hours.
Do they mourn me, I wonder, as one that passed
While the sentinel snapdragons slumbered fast?
Or is it they seek me, all loyal-hearted,
And dream they shall find me, at last, at last ?
I know not; ever the red suns rise
And roll to their rest in the western skies,
But the loved lost voices are silent, silent—
And leaps no light to the darkened eyes.
Only when Twilight lifteth her wand
And turns the glory to shadow-land
I hear in the stillness a sound of weeping—
And know the meaning, and understand.
They have passed the boundaries mortals know,
Where the asphodel blooms and the dream-stars
glow,
Though I seek them, seek them till suns be
ashes,
I shall never find them wherever I go.
They will come no more with the dancing feet.
Where the daffodil chorus rang sweet, so sweet ;
Where the white owl cries in the haunted
meadows
We shall meet no more as we used to meet.
VIC.
M. E. J. PITT.
The Wageman.
With mind that never wonders why
The Fates but gave him leave to moil,
The wageman lifts his pick on high.
And labors through his daily toil.
Long years have shackled him and held
Him hopeless to the wheel of strife.
He is content so he has quelled
The calling needs of child and wife.
The gaudy cars flash quickly past —
He asks no question of his God.
“ So it will be unto the last,”
And bends above the yawning sod.
The straying kine along the street
Share board with him ; he does not f mi
It strange that they should cross his feei,
And sniff above his mid-day meal.
The years that lead him to the grave
No visioned fields of pleasure yield.
Nor stilly lakes whose waters lave
Pond lilies bursting from the shield ;
But when the twilight woos the night,
And from the skies the white stars peer,
He seeks the welcome tavern light,
And broods above his pot of beer.
TAS.
C. LOUIS RANDALL.
Hallet's Cove.
You ask me where I went, sweetheart, last Sunday
afternoon ?
I wandered down to Hallet’s Cove
And mused beside the ti-tree grove.
A pretty yacht approached the spot,
And anchored off the dune.
Three maidens and three men rowed in
And leaped upon the shore ;
And two went slowly down the strand,
And other two went hand-in-hand
Round to the right, and out of sight,
As lots have done before.
But one came pacing solemnly, all lonely and
demure,
Leaving the other standing there
To kick his heels and chafe and stare,
And smoke and fret and fume and sweat,
(And swear, you may be sure !)
But when the one had strolled a mile (a mile but
barely half)
She stopped beside a shelt’ring rock ;
(She wore a pretty “Sky-blue frock),
Then down she sat,
Took off her hat
(And I could hear her laugh !)
And then came striding up amain, the one who
chafed and swore,
And sate him down beside of her
And seemed to ask her, as it were,
What HE had done, beneath the sun !
(And quite a great deal more !)
I don’t know what the maiden said, but presently
she took
His head between her hands, like this,
And gave him kiss and kiss on kiss !
How many ? Well! I cannot tell!
1 didn’t like to look !
S.A

Hour to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige, the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods o f address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojficials. The person may not be there ; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, APRIL 30, 1908.
THE FEDERAL CITY, AND SYDNEY’S
NEED FOR A NEW CUSTOMER.
During last week The Bulletin received a rather
stained postcard from (of all places) Bull’s Creek,
in South Australia, stating that the Federal
capital question threatened to become a weari-
ness, and urging that a change of subject was
desirable. And to this the only possible reply is
that, even if the Federal capital question becomes
a weariness twenty times over, it is a matter of
such vast importance that it can’t be allowed to
rest. In the selection of this capital, Australia
has a chance to make history as it was never
made before. It has an opportunity to do some-
thing that has no precedent in all the world’s
records, from the days of Adam onwards. Never
previously, so far as we are aware, has a free
people set out to create a city that was to be the
capital of a whole continent, and that a con-
tinent that was wholly under one Govern-
ment, and occupied by people of one race
and one language. The event is unique,
and an effort should be made to realise
the greatness of the occasion and live up to it.
As a rule the men who are mixed up, for good or
evil, in the greatest events of history fail to realise
the size and importance of their doings. Judas
Iscariot, when he broke faith and sold his
Master, probably didn’t know that he was doing
anything more important than George Reid did
when he broke faith with the other States and
sold Australia over the matter of the Federal
capital site. Pontius Pilate, on the one im-
portant day of his life, probably felt as if he had
merely done an ordinary morning’s work, the
same as any other magistrate might do, and didn’t
feel even so abject as Pontius Johnson,
M.H.R., ought to do when he howls in the
press for the subordination of the interests
of a whole continent to the overwhelming
necessity for having the political centre (if
Australia in his own electorate of Lang (N.S.W.).
The small men—the Pilates and Johnsons and
so on —who are permitted by an inscrutable
Providence to fool with great events, view these
events from a purely twopenny or three-halfpenny
standard, and, as a rule, they don’t live long
enough to know that they were unconsciously
making or marring history. The parochial-minded
Mohammedan ass who destroyed the library of
Alexandria probably felt just as complaisant over
his work as will the allegedly Christian but
wholly parish ass of this country, if he succeeds in
destroying something of even greater importance —
the chance of making the world’s one continental
capital a place worthy of the continent it represents.
For the sake of beauty and cleanliness and
other advantages, the first idea of the Builder of a
City is to place his work beside a river—a large
and beautiful river, if possible, but at all events
a river. Even the person who built Damascus, in
the days when Baby Genesis was in its cradle, and
Exodus was still unborn, knew that much. The
fact was so obvious that it obtruded on the popu-
lar intelligence before Abraham was heard of.
And this fact obtrudes especially in Australia,
where water is scarce, and rivers, in proportion to
the size of the country, are few. Yet Australia
has some rivers to be proud of. The Victoria,
Daly, and Roper, in the Northern Territory, are
sizable water courses. There are five reasonable
streams flowing into the Gulf of Carpentaria. The
Burdekin, Fitzroy, Mary, etc., in eastern
Queensland are respectably wet. There are
two or three notable bits of water in Westralia.
In N.S.W. the Clarence and Macleav have
points about them, and the Murray-Darling
represents length without breadth —it is the Big
Trickle that the dwellers by its banks call it.
The Snowy is the best of the "N.S.W. streams for
purity, for scenery, and for the unfailing nature
of its supply ; in the great drought year of 1902 it
COTTER CREEK,
Which Premier Wade, of N.S. W., thinks good enough for the water supply of an Australian
Capital at Canberra. Even this is more than a dozen miles away from the proposed city, which,
as far as scenery is concerned, will be quite dry.
was the only river in the State which showed no
failure or diminution. And away in the centre of
this continent the Finke roars along (in modera-
tion) and foams (to a certain extent) till it loses
itself in the sands. Its career is short, but while
it lasts it is a river that (in its better moments) is
worthy to have a big centre of population on its
banks, and the unbuilt Finke City might have
been a fairly good capital.
* ** * * •
Yet by a curious and insane perversity or mal-
evolence or cussedness, we find the press of Sydney
crying out almost unanimously for a Dry Capital.
In Australia, as in all drought-cursed countries,
water is the first essential, and the man who
builds even a hamlet away from water, when he
can build alongside an ever-flowing stream, seems
the last possibility in the way of a raving lunatic.
All the same, the N.S.W. State Government, and
the Foreign-trade papers which back it up in its
unaccountable vagaries, have fixed on Canberra as
the ideal place to be the world’s one really conti-
nental city. The Canberra site is about five
miles from a small creek, and 13 miles from
a rather larger creek. It has no river or
stream or rivulet in sight—in fact, it seems to have
been selected in order that its drouth may be a
voice howling in the ear of the visitor from
afar, and telling him that this is a dry country
with the spectre of Thirst brooding over it at all
times. Mr. E. M. de Burgh, a N.S.W. engineer,
was put on last year to report on the possibilities
THE OTTAWA RIVER,
Alongside which Canada built its Capital.
of water supply for this Dry Spot, and the best
suggestion he could make was that water should
be pumped up to a height of over 500 ft. to supply
the city. When the pumps fail the supply will
naturally stop. The insanity of deliberately
building a city which, according to the best method
engineers can discover, is to depend on pumps for
every drop of its water, when sites can be found
to which a splendid flow will come, day
and night, all the year round, simply because
water always takes a downhill course, ought to be
fairly obvious. And the folly of creating a city
where there is no moisture within miles to
brighten the landscape, when lovely water scenery
can be found not far off, would have seemed like
madness even to the builders of Damascus. They
at least had sense enough to keep an eye on
Abana and Pharpar for a drinking supply, and as
a means of irrigation, and as something that was
calculated to brighten the surroundings, and make
the desert blossom like the rose.
Still the Dry Spot, far removed.from any visible
sign of water, where a wretched city is to depend
for a drink on having its fluid pumped up to a
height of over 500 ft., has been made into a sort of
gospel by the Sydney daily press. Australia is
full of dry spots, but for the sake of keeping up
the perpetual quarrel with the Australian Govern-
ment, this particular Dry Spot has been made a
fetich. Its visible advantages, in the view of the
anti-Australian press, are these : (1) A capital built
there will be, by reason of its disadvantages, a
small, mean city, which will always be over-
shadowed by the State capitals, therefore it will
be a constant evidence of the triumph of State
Frights over Federal interests; (2) it can have
no port, so it will be kept poor and humble; and
(3) it will be wholly surrounded by N.S.W. State
territory, and will be dependent on the forbear-
ance of the State for road and railway communi-
cation and for water supply, therefore it will be
kept weak and dependent. Also (4) if the anti-
Australian anti-Federal gang can force on Aus-
tralia as a capital its particular Dry and Un-
promising Spot, it will have the satisfaction
of insulting and humiliating the Government
of Australia. Even if the capital were
fixed at a Still Drier and More Undesirable Spot,
so long as it wasn’t the anti-Australian party s
special spot, the triumph wouldn’t be complete.
• * * *
Yet all this time it is practically certain that a
Federal capital on the Snowy River would be
vastly more profitable to N.S.W. than one at
Canberra. There is a chance, by reason of the
splendid water-supply and the magnificent water
power, to raise a great and noble city beside the
Snowy, and the bigger the city the more business
would there be to be done. The capital would
have its own Federal port at Twofold Bay, but
that would benefit Sydney rather than injure it.
The vessels that went in there must come from
somewhere, and the vessels that left there must
go somewhere. Most of them would come from
Sydney* or go to Sydney, as the nearest coastal
city. It would be a hideous loss to -Sydney’s
commerce if Melbourne were destroyed, for then
all the Sydney shipping that exists by trading
with Melbourne would become a mere bad debt,
and most of the £2,000,000 worth or so of goods
which N.S.W. sends annually to Victoria would
be unsold ; and it will be the same loss to Sydney
if the Federal capital on Monaro and the Federal
port on Twofold Bay are never called into exist-
ence. It would be a vast financial disaster to the
N.S.W. railways if the big city of Melbourne dis-
appeared off the map, and it will be the same kind
of loss if the big city that is capable of growing up
on the Snowy doesn’t come on to the map. In a
certain sense it is gratifying to the N. S. Waler to
know that Sydney is more populous than Mel-
bourne, and that N.S.W. has more people than
Victoria, but that kind of gratification isn’t busi-
ness. If Victoria, with lj million people, buys about
£2,000,000 worth of N S. W. goods annually, there is
always a fair chance that Victoria with six million
people would buy £8,000,000 worth of goods. On
this same basis it is to the sordid interest of
Sydney to have the Australian capital planted
where "it will have the best possible chance to grow
big, and to give it every opportunity, by furnish-
ing it with all the room for expansion that it
wants and all the sea-outlet that it desires, to grow
as big as possible. It is absurd to say that Dalgety is
too far away for Sydney’s trade to reach it, when
Sydney sends so vast a quantity of goods to Mel-
bourne, which is twice as remote. And while it
may suit the petty silly vanity of politicians
like Johnson to clamor for a small, mean,
unpopulated Australian capital on some Dry
Spot where there is no hope of expansion,
it is up to his constituents to remember
that Johnson has no goods to sell, and it is all
the same to him whether there are 20,000 people
in the new city, or 150,000. And it is good enough
for Premier Wade, with only his political dignity
to consider, to denounce the absurdity of the pro-
posed Federal capital at Dalgety with a Federal
port on Twofold Bay ; Wade has no ships that
want a new port to trade to, and he isn’t
even a seaman, or a lumper, or a ship’s carpenter.
In their silly little parish-mindedness these
Sydney politicians hold that Sydney would rather
have a small customer than a big one, and that
Sydney shipowners don’t want new ports to trade
with. So they clamor for the small capital on the
Dry Spot.
* * * *
Australia should aim at having the most
glorious capital it can raise, merely by reason of
patriotism and national pride. It should aim at
being able to take the foreign visitor there with
satisfaction, instead of being reduced to getting a
bogus medical certificate to say it is too ill to
travel in order to avoid accompanying its foreign
guest to the dreadful place. But if part of
Australia isn’t capable of being influenced by
these considerations, that objectionable section of
the community should at least think of business.
It should have ambitions for a new city to which
McMillan can send his fluff-traveller with profit
and Toohey his beer-traveller with remuneration!
It should remember that the A.U.S.N. boats can’t
go up Cotter Creek, and that there is no money in
loading the ship that is going to Canberra, or re-
pairing the vessel that comes back from
Lyndhurst. And when Wade and Johnson
holler for a hopeless little city in a Dry Spot, it
should always remember that Wade has no fluff to
sell and Johnson has no beer to dispose of. These
are sordid considerations, but even sordid con-
siderations can’t be wholly ignored.
ABOUT THE ALLEGED FREETRADE
OF N.S.W.
A correspondent writes with an axe dipped in
blood :
I read and studied the statement of vour
friend, Mr. McLachlan, in last week’s Bulletin
to this effect:—
Knowing- as I do (as a Protectionist) that the other
States had for years the run of our open markets, with
which to build up their industries, whilst their ma'rkets
were closed to N.S. W., I challenge The Bulletin to prove
that this State has, by Act of legislation or commercial
intrigue, ever done one action to the prejudice of the
other States, or any of their inhabitants.
Now I think it is up to some one to reply to that
monstrous piece of cheek and bluff, and here is my
reply
The Queensland people grew sugar, and wanted to sell it
to “ Free trade ” N.S.W.—it was the only thing of any very
great importance that they had to sell. Did they find the
open market that Mr. McLaculan shouts about? Not
much 1 They were stunned by a duty of £5 per ton put
on to protect the N.S. W. sugar industry. It was at long
last reduced to £3 a ton.
The Queenslanders had timber to sell. Right up to
1896, Freetrade N.S.W. met them with all sorts of duties
—3s. per 100 ft. on hardwood, Is. Od. per 100 ft. on cedar,
and so on.
The Victorians and S. Australians were great cn wine.
Freetrade N.S. W. met them at the border with a Pro-
tective duty of ss. per gallon.
In Northern Victoria (Wangaratta, Beechworth, Bright,
etc.) they can make a splendid beer with the mountain
water. They tried to send it into “ Freetrade ” N.S.W.,
and found Ma State waiting for them at the border with
a Protective duty of 6d. per gallon.
Victoria got early into the bacon, cheese, ham and
butter business. “Freetrade” N.S.W. met them with
Protective duties of 3d., 2d., 2d., and Id. per lb. respect-
ively, and kept these duties on till its own industries
were well established.
Victoria was ahead of N.S.W. in the tobacco industry.
“ Freetrade ” N.S.W. put a huge Customs duty on
imported tobacco and a very small excise duty (for a long
time it had no excise duty at all) on local tobacco.
S. Australia had a salt industry. “ Freetrade ” N.S.W.
put 20s. per ton Protective duty on salt.
Except for the period between 1896 and 1901 the
“ Freetrade ” policy of N.S.W. consisted mostly in letting
in free the things that Europe and Asia had to sell, and
levying Protective duties on the few things the neighbor-
ing States had to sell. And even in the 1896-1901 period the
enthusiasm with which N.S.W. protected itself against
Queensland sugar, and the wine, beer and tobacco of
Victoria and S.A., was marvellous. True, it offered us a
free market for tea, coffee, jute, cotton, and so on, but
we didn’t produce these things.
N.S.W. carried its own newspapers free, while those of
the adjacent States had to pay £d. postage within its
borders —this in order to protect its own mostly-
Freetrade press. Victoria never got down to this level.
It was N.S.W. which caused the break of gauge in Aus-
tralian railways. In 1852 an Act was passed in Sydney
fixing sft. Sin. as the standard gauge. South Australia
and Victoria agreed to follow suit. Then, when these
States had let contracts on the sft. 3in. gauge, N.S.W.
changed to the 4ft. B£in. gauge without any reference to
the other States —just did it in an offhand way, as if it
were the whole show —and chaos set in all round.
It was N.S.W. which destroyed the old system of
border freetrade with Victoria. There was freetrade
on the border for a number of years, but N.S.W.,
in its usual large way, demanded that Victoria
should pay a big annual subsidy if the arrangement was
to continue, and failing that (in 1864) it built Custom
houses and commenced to collect duties. In 1865 Vic-
toria agreed to pav the subsidy, and there was freetrade
on the border till* 1873. Then N.S.W. altered the tariff
(which had been a matter of mutual arrangement) with-
out consultation with Victoria, just as it altered the rail-
way gauge, and chaos again set in.
In addition to its tariff Protection, N.S.W’. always pro-
tected its industries in a large, general way, by a system
of differential railway rates.
* * * *
Mr. McLachlan makes a huge fuss about how the other
States had the run of N.S.W. territory, while shutting
their own territory against N.S.W. Well, here are some
figures of trade between N.S.W. and Victoria, as set
out on page 130 of the “ Wealth and Progress of N.S.W.”
for 1901. lam sorry they are not more complete
N.S.W. Imports N.S.W. Exports
from Victoria. to Victoria.

Thus in every year, save 1898, Victoria took more from
N.S.W. than N.S.W. did from Victoria. And even in
1898 Victoria took more goods from N.S.W. than N.S.W.
did from Victoria ; of the £5,301,214 worth which N.S.W.
took from Victoria £3,489,150 was gold. This was the

That was the real situation, during the one year on the
list published by the N.S.W. Statistician, when it aw
seem that Victoria was indebted to N.S.W. for ben g
good customer. t
Now remember, I don’t object to the N.S.W. tariff, or
the N.S.W. differential railway rates. Victoria n ~
no right to object to these things, tor "hey
all in the game—the game it played itself. What
many others object to is the insufferable and ln !f rn d
of patronage assumed by some Freetraders and Tree
newspapers in Sydney. While for years and y e *J®
taxed and protected almost everything we Victorian
to sell them, they called themselves Freetrader •
They professed to look down on us as P j
benighted Protectionists who hadn’t their bea
Freetrade gospel. They lectured us ; I have seen aDOu
30 scoffs at Victoria in one issue of Sydney Teles
While we were admitting so much of their gooas >
or at a very low tariff, that they, almost every yea >

acceptance of the first Federal Constitution, and got a new
Constitution fixed up w hereby the capital was allotted to it.

N.S.W. Assembly to practically fix the site of the capital,
or to veto the site chosen by the Parliament of
Australia. But the parental and superior attitude of
people who talk about having given us free access to
their markets, when they know' quite well that they did
nothing of the kind, gives us the tired sensation. And
what do these alleged Freetraders gain by it? As far as
I can see —nothing, save the pleasure of being coarse and
ill-bred, and the satisfaction of bounding a great deal.
THE POST OFFICE.
How much can be done towards smothering
reason by mere vigor of assertion is shown by the
general acceptance as truth of the charge of
“Federal postal maladministration.” Because it
has been said so often that Federation has brought
chaos to the Post Office, most people are inclined
to believe that it is so ; even those in the position
to know better are half convinced that there is
something very scandalous in the fact that a few
little hitches have marred a truly marvellous
record of postal progress and economy. In the
recent Parliamentary debates on the Post Office,
it was hardly questioned that the administration
needed mercy rather than justice. Yet an impar-
tial investigation of the facts suggests that no
special pleading is needed to make out a case for
the much-abused department; that, given bare
justice, it will escape censure and deserve praise.
Primarily, of course, the Post and Telegraph
Department should be an instrument of civilisa-
tion and good government. Considering the
help that a good postal and telegraph service
gives to education, to the promotion of rural
settlement, to the preservation of public health,
to the maintenance of the Jaw, a community can
very well afford to face a small loss on postal
transactions. Note the growth of the functions
of the Post Office since Federation, and the con-
sequent growth of its incidental services to the
community:—
Pre- Post-
Federation, Federation,
1901. 1906.

value £8,275,354 £10,049,062
Postal Notes, number .. 3,695,952 6,267,569

Kevenue £2,372,861 £3,130,034

Newspapers are excluded from this comparison
because, whilst the Federation has enormously
decreased the general charges on newspapers, it
insisted on abolishing the special Protective free
postage enjoyed in N. S. Wales by the morning
Freetrade newspapers, and consequently those
journals now employ carriers to do much of the
work which the Post Office used to perform
gratuitously. A comparison of telephones is not
possible owing to the fact that the States did not
separate their telephone and telegraph figures.
But the increase in telephone business has been
in correspondence with the enormous increase of
facilities and reduction of rates. A proportion, a
small proportion, of this general increased use of
postal and telegraphic and telephonic facilities is,
of course, due to the natural increase of population.
But most of it is to be ascribed to the radical
reforms in Post Office administration effected by
the Federation. The Commonwealth found the
Post Office lagging a good deal behind the current
of modern progress. It has brought it, in regard
to rates and facilities, well to the van. Telegraph
rates, for instance, have been reduced by the
Federation by from 33 to 75 per cent, throughout
the Commonwealth. Compare some of the tele-
graph charges

In the postal rates there have been also large
reductions. Victoria has had since Federation
(the change was made just at the dawn of union
so that the Commonwealth would have to find the
money) a penny postage system within its borders.
Ihe Australian rate to Great Britain has been
reduced from 2Jd. to 2d. per half oz. Interstate
newspaper postage has been enormously reduced ;
a paper is carried now for £d., whereas the pre-
federation rate in some cases was Fees on
postal orders and postal notes have been cut down
ny as much as 50 per cent.
These huge reductions of rates have not, as is
represented in some quarters, been matched by
reductions in salaries and wages. The lower-class
employees have, by the “ minimum wage ” pro-
vision of the Commonwealth Public Service Act,
been assured of at least £llO a year when they
reach the age of 21. This clause alone involved
wage increases totalling £190,362 a year. Summar-
ising roughly, postal employees, since Federation,
lave been better off as regards wages in most
7 ," ateB > though a little worse off in Victoria. But
be condition of the lower-grade employees has
been everywhere improved.

In Australian State administration it is a fre-
Que.nt b'istom to found an apparent reform on a
oasis of borrowed money. Rates are reduced and
ages increased in a service, and the deficit thus
'mated is met out of loan funds. It has not
p ee " 'With the Federal administration of the

rned into a profit in 1906, not a single penny of
1 uiutiey has been expended from first to

It is a record that the Commonwealth has every
reason to be proud of. It is not, however, a
record that is put forward to justify any failure to
correct obvious faults in administration which
have developed. In the quick advance from an
old-fashioned and expensive to a modern and
cheap system there have been mistakes. The
staff has had to suffer unduly from over-
work. Effective decentralisation, though often
ordered by Ministerial minute, has never
really been secured. Now and again political
influence has been allowed to intrude mis-
chievously. Mistakes have been made in the Post
Office censorship. These faults exist, and should
be remedied (as regards most of them, the Post-
master-General can be credited with a sincere
desire to see them remedied). But even if they
are viewed through the most unfair of magnifying
glasses, they cannot be made big enough to over-
lap the essential fact that the Federal Post Office
has been one of the great blessings of the union.
It has multiplied facilities by two, it has in many
branches divided charges by three ; and it stands
self-supporting without calling for a single cent of
loan money for renewals or new works. It would
be a good thing for Australia if all State depart-
ments were managed as well.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Scheme that. Fails.
There is every reason to believe that *he con-
spiracy which blocked the Capital Site Bill the
other night will fail badly. There had been an
anxious counting of head's on that memorable
Ihursday, and it was found that a small majority
for Dalgety was assured. So the party that wants
a small, mean capital which won’t overshadow
the provincial cities, did as does the gambler who
upsets the table when he finds the game going
against him. There arose Webster, ofN.S.W.,
with what was practically a vote of censure',
and no minute in all the year would do for
Webster and his bogus Post Office grievance
except that minute when the vote was about to be
taken which would knock, Webster’s Canberra
capital into a cocked hat. With the aid
of Cook, Reid, and others the Capital Site Bill
was side-tracked by Webster’s sudden and
incurable grievance. Then the Sydney Telegraph
found its usual correspondent, who wrote to it
“ Again Melbourne has been too clever for us
and its usual Johnson, M.H.R., who wrote
to say bitterly that in the matter the
Federal city, N.S.W. could obviously Hope for
no justice from this Parliament. Yet, after all
this worry and scheming, there is reason to believe
that the scheme will fail. The obvious idea was
to postpone the capital question till next session
or next Parliament, in the hope that a Can-
berra majority would be found somewhere
in the meantime, and meanwhile to raise
the usual clamor to the effect that N.S.W.
was again the victim of a conspiracy of the
other States. But it is practically certain that
the Capital Site Bill will come on this session after
all, and that the idea of hanging it up till a
Canberra majority can be got together will fall
through.

Johnson’s Capital.
Last week the dreadful Johnson, M.H.R. for
Lang (N.S.W.), wrote to Sydney Telegraph a
long letter about the selection of the Australian
capital. In that letter he contrived, with marvel-
lous ingenuity, to avoid a single reference to the
interests, or the rights, or the claims, or the people
of Australia. There wasn’t in it one word about
the advisability of selecting a good site, a place
where there is abundance of water and water
power, where the climate is healthy, where the
scenery is something for Australia to be proud of,
where it will be possible to raise a great and rich
and beautiful city, worthy of its dignity as the one
place in all the world that is the'capital of
a whole continent. These things never struck
Johnson. The Australians are proud of their
country, their race, and their future; and
when they take the casual English or
Yankee or Canadian visitor to their metropolis
they would fain take him to some place that isn’t
a mere dry little town without even a creek with-
in seven miles of it, as would be the case with the
proposed capital at Canberra—they would like to
show him a city that he couldn’t truthfully declare
to be a poor, one-horse town worthy of a poor,
one-horse race. That is the first idea that
would strike the man who is a real Aus-
tralian, not a mere parish Bumble, pottering
over little local grudges and tenth-rate village
animosities. But it didn’t strike Johnson.
Johnson’s attitude was purely local, and
wholly confined to his own alley. In his
long letter he mentioned New South Wales
seven times, “our own State” (N.S.W.) once,
Sydney three times, George’s River once, National
Park once, Botany once, Cronulla Beach
once, Kurringai Chase once, Gosford once,
Hawkesbury River once, Illawarra once, Mel-
bourne (as a malignant place that was always
trying to do something wicked and horrible) five
times, and Victoria (as a locality full of baneful
influences) five times. Queensland, 8.A., Tas-
mania, Westralia, weren’t mentioned at all. And
of Australia, our mother-land, the only continent
which was ever peopled by one race, speaking one
language, and living under one free Government
—about its rights, its hopes, its prospects, and
its future, the petty, wretched Johnson had
nothing to say. He never even mentioned Aus-
tralia in connection with the choice of Australia’s
capital. Apparently the poor little creature had
never heard of his own great country. There are
2,972,000 square miles of Australia, and all N.S.W.
and Victoria put together make less than 400,000
of these, but Johnson, dealing with the question
of the capital of this great continent, could think
of nothing but the bogus “wr«ngs” of the Ma
State, and the alleged machinations of some
people at Melbourne. He told the old lie about
the people of N.S.W. having: rejected the first
Constitution (they really accepted it, but their
acceptance was annulled by a petty act of
treachery on the part of the State Parlia-
ment). He represented the people of N.S.W.
as a crowd of dirty little schemers who,
after their State had started the Federa-
tion movement, wouldn’t go on with it
unless they were bribed by being promised
possession of the capital. He told a garbled
story about something, that he said was privately
decided, re the location of the capital, at a con-
ference of six State Premiers, as if the Australian
people were bound by any alleged agreement that
was never accepted by them or even submitted
to them for acceptance. And he said that it
was now the business of the people of N.S.W. to
get the capital fixed as near to Sydney as the Con-
stitution would allow. There was no mention about
selecting the best capital site in the interests of
N.S.W.—of which, in a population sense, Sydney
only represents one-third. And as for any attempt
to seleqt the best capital site in the interests of
Australia, Johnson never alluded to Australia.
W hat he did most enthusiastically recommend was
a capital in, or close to, his own electorate, down by
historic ” Botany. He didn’t say that Botany
was the sort of place where we could grow a
capital that would be wprthy of Australia, but he
said it was “ historic.” Also, it is convenient to
his own electorate. The selection of an Aus-
tralian metropolis is a unique event, for never
before is there any record of a people setting out
to build a political centre for a whole continent.
And into this matter, which is a matter of some
size, and worthy of consideration from the highest
patriotic motives, intrudes .Johnson, who appa-
rently never heard of Australia, clamoring for a
capital at Botany adjacent to his own garbage tin.
The Ruins of Sydney.
Dear Bulletin, —As one who is cursed with a
strange, weird detestation of the glorious tenets
of Freetrade, I rejoice to note the ruin—the
damnable and devastating Ruin—with which Pro-
tection has blighted your fair city of Sydney. I
return after an absence of eight years (a period
curiously close to the time during which Sydney
has groaned under the tyranny of a tariff), and
what do I find ? I find so much ruin, masquerad-
ing in the guise of an unholy Prosperity, that I
am constrained to wonder how you can stand it,
instead of rushing into the gory battlefield behind
Johnson and Joe Cook, and flinging away your
lives, or at least your livelihoods, in the sacred
cause of the foreigner. The first thing that struck
me, after leaving Owe’Sullivan’s new railway
station, was the happy and contented look of the
S. M. Herald and the Daily Kellygraph. For
journals that have been pining away for eight
years in the cold, moist shadow cast by an unfeel-
ing Federation, aggravated by Protection, they
seem remarkably well-nourished. Ruin seems
to agree with them, while it has—paradoxically
enough—caused a great distension in the size and
advertising revenue of the Evening News. It isn’t
only the papers that Federation and a tariff have
ruined so satisfactorily. They have provided vast
material for far-in-tne-future Ruins in the New
Sydney which I see rising out of the Old—those
huge, ornate, solid buildings like Challis House,
the Bank of Australasia, Hordern’s, Grace
Bros., and a score more. And the population—
the people whom G. Reid prophesied as pattering
about in bare feet, or words to that effect—how
nobly they are bearing up against the Ruins
which daily encompass tliem ! “Doing the
Block,” the other afternoon, I noticed with dis-
may that the girls were still there—only more so !
A bewildering procession of youth and beauty
promenaded past me for three mortal hours, and
I am perfectly certain that Solomon and the
Queen of Sheba in all their glory would not have
attracted extra attention in that crowd. Why,
even Melbourne, where this kind of Ruin actually
receives encouragement, could not have excelled the
spectacle—indeed I’ve serious doubts whether it
could have equalled it. And the Ruin in the
shops! They were packed with it; the most
beautiful and expensive kind of Ruin you could
think of—silks and satins, and jewels, and knick-
knacks, and gorgeous bonnets, and stupefying
“creations,” while the places where they sell
small lingerie blazed like perpendicular flower-
beds. And down the bay, and all along the
Harbor—which Bruce Smith said was to be a
silent Desolation, set round three shags and a
solitary fisherman I found traces of this
awful Ruin in the splendid, flaming terraces
of new villas crowning the heights along
North Shore, Mosman’s Bay, Chowder Bay,
and Middle Harbor; while all the ferry boats
seemed to have contracted Ruin badly—so much
larger, swifter, more luxurious and numerous were
they than in the days of Separatism and Free-
trade. And I went out and had a look at the
Ruin of Manly and the Ruin of Watson’s Bay,
and a lot more Ruins, each more Ruinous than the
last; and what with new houses and newly-built
pubs, and new parks and swimming-baths, and
gardens, and statues, and motor-boats, and golf
club-houses, and private piers, and surf-bathing
houses, and new tram-lines, and ice-cream shops,
and other things, I came to the conclusion that I
must end my days in Sydney, so as to imbibe
some of the strange stuff and be comfortably
Ruined myself under a cold, hard Federation with
a devilish, calculating tariff.— O. C. Cabot.

Britain’s Hunger Duties and its Free Food
Joke.
During the recent election at Manchester
(England), the Freetrade person and the
Tariff Reform person shouted at each other with
excessive bitterness. The Freetrade person said
that the Tariff Reform person was irrevocably
committed to a tax on the food of the people, ” and
on account of this sin he vainly urged the people of
Manchester to wipe out the Tariff Reform in-
dividual. He is a Free Fooder of the deepest
dye, is the Freetrade person; and can’t stand
the Tariff Reform inhabitant. And the simple,
trusting- folk who don’t investigate for themselves,
but just assimilate the bluff of cheeky frauds like
the British Free Fooder, hardly begin to realise
that this sort of thing is all bluff, and that Britain
is really an inveterate taxer of its people’s food
and drink. Here is last year’s record from the
land of the Free Food swindle :
British Customs Receipts 1906-7.
Sugar £5,839,866
Tea ... ... 5,596,907
Molasses 268,374
Cocoa, etc 246,990
Raisins 215,859
Coffee 175,319
Confectionery 152,123
Figs, plums, prunes .. .. 129,739
Currants 121,721
Chicory 48,600
Total Food Duties.. .. £12,795,498
And that is saying nothing of £13,316,018 levied
on tobacco (largely a poor man’s luxury), and the
beer tax, and other trifles that made up the Customs
taxation ot Freetrade Britain last year to
£33,169,804 A huge raiser of revenue at the Cus-
toms is Freetrade Britain ; it gathers together
more money in that way than does any other
country on earth bar the United States (and
the U.S. has twice its population). And a fierce,
hard taxer of the people’s food—mainly the poor
people’s food —is Bull’s country ; it raises more
money by Customs duties on food than does
France, Germany, Russia, Austria, or any other
country on earth, with the doubtful exception of
the United States. The British Free Food lie
is an impudent jest from basement to roof. It is
contradicted and disproved by every book of
reference or official publication that deals with
British revenue matters. It is as foolishly un-
convincing a yarn as would be a statement that
all Englishmen are niggers, or that the Pyramids
are located in Ireland, or that Edward VII. is a
Hottentot. It is vaguely suggestive of the case
of the old-time British seaman who is supposed
to have sung ‘‘Britons never shall be slaves”
before and after the daily flogging. It ranks
with the legend that Britain is a land where want
is practically unknown, and where Freedom has
its special and most glorious home and location
and roost. On this glaring Free Food or Free
Breakfast Table mendacity is based that lofty
and patronising compassion which the Briton is
taught to shed upon the allegedly oppressed and
over-taxed foreigner. Yet it must be admitted
that there is a material difference between
Britain’s food duties and those of most other
nations. Other nations, when they tax food for
revenue purposes, mostly give preference to
articles which their own lands can produce, so
that the taxes may incidentally encourage and help
along local industry in addition to filling the local
Treasury. Britain confines itself rigorously to
taxing articles that can’t be produced locally, so
as to make dead sure that its Food Taxes won’t inci-
dentally help along any local industry, or find any
British person a job, or do anything at all for him
except raise prices and make his life more miser-
able. The taxes enumerated are Hunger Duties
pure and simple. The Manchester election was,
to a great extent, fought out on the Free Food
issue, and the Free Fooder, representative of the
party which collects the Hunger Duties, was
defeated. Bull begins to see the joke.

Cheapness and Smash.
It is too early yet to say what was the immediate
cause of the appalling railway smash at Bray-
brook (Vic.), or how many of a dozen small acts
of foolish, ignorant economy and muddle combined
to make the trouble. But something of the causes
that lie behind the immediate cause seem visible
enough. Things are coming to light which make
it seem a marvel that Victoria hasn’t a fatal
accident daily as a steady average. The Victorian
railways start under a gigantic handicap. All
over the Western District, the most fertile part of
the country, a crowd of brutishly selfish rich land-
owners are allowed to shut out population, and
keep the country a grassy wilderness. These con-
ditions make it impossible for a huge proportion
of the Victorian railway lines to pay under ordi-
nary conditions, for there are practically no people
in the country they traverse to use them. If Premier
Bent were anything but the roaring fraud he is,
the difficulty might be got over by breaking up
the big estates, and settling the country, and
making work for the outlying lines to do. But as
Bent is only a roarer in land settlement matters,
Victoria is trying to make its railways pay by the '
other alternative. Repairs are starved, wages are
low, a woman does the work of a man, and a man
does the work of two or three men, and a bov tries
to do the work of a horse. For instance, Bray-
brook Junction, where the recent horror took
place, has a staff of three men and a boy. That
enables one man to be on duty at a time. The
man on the job has to look after the Junction
generally—it is within seven miles of Melbourne,
and the Ballarat-Adelaide line and the Bendigo
line, both of which carry a heavy traffic, join
there. He has to supervise (by telegraph) two
adjacent stations where a horrible economy has
cut down the staff to one woman. He has to look
after a private siding, where a large business is
done. He attends to two busy telegraph instru-
ments. He looks after a signal box with 33 levers.
He operates the block system. He opens and
shuts the gates by hand ; a few pounds have been
saved by not interlocking them with the signal
apparatus. Also he sells tickets, books parcels
and heavy goods, yells the name of the station,
opens and shuts carriage doors, answers idiotic
questions, and is his own station-cleaner. Like-
wise he keeps the cash and the accounts. The
removal of the great Sunshine Harvester Works
to Braybrook made an enormous increase in the
work of the station, and by way of celebrating the
event it is alleged that the staff has been cut down
from seven men to three and a boy. (To compensate
for this the name of the station was changed to
Sunshine.”) Three signalmen are said to have been
retrenched, and for want of signalmen (who cost
money) and an extra signal-box (which costs
money) it seems to have been necessary to make
variations in the safe and reliable block system,
which variations are calculated to go far towards
making it a mere gamble and quite unreliable.
Then, under the slipshod, run-down policy of cheap-
ness at any price, trains were late. The shambling
device of having a gigantic train hauled by two
engines, instead of two trains with one engine
each, enabled one guard to do the work ofHwo ;
but as the platforms were too short for the length
of the train, it had to make two separate stops at
almost every station, and it got later and later
as it went along. This added to the con-
fusion. And some money seems to have been
saved by having the old, weak, out-of-date
Westinghouse brake of 1873 instead of the vastly
superior one patented in 1885. By these and like
devices the Victorian railways are made to pay.
It is an awful job to make a system pay which has
to meet the interest bill and the expense bill on
hundreds of miles of line where there is practically
no traffic because callous land-grabbers shut out
the people who would make traffic, but the marvel
has been achieved. Therefore the verdict in the
first place is one of murder done for the sake of
sordid gain by certain big landlords of Victoria.
His Programme.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. J. H. Clayton, who is contesting' the St. George
referred in his speech at Rockdale last night to the
federal capital question. If he were returned, and the
matter was not settled, he would go so far as favoring a
cry for secession. Of course, they might not get it. They
would no doubt receive a reply from the British Govern-
ment stating that secession could not be extended, but that
the capital site question should be settled, and the capital
put in the place intended for it in New South Wales, etc.,
etc.— Sydney Daily Telegraph, 25/4/’OB.
Gents, I would be your member ; I would stand
By Mister Wade, and wail and weep and gnash
My teeth about Our Wrongs, to beat the band.
With me on deck something will have to smash.
Here is my scheme for boosting up this seat
And doing Noo South lasting service : I
Propose to make the Commonwealth cold meat
Unless it hearkens to St. George’s cry.
My maiden speech shall be devoted to
A burning plaint in re the Capital;
Rockdale I pick—failing its choice, I’ll do
This mischief to Lyne, Deakin, Groom et al.
I shall go ’Ome— l, J. H. Clayton ! —and,
In full view of the British Government,
Heap ashes on my skull, and likewise sand,
Proof of my grief o’er “The Agreement” rent.
This grisly sight I hope—nay, gents, I know—
Must o’er the Ma-land cast a dreary blight.
“Look at im,” folks will say. “Doesn’t this show
That Clayton’s seat’s the only proper site ?”
The move may fail; Australia may stand fast
To the Monaro plains, which now enmesh
Her fancy. So? Then straightway to the mast
I nail my watchword—“Rockdale or Secesh ! ”
N.S.W.

Johnson—His Trucks.
Thus the S. M. Herald in a leading article on
“Stock and Railways” last week :
The shortage of trucks, which has inflicted loss on the
country running into hundreds of thousands of pounds
a member of the deputation estimated that half a million
pounds’ worth of sheep was lost in Riverina alone—-has
already been publicly regretted by the Chief Com-
missioner ; but we may hope that he. is now convinced of
the unsoundness of the policy of keeping the rolling stock
down. Clearly there should be, as the President of the
Chamber urged, a reserve of trucks sufficient to meet any
emergency that may reasonably be expected to arise. If
they must lie idle for part of the year it cannot be helped.
They should be there, if only as an insurance of the pro-
ducers against preventable loss.
Now, supposing it had been Oliver, the Aus-
tralian, instead of the imported Johnson, who
was charged with spilling £500,000 of good
Cornstalk money in one district in one year
how would the Herald have raved ! Even
the Herald couldn’t find any charge of
fooling away the public money or of mud-
dling away the public interests to level at
Oliver when he was Railway Commissioner, so
it howled day after day, and week after week, for
his official head on the alleged ground that, in the
seclusion of the office, he and two other officials,
who had tried to usurp his authority, didn’t agree
too well. The excuse was so paltry that many
people set down the frenzied denunciation of
Oliver to the sordid fact that he objected to the
free carriage of newspapers by train. Then the
8. M. Herald discovered Johnson. It guaranteed
Johnson. It went bail for Johnson. It assured
N.S.W. that not only would Johnson put every-
thing right with the railways (it didn’t say what
was wrong with them), but that he would solve the
tramway problem (it didn’t say what the tramway
problem was). It even said that the only thing that
would put the Lands Department straight would be
the handing over of the management to “a man like
Johnson.” It became silly and raving mad about
Johnson, and its Johnson obsession made it a
laughing stock. Now, what with the vast increase
in the number of accidents, the alleged shortage
of trucks, the heavy stock losses which the
Herald itself ascribes to needless . muddle,
and the complaints about confusion and
demoralisation, the old paper is fighting
a desperate rear-guard action to save the
battered remnants of its idol from falling into
the hands of the enemy. The community knows
the value of the S. M. Herald’s judgment now.
Every time that paper recommends Canberra as
an ideal capital the public will think of Johnson,
and Canberra it will think of as Johnsonville.
The Herald’s yearnings for a place like Canberra
and for a man like Johnson are twin emotions.

Johnson—His Luggage Van.
TheN.S. W. people who travel on Johnson’s trains
oughtperhaps tobethankfulif they are landed with
breath in their bodies and their limbs unbroken.
Still there are ungrateful reprobates who expect
more. Some of them, for instance, think that
their personal luggage, grabbed from them at
their home station, ought to be landed along with
them; but nowadays that sort is liable to be dis-
appointed. At Albury the Melbourne express is
frequently cut up into two divisions; and though
one lands at. Sydney (with luck) at anything from
three-quarters of an hour to two hours ahead of the
other, only the other one carries any luggage. Also
the other one doesn’t stop at stations for which it
has no passengers ; consequently people who have
travelled by the first division, and have got out at
some station for which the second division of the
train has no passengers, subsequently seethesecond
train whiz past, carrying their luggage with it on
to Sydney. From a mere plain Australian point
of view it seems a rotten way of doing things.
But presumably it’s all right—the imported Mr.
Johnsing is in charge, and the S. M. Herald
gave the foreign Mr. Johnsing a sort of Sunday-
school certificate even before it knew him.
