The Very Sudden Awakening of Bull.
The Manchester election result constitutes the
most smashing set-back Freetrade has endured in
the United Kingdom during the past half-dozen
years. It is Churchill’s misfortune that he
stands in relation to the Freetrade principle, in
the voting public’s eye, as no other member of his
party does. It was on the fiscal issue alone that
he seceded from the party of his forebears ; it was,
he pointed out, only the “urgent necessity for
keeping inviolate the doctrine of Bright and of
Cobden” that impelled him to break with
Chamberlain, Balfour, Chaplin, and his other
khaki associates at the last British general election.
As a candidate, Churchill looked incomparably
strong. He was sure of a measure of Home Rule
support; and still surer (since no Englishman
believes him, on his record, to be other than anti-
Home Rule) of practically every Unionist Free-
trade vote polled in the constituency. (These, as
a recent Reuter’s message indicates, he duly got.)
Withal he is a picturesque figure, a BARNUM-like
self-advertiser, the coitler of pungent and telling
phrases, a fine platform speaker, the most mag-
netic personality in either House—the type, in
fact, the British elector well loves and is cheerfully
prepared to trudge through miles of slush and
drizzle to support. Said Harcourt, a hereditary
Freetrader and Whig, when the four-figure
victory of the Protectionists at Hastings was
made known last month :
I now know of no constituency safe for the Freetrade
cause.
Manchester has proved how truly he judged.
The Briton is changing his views in a hurry.
He feels the crying need of Protection and of a
modern system of land defence, and so is turning,
despite himself, to the party which, with all its
demerits, promises him those things.

Again the Tocsin.
Richard Arthur, M.L.A. (of Sydney), writes
The stars in their courses fight awhile for Australia.
Who could have dreamed that when the Maine flew
heavenwards in Havana harbor, the rebound would
reach to Australasian seas? And yet without this, no
Stars and Stripes would wave in Manila, and no battle-
ships save those of Nippon float to-day in the Pacific.
Now the great and efficient war fleet of a kindred people
is making for the Philippines, where it will stay, what-
ever the reports to the contrary, and Roo«evelt is
demanding four greater Dreadnoughts, that the peace
of the Pacific be not threatened. Never had the
Australians a better friend than this strenuous, dis-
interested, conflict-loving man, who has sent again
and again kindly greetings and pregnant warnings across
the seas to the younger democracy, and now, as a gage
of the bonds of kinship, orders the battleships of the
Great Republic to our shores. He has been called the
Peacemaker—a title not without warrant, and yet listen
to his words : “ Neither arbitration nor any other device
can prevent the gravest and most terrible wrong to
peoples who have lost their capacity for self-defence. The
nation that is opulent and unarmed invites disaster.
Even more direct comes the warning from the most philo-
sophic present-day student of the destiny of nations—the
author of “The Influence of Sea Power in History ” : “I
wonder at the improvidence of Australasia in trusting that
laws, though breathing the most popular convictions and
purpose, can protect their land from that which threatens.
All the naval power of the British Empire cannot suffice
ultimately to save a remote community which neither
breeds men in plenty, nor imports them freely.” The
American fleet in the Pacific has probably delayed our
day of trial, will possibly give us an ally in our hour of
need. But nothing can abrogate the eternal law that
nations hold their own, not by help from without, but by
the power which they themselves possess. And no
people need to have this truth thundered without ceasing
in their ears as do they of Australia.
*.
A scrkam from Sydney Telegraph :
Even manufacturing for our own requirements is an
oppressively costly business, as is made painfully evident
by the present fiscal burdens. How much more profit-
able nationally could the sum we pay in excessive Cus-
toms taxes be spent in bringing more land under cultiva-
tion, in experimenting to find suitable crops and to sup-
press plant and stock diseases, in irrigation, in perfecting
dry-farming 'methods, in attracting new settlers and
giving them facilities to immediately become producers !
To take concrete instances, what venture in protection-
ism is remotely comparable in potential significance or
possible results to the achievements of the late Mr.
Farrer in developing disease- and drought-resisting
wheats ? Or what equivalent amount in Customs taxes
to that spent on the Barren Jack dam could equal it in
returns to Australia —for should Barren Jack prove a
practical success, the lesson it will teach the Common-
wealth, the possibilities it will illustrate in a land where
sites for storages are so abundantly provided for by
Nature, must be inestimable.
That is the sort of silly argument that the anti-
Australian gang is always trying to pass on an
innocent and guileless public. The Protectionist
policy has supplied N.S.W. with about £2,000,000
a year more revenue than it had before. It has
enabled N.S.W., for the first time in about 60
years, to reduce its debt a little. It has increased
the value of N.S.W. manufactures from £9,000,000
a year to £12,000,000, which is about twice
the value of all the State's agriculture put
together. And it has increased the number of
people employed in N.S.W. manufacturing in-
dustries by more than 50 per cent. And mean-
while the wonderful and blastiferous “achieve-
ments of the late Mr. Farrer in developing
disease- and drought-resisting wheats ” have done
so little for N.S.W. that the value of its agricul-
tural produce was less in 1907 than in 1901; and
as for the Barren Jack reservoir, it hasn’t returned
a copper to Australia, though it has swallowed a
considerable amount of good Australian money.
Moreover, it is the Protectionist policy which has
supplied the funds to pay for the Barren Jack reser-
voir, and which has lifted N.S.W. out of the old
condition of chronic deficit, and made it a State
with a surplus. “ The achievements of the late
Mr. Farrer in developing disease- and drought-
resisting wheats ” haven’t been worth 2d. to
Australia as compared with the achievements of
the present Alfred Deakin, and William Lyne,
and Austin Chapman in developing disease- and
drought-resisting manufactures.
“Sr.”: I support your motion for a searching
Federal inquiry into Australian railway manage-
ment in general, and the Victorian article in
particular. Tait introduced Canadian methods
to Victoria. He would make the railways show a
profit. He would start the “economic” regime
in solid earnest. And he did. He bobbed his
head to the statue of and scoffed at
adequacy. Boys at 2s. and 3s. a day are substi-
tuted for the men at 7s. a day. Porters and
assistants are now mostly lads. Gangers are
given extra lengths, and less men are employed
on the permanent way. At Gembrook, where a
recent accident happened, six men were employed
on the road at the time of the mishap, whereas
before Tait’s Dictatorship 16 men looked after a
similar mileage. In 1891 engine-drivers received
13s. 3d. per day; in 1905 they got 12s. sd. All
the Departments have more or less surrendered
competency in the interests of cheapness. Here
are some figures computed from the De-
partment’s statistics in relation to accidents:
For ten years from 1885-6 to 1895-6 the num-
ber of persons injured on the Victorian railways
was 3073. From 1895-6 to 1905 the number was
5343, or an increase of 2270. The number of rail-
way employees —either contractors’ or Depart-
mental-injured in the same term of years was
1437 from 1885-6 to 1895-6, and 2852 from
1895-6 to 1905, an increase of close on 100,
per cent. Solly (Railway representative in the
Victorian Assembly), in commenting upon the
salaries, hours and conditions of railway men under
the Canadian Tait’s management, is reported
in Hansard (28/11/’O5) to have said : “ The result
will be a great smash on our railways. In con-
nection with it everybody will be blamed but
those responsible for the conditions that presaged
the accident.” Bent, as Minister for Railways,
is a sawdust nonentity. The Department’s own
officers report to him that the rolling-stock,
machinery, permanent way, etc., are all in tip-
top condition, and Bent is satisfied, just files the
reports and goes out to a bowling-green and
records his marvellous reminiscences. Just
imagine a Department’s officer furnishing a re-
port to the effect that the Department that he
administers, and is paid a fairly round screw to
look after, is in a chaotic, broken-down, busted
condition, and needs someone to get to work at
things. As you say, a Federal inquiry into the
management of Australian railways is badly
wanted.

John Bull’s views on his own stomach, and his
views on the other fellow’s stomach, are evidently
two widely-different sets of view. A Royal Com-
mission is inquiring into the mysteries of British
whisky, and it has learned, among other things,
that though there is strict Excise supervision over
the things that are put in whisky for local con-
sumption, any blamed old substance can go into
whisky intended for export. There seems to be
no limitation at all ; the line isn’t even drawn at
kerosene and old boots, supposing any loony dis-
tiller wanted to shove them into the barrel. In
fact, whisky for export need not be whisky at all. It
may be a mere compound of essences. These
essences, despite the fact that their employment
in Bull’s own drink is quite illegal, are openly
advertised in England, and as they must be
used for something, it is assumed that the
liquor exported for the benefit of the “colonial”
stomach very often consists of them. No
wonder Scotchmen and Irishmen say that the
liquor they get here isn’t the same as they got
on the old home heather or the old, home bog.
Anyhow, it’s time Australia made its own whisky.

The five Judges of the High Court have decided
that Archibald Currie and Co.’s steamers, ship-
ping their crews in India, do not begin and end
their voyage in Australia, wherefore the Arbitra-
tion Court has no jurisdiction over them. Very
well; then what else are they but foreign ships ?
Yet, in spite of that, one is subsidised by Bent
to run to Singapore, and all are allowed exemp-
tion from pilotage in Melbourne as Victorian-
registered ships. Further, by some juggling, they
are allowed pilotage exemption in N. S. Wales,
though nearly all their masters are supposed to
have the new certificate that is limited to ships in
the inter-State or South Sea Island trade. Thus,
when it comes to dodging the Arbitration Court
they are foreign, and when it comes to dodging
pilotage dues they are not foreign, but Australian.
The Curries ought, under these circumstances,
to be promptly called upon to make up their
minds which they are going to be. If they prefer
to be Australian—all right, then the Australian
Arbitration law must apply to them. If, how-
ever, they prefer to be foreign, let them run up
the Chow or whatever other Asiatic flag they
please; only in that case they can’t expect to have
the rights and privileges of Australians. Cer-
tainly Australia can’t allow them to pretend to be
both foreign and Australian at the same time—
can’t, that is, if it wants to preserve its reputa-
tion for being moderately sane.

The usual female, with the usual tale of assault,
battery and reft virtue, has turned up at Auckland
(M.L.). She had wandered on to the wharf one
calm Sabbath eve, wrapped in holy meditation,
whenthefirst engineerof asteamerruthlessly seized
her and dragged her up gangways, flights of stairs
and ladders to his cabin on the upper deck. Then,
bereft of her above-mentioned article, she left the
ship to keep an appointment with her lover, who,
“on account of something he heard,” told the
police. Then Kettle, P.M., took a hand. Wit-
nesses mentioned that there was a crowd on the
wharf at the time, that the silky stillness
of the night was unbroken by any cries,
and that as it was high water it would have
taken an acrobat to swarm up the ladder to the
deck. Also the usual female admitted that she
had left the ship and walked up the wharf before
complaining to anyone ; likewise that she refused
to allow a doctor to examine her for the bruises
sustained in all the frightful dragging and maul-
ing. Instead, however, of bundling her out
or committing her for perjury, for the proper
encouragement of others of like mind, Magis-
trate Kettle took three days to think over the
matter before throwing the case out heavily.
Meanwhile, the accused hp.s probably lost his
job, while the woman stalks abroad in the
clanking noonday, and there is no police authority
with sufficient starch in his vertebrae to haul her
up to a place where she could get the reward of
her efforts.

“ F.C.,” from Melbourne
Now that things are sorting themselves out somewhat,
in a manner of speaking, the Victorian public is remem-
bering things it had forgotten and learning others that it
ought to have known but didn’t. Only three short days
after the Sunshine horror, the Adelaide-Melbourne ex-
press was at Murray Bridge, when it was found necessary
to temporarily divide the train. While the rear part—five
carriages and engine—was being shunted, it unac-
countably dashed into the foremost stationary carriages.
Nobody was killed, but several passengers were more or
less injured, and some of the carriages were tossed off the
rails. The train got away over two hours late, but, says
the Age report, the driver made up one hour of the lost time
in the run to Ballarat. Apparently the usually
gradual Victorian trains can run a good deal faster
than is supposed, when the driver has to make
up time. Another interesting item of informa-
tion is the amazing fact that the brakes now'
in use on Victorian passenger trains are the old-style
Wf.stinghousk brakes produced in 73, though the Bent
State goods trains are provided with a much more power-
ful Wbstinghouse brake patented in ’B5, the reason
alleged for this thrifty folly being that the goods
trains are too heavy for the slow-acting, old-
fashioned Westinghouse. So the Bendigo express was
so heavy that it required two of the most .powerful
engines to draw it at the scheduled speed of 36 miles
per hour ;it was admittedly overcrowded it was run-
ning on a continuous arid notoriously dangerous down
grade; it was behind time; the driver was expected to
make up lost time ; and it was supplied with a slow-
acting, venerable make of brake. Yet certain people are
surprised at the driver saying the brake wouldn’t act.
Two other accidents happened on the day of the
Murray Bridge occurrence —a connecting rod broke on the
Bendigo-Echuca train, and some trucks fan off the line
at Pirron Yaloak. Neither of these casualties could be
due to negligence of drivers, guards, or signalmen.
Mr. Wade, of N.S.W., has stated that his
attitude at the Premiers’ Conference will be one
of “ give and take.” It always is Wade’s idea in
politics to give “ cheek ” and to take the earth.

“ 10-2-4 ” spends a dreary evening :
I was in at the death of Wonderland City last Saturday
night. One requires scant imagination to grasp how de-
pressing a thing it needs must be to see a Wonder-City
moribund, with the death-rattle in its main street, as ic
were ; and so I had made an almost hysterical effort to
attend a merrier function that evening. But a stronger
will than mine compelled ; wherefore at 9 p.m. I stood,
imprecant, by her side, my shoes full of Bilanderson’s
private beach sand, a screaming easterly gale whizzing
past my ears, gazing dully at the antics of a number of
supers in front of an alleged castle. This was the act the
daily papers had billed that morning as
FIRE. FIRE AND FLAMES. FIRE.
FIRE. A Thrilling Drama in Dumb Show. FIRE.
FIRE. FIRE. FIRE. FIRE. FIRE. FIRE.
Presently a gas-jet flared at one of the windows, whereat
the supers massed together and wailed “Fire!” very
eerily. Forth fared the firemen. Gas was by this time
burning merrily in several places. The flatne-subduers
sped nimbly up and down ladders amid ribald laughter
from the onlookers. The gas continued to burn. And
now, very, very slowly the front of the castle swung out-
wards and deposited itself cautiously on the beach. At
the same time a well-aimed limelight shaft disclosed a
large person in white slumber-wear tick-tacking frenziedly
on a high-placed rafter. Later, having secured a measure
of attention, he leaped into a net below, and the crowd
dispersed, sneezing and making cynical remarks.
I hurried off with my companion and dispensed hard-
won coin to view “ The Mermaid.” In a chill, crypt-like
wilderness of unstable cane chairs we sat alone, and
waited. I brooded on the Grave, the Wrongs of my
State, the Apostolic Secesh Succession, the Hereafter.
Some’ small, dirty boys came in riotously, but grew
reverent in sudden fashiom, and crouched abjectly silent
in the middle distance. A young man, attended by three
maidens, entered, and “ The Mermaid ” was exhibited.
We fled from this young woman as soon as was feasible,
and stood on a platform above “ The Maze,” observing
two little girls in immense hats wandering with the pur-
posefulness of extreme youth amongst a number of
revolving corrugated-iron drain pipes. The strains of
weird music mingled sadly with the blast; sharp, anxious
steam whistles sounded from the beach below; the
attendants, ticket-purveyors and stall-holders looked in-
expressibly dejected to the eye, great mournfulness
seized me in its grip. . , , ,
Mv companion, cheery to the last, pointed to where a
small band was grouped by a tank. Thither we pro-
ceeded at a hopeful trot, or amble, or canter. An inclined
wooden shute shot up to a stand about 20ft. above the
level of the water. On the stand was a stout young man
with a bicycle. A small emotional official, grotesque in
outsize coat and cap, gesticulated at the tank-side, pas-
sionately exhorting the multitude. Of a sudden he turned
dramatically to the cyclist and howled the following:
“ Daredevil Dee, rekerllect that in doin’ the feat you now
propose, vou take yer life in yer own ’ands ! (A pause.)
That bein’ understood, grip yer ’andle-bars, compose yer-
self, Daredevil, hand go ! 1 ”
Whereupon the young man rode smartly down the
slope and fell into the pneumonia-trap at his feet. Most
of us then wended homewards. Not while I live will I,
if I know it, attend at the death-throes of another city.

At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) “Humpty
Dumpty” has been breaking himself out of his
shell eight times a week, and getting a start of six
“ wishes” in consideration of his motherless con-
dition ; and Bert Gilbert goes submarining six
nights a week and twice on Wednesdays and holi-
days, and bores a hole in the machine to see if any
mermaids are about, and shoots the sharks that
swim in instead; and Harry Phydora, who is
King Solium’s own Martha, insists that the king
shall have no truck with mermaids—“ half-women
and half-kippers” ; and the Wheel of Life flashes
its hundred tights and its thousand lights; and
Harry Shine is cured of a fearful cold ; and
in the ballet of the States the dashing Wes-
tralians, clanking with golden sovereigns, continue
to get the loudest cheer—and 437 other things
conspire to make this writer lament that he can t
be H. Dumpty for just one night. If J.L.vV.s
fairy queen gave him a start of two wish e s, he
reckons off-hand that he would demand (1) halt ot
J.C.W.’s pantomime profits delivered in seven (or
more) motor-cars, and (2) the instant execution ot
the crow that has been scattering lamentations
from thebird-and-monkey shop in The Bulletin s
neighborhood for the past three months. Alter
that the other four wishes could stand over in
more comfort for further consideration.

At Sydney Lyceum, Spencer’s Theatre-
scope still varies scenes of flood and rugged coast
with the concentrated essence of “ The Shriek in
the Bloodstained Dustbin,” and shows sky-scrap-
ing romances, where wild-eyed men struggle like
demons among chimney tops, fall down frightful
flues 10,000 ft. deep, and come up smiling for the
next round. Spencer caters for all classes,
and his fearsome melodrama is counterbalanced
by a series of idyllic pictures entitled “Cupid’s
Pranks.” Also, there is a flavor of ghastly up-to-
dateness in the pictures of Sunshine station with
the debris lying all around, and the arrival of
Tait and other potentates on the scene. As a
good stirring set of Aims, The Bulletin suggests
that Spencer should bioscope the book of Genesis
and Revelation. It would be interesting if well
done, and would suit the atmosphere of the
Lyceum.
** * *
West’s Pictures are now located at Sydney
Town Hall, the Glaciarium being required for a
purpose signified by its icy name. Among the
films is one that reproduces the recent Show, but
although it is well-rendered, the Show has become
a great weariness.
*■ * * *
It seems a paradox to call a shadow bright,
but The Shadow, a little paper issued on behalf
of the Women’s Labor Bazaar, has brightness and
brains and versatility. It starts with a kindly
message to the editor from Lord Beauchamp, and
runs a number of serious articles, leavened with
romantic prose and verse. Mrs. Ada Holman
edits the bantling, and artist Watkins is credited
with the artistic cover-picture. The Women’s
Labor Bazaar makes for a successful close in the
Sydney Trades Hall, down in Goulburn-street.
* * *
The Sydney “ Muffs ” are lashing out like
a blood steed. Next Tuesday and Wednesday
this bunch of tried amateurs will stage “As You
Like It,” under the direction of Mr. Ivan Hill, who
was here with those round, cheery cherubs, the
Westminster choristers.

Max Schliiter, the Danish violinist, has
returned to Sydney, and under J. and N. Tait’s
direction commences a series of three concerts at
the Centenary Hall (the Town Hall being booked
to another management) on Monday evening
next. The other two concerts transpire on
Thursday and the following Saturday. Miss
Emma Sussmilch and Messrs. Godfrey Smith and
.T. Edward Sykes are to assist the BeethovCn-
visaged violinist during the season.

Sydney Tivoli still holds strongly to the
same site, and also with reasonable reliability to
the same programme. The Kaufmann cyclists
have discovered a few more positions in which
people may risk their necks on bicycles, and
Charles Mildare whistles more like a canary than
ever.
** * *
“John Glayde’s Honor,” produced at
Melbourne Her Majesty’s last Saturday night, is
chiefly a study of temperaments. The theme is
the delicate but persistent problem of the “odd
man.” Mrs. Glayde is a lady of thirty-one, and
her lover, Trevor Lerode, is a man of twenty-
eight. He looks less than that in the play, and
seems to be struggling hard to conceal his feeding-
bottle. The play opens in Mrs. Glayde’s Parisian
flat. The time is night, and Mrs. Glayde and a half-
dozen friends, including the estimable Lerode,
are seated around the supper table. The conver-
sation is of the brand that is made in Mayfair,
and bottled for export. Prom this bottled
dialogue, it transpires that John Glayde is an iron
king, and a magnate who is fast swallow-
ing the continent that Columbus discovered.
He is so busy swallowing that his wife is shame-
fully neglected. Lerode is an artist, and is paint-
ing her portrait. He doesn’t neglect Mrs. Glayde.
The arrangement, however, isn’t to the taste of
Lerode’s ma, a lady whose innate worldliness is
touched by superfine idiocy and Puritanism. She
wants her son to wed the daughter of a titled
brewer, and she cables in cryptic language to
•Tohn Glayde, who is away, swallowing as usual.
He leaves a partially-eaten republic on his plate,
with teeth marks on it, and flies homewards, and
hounds into his wife’s supper party. His appear-
ance is surprising, and his welcome is of the tem-
perature of a polar bear. Thereafter Glayde
learns things by degrees, and offers to give up
swallowing, and be a more attentive husband.
His wife upbraids him, but later capitulates, a
heap of expensive clothes, in his arms. Then in
the midst of the reconciliation she elopes. The
news rouses John the Swallower from his tender
reflections and his dreams of a happier and better
life. He puts on a corrugated brow, and sticks
his jaw out eight inches. Also he overtakes the
pair, and in a fine dramatic scene casts his wife
oil, promises to divorce her and provide her with
an income, and then departs, leaving two poor,
purposeless beings with the expression of the lost
dog at the corner. The end is something of an
anti-climax, but is startling and powerful withal.

A second inspection of “Miss Hook of
Holla,nd, at Melbourne Royal, induces no sub-
stantial change in last week’s pronouncement.
.Like its forerunners, the show is as orthodox as %
meat tea in a serious Baptist family, though it
lacks the heaviness and stolidity of that respect-
able Sabbath feed. As to the yarn, its discovery
or tracking out is often a difficult matter,
especially at a time like the present, when the
ottice bloodhound is laid up with inflammation of
the tail. The most definite fact about “Miss
is that it supplies a number of tunes that
will be memorable, six months hence, on boarding-
house pianos. Bast week the virtues of Miss
Rssie Perrin and Mr. Tom Payne were
unwittingly left unrecorded. The glamor
of their duet, “ Toddle Round Amster-
J am > 1S now duly recognised and admitted,
in addition, each possesses a sprightly pair of
legs. Meanwhile the average masculine ear and
eye are attracted by Miss Emmeline Orford, who
sings of “ A little pink petti, from Peter.” Miss
Orford is likely to become popular, though her
mannerisms, on occasion, are rather more outre
than expressive.
* # *
The memoirs of the Lisle family, known
to the populace as “ The Southern Cross,” still
cause agitation in the interior of Melbourne Bijou.
This agitation is Certainly more noisy than con-
vincing; but its aim is Liberty and its end is
Bathos, so it may be excused. Yet the incidental
downing of Britain in the process of making the
bathos is calculated to aggravate the Imperialist.
Compensation for this scurvy treatment of “ pore
ole Hingland ” is, however, plentiful. By 11 p.m.
the spectator is thoroughly sated with vivid
impressions of England’s aristocracy. Before
him struts, in hunting togs, Lord George Lisle,
the archetype of a patrician noodle, whose super-
ciliousness is only redeemed by his arrant
villainy. Then comes the Honourable Walter,
his brother, a curious mixture of Soshand Conser-
vatism a sort of mild anarchist diluted with
Reidism. Follow Lord Julien and Lady Linda
Lovelace, Lord Brandon, Lady Lisle, and
Lady Dunmore a band whose convictions
might be likened to fungi adhering to old dead
trees. Altogether, it is a fine array of talent,
though its collective intelligence doesn’t amount
to. a tolerable Willie Kelly. A word or two
might be imparted on James, the valet, and his
stately side whiskers; but the subject is getting
complex.
** * *
“ Peter Pan,” at Melbourne Princess’s, is
winning the devotion of most people who see him.
He has already become a hobby; a little while
longer he’ll be a full-grown habit, even with
superior people who usually study the drama
only by means of books borrowed from the
Public Library. On second or third seeing,
one concludes that “Peter” charms our
egotism. Anyone this side of senility (which is
that afflicting period when one can recollect no
further back than the time when he was a devil of a
fellow)—anyone may find in “ Peter Pan ” the very
words he or she spoke when going to bed by candle
light hundreds of years ago. Anyone can see that
Peter’s a plagiarist who lives the life we dreamed
about as we lay awake in our white enamel cots
and watched the lights flicker. We played
his pirate game, armed with broken clothes-
pegs, as we hid behind big tree-trunks, and yelled
hideously a,t nothing in particular. The most
unimaginative citizen can get a thrill by watching
Peter. Memory will say to him, giggling : “Pooh !
we did that thirty years ago or more.” Or
else : “ Good Lord ! Barrie’s not very original.
Our kids have been saying, and doing, and
talking about such things ever since they
could stand up without clinging to the leg of
a table.” And a superbly-stodgy personage,
with a massive little Mary and a very bald patoh,
can easily feel that he’s more than half responsible
for the delicate phantasy that drifts past his eyes ;
and think that that dashed Barrie fellow has
swindled him out of one of his v.ery own ideas.
** * *
The Melbourne Opera House show bowls
along at the rathe: leisurely pace it has cultivated
of late, and nothing very new agitates its dead
calm. The comic element just now is represented
by the Scott Brothers and the Ueane Tribune
Trio. Both these turns remind the expert
psychologist who views them of a sunlit
corner of the garden of an idiot asylum.
Both are cheerful—as they claim to be—but
both are inane and unhumorous. Alma Gray is
still singing “ My Waratah.” She sings it through
her nose now. Baby Watson, with her charmingly
clear articulation, has an excuse for her extreme
youth. The babe has more idea of piping her
native tongue than most of her seniors at the
O.H. Fanny Powers and the Artois Brothers are
other saving clauses in the list.
* * *
Bland Holt has been doing splendid busi-
ness at Adelaide Royal with the “ White
Heather.” The crowded audiences do not worry
at all because the play so heavily dates itself back
to last century by showing cycling as a fashion-
able fad. Harrie Ireland is the remarkable
heroine who cannot honorably love Jim unless
she can prove that she is the lawful wife of Bill.
Arthur Styan is the villainous lawful hus-
band (and Walter Baker is not in the
co.). As for Bland Holt and his wife, they
both score heavily in their old parts. Clara
Butt has paid a flying visit to Adelaide, and
after assisting at the wedding of an old friend,
has profitably employed her voice at the Town
Hall on her way to catch the Melbourne express.
William Lang, too, has done his best for the city’s
amusement. Widely advertised as “Australia’s
only champion,” he has disfigured the faces of
several prominent citizens at the City Baths,
which have dried up for the winter to make a big
athletic arena.
Sydney Telegraph (27/4/’08) on Winston
Churchill’s defeat at Manchester (England):—
He has been defeated, not on the straight-out issue of
Preferentialism or Freetrade, as the whooping of the
tariff reform press would like the public to believe, but on
a multiplicity of puzzling minor issues that have com-
pletely overshadowed the fiscal question.
And Winston Churchill (25/4/’08) on the same
subject:—
Mr. Churchill himself admitted that the result was a
heavy, bitter, and crushing blow at Freetrade, a blow that
carried disastrous consequences.—(Reuter, 27/4/’08.)
RESUROET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Wonderland City closed on Saturday night.” —Daily
paper item.
Are you quite dead, my Wonderland, I wonder,
Like Pompeii,
Gomorrah, and such towns, whom Heaven’s shocked
thunder
Hid from the public eye?
Nay ! I’ll not think it; you were stainless ever.
Ne’er did you drink
Supply, howbeit ardent the endeavor
Of toper’s avid wink.
Always, in your chaste glades, the electric spark,
Bright, clear and placid,
Shone tireless ; and on cuddlings in the dark
Imposed the acid.
The gambling person shunned you like the Devil.
(Bookies won’t bet
On rampless switchback sprints, devoid of evil
E’en if they’re let.)
By all of which I hold that, like the Phoenix,
You shall arise ;
And as of yore cause rival towns to be nix
In “Noo South’s” eyes.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ Acacia ” : Usually when a woman goes into a lion’s
den, her name is written large on all the hoardings and
programmes. But in Wirths’ circus there is a placid-
looking German woman, who wears short pants, a red
coat and shoes and stockings, and goes unarmed into an
iron enclosure, where six lions, two leopards, a Russian
bear, six wolves and sundry dogs are met together, and
she lies down among them as unconcernedly as though
they were eggs or Bibles. Also she makes an obstacle of
herself, and the beasts jump over her. She looks as un-
concerned as Daniel probably didn’t; so those male
creatures who assert that woman is afraid of a mouse may
for ever hold their peace, and if they feel it slipping they
can sand their hands and get a fresh grip.
“ Pam ” : Lizzie Newbold, the Richmond (Vic.) soprano,
has been taken in hand by a local committee. The
treasurer has now over ill2o in hand as the result of a
local benefit (£4O) and contributions. The idea is to give
a succession of benefits round the suburbs, and thus
raise £5OO wherewith to send Lizzie to learn her art at
the feet of Marchesi. It was suggested that the next
concert should be held at Collingwood, but the scheme
ran into a snag in the shape of a certain A.N.A. man
and local councillor, who didn’t see that the girl with the
wondrous voice had any claims on Collingwood !
You see, Lizzie Newbold hails from Richmond, and State
Frights having now degenerated into Parish Frights—and
Parish Pump Frights at that—the gentleman doesn’t see
where the claim comes in. On Melba’s say-so, Lizzie has
the best voice she has met with. The girl is a fellow-
Australian, a fellow-Victorian, and a fellow-Melbournian,
all which things might count. But she doesn’t come from
Collingwood, so this truly wondrous person doesn’t see
what Collingwood has to do with her case. Ye gods ! The
little girl is a national possession—an all-Australian pos-
session. Melba can’t last for ever, and it’s up to us to
keep an Australian at the head of the world’s soprani,
if we have one who can take the top place. Everybody
who knows anything about the game reckons that Miss
Newbold has the brains and the voice to get on the roof
of things. And, what’s more, she will get there, and
thick-headed aldermanic obstructionists will be glad
enough some day to flee from ridicule and hide from
public view behind a pile of unsold brown sugar at 2Ad.
a pound.
Talk about realism ! ' In “ The Southern Cross,” at
Melbourne Bijou, the good ship Dumbarton tosses (that
is to say, all except the poop tosses—the poop seems to
be a fixture), and the winds howl, and the waves dash,
and the angry waters leap. But still you don’t realise
how true to Nature Bilanderson’s stage-carpenter is until
the Irish coachman pauses dolorously at the side of the
heaving vessel, and !
“ Star ” : A good deal of Edwin Brett’s Huntley business
in “ Miss Hook of Holland,” at Melbourne Royal, is rather
old rope, being only a variant of the stuff the comedian
gave us in “The Girl from Kay’s” and other musical
shows. Brett follows him in every attitude and accent,
and tries to catch his stammer, but the suave and gracious
fatuousness with which George P. H. can invest a
character is missing, and with it the true flavor and
fancy of the original Hoggenheimer.
“JeanSibi”: Miss Ruth Lincoln, of the “Miss Hook
of Holland ” co., is said to have put an edge on her warble
at the same grindstone as Mrs. Butt-Rumford, the ma of
Joy, Coy, Annoy, VVoy VVoy, etc. But this fact pales
beside the programme statement that Miss Lincoln is a
connoisseur, with unprobed depths of aesthetic feeling.
Her private vice is the collection of old china and antiques
—which is so much tonier than Miss Tittell Croon’s pub-
lished predilections for mere “ gy-urls.” Still, even Miss
Lincoln’s pretensions are nothing to blow about. Writer
knew a lady who collected antiques and buried four ; and
it still knows a professional lady who collects dried dogs’
livers and hangs them over her bed as charms.
“J. 8.”: I rise amid much acid clamor concerning
Julius Knight’s legs and limelight to pay him a deserved
tribute in another direction. He is a tine and discrimi-
nating stage director and effect producer. An anachronism
or a solecism is a rarity in a Knight production ; in most
of the other imported shows the futile mistake arises and
strikes the onlooker severely in the eye about three times
a scene, or thereabouts. Julius handles his crowds well,
and gets the last ounce of effect out of the gentleman
with the pike or halberd every time. In this important
respect he is ahead of any mime Australia has known for
years past. The local stage super is unlike his English
brother inasmuch as he gets no lengthy rehearsals
(Irving, that greatest of stage managers, thought nothing
of rehearsing an ordinary “ street crowd ” daily for a
month), and he is also unlike the French super., who is
an embryo Coquelin often enough, and only suping as a
preliminary to stepping into the principal’s shoes. Julius’
success in getting the good work he does out of the local
article is, in the circumstances, notable and praiseworthy.
Alfred Dampier, the like of whose Voice is not heard on
present-day Australian stages, is living in retire-
ment in a Sydney suburb. He is paralysed down one
side, but retains all his faculties, and more than an echo
of the Voice. A daughter acts with Geach, and a son
started on a country tour last week with a sheaf of the
old man’s plays.
An Australian prima donna had an encouraging, albeit
nasty, experience the other day. She was on the point of
signing for an engagement in one of the big European
opera houses, when the management (old, bald as to the
skull, and possessed of the purposelessly waving lower
limb of the paraplegic subject) arrested her hand and
said, “Stay, mademoiselle; have you a lover?” The
singer, removing her paw with all the bewildered rage of
a stout healthy young Hibernian who had had her princi-
ples whacked in deeply by the good sisters of an up-
country convent, replied that she had not. “ Good ! ”
observed the human ruin, contentedly. “Then I shall be
he. It is settled. Come back at 7 p.m., and we shall ar-
range details over a little dinner.” The songstress retired
without signing, and omitted to return. Also, she didn’t
say what she wanted to say, for her voice was stuck in her
mental doorway. The evasion of managers is as exciting
a sport in the Old World for those singers who have
arrived but mean to keep “ straight ” as is the evasion of
duns for those who haven’t arrived ; nor is there any close
season for either pastime. Even as a glut of writs and
judgment summons indicates her status to the vocal
failure in Europe, so a surplusage of shameful and utterly
uncalled-for proposals from aged and staggery males tells
the aspiring Voice-possessor that she has of a surety
made good.
“Blue Peter”: The new Meynell-Wren-Clarke syndi*
cate has spent a lot of money on its “ Hook of Holland ”
venture, but if it wants to stir up J.C.W. to any extent it
will need to spend a bit more. Melbourne Royal, where
Miss Hook fascinates, clamors for attention in front as
well as behind. The awful old blue curtain rag', with its
beer and corset advertisements, should be scrapped ; it is
an eyesore. The traditional Royal programmes which
the syndicate adheres to—a thin trickle of cast meander-
ing through a book of advertisements—madden more
than they inform. And lastly, the gritty-looking little
boys who vend sweets and the like with raucous cries
between the acts should have their occupations taken
from them. These reforms instituted, and a paying
crowd should fill the Royal in paying numbers. It is a
foolish theatrical syndicate which spoils its show for a
ha’porth of grease-paint.
“J.A.C.”: Mrs. Melba has been giving an American
magazine some points on singing. Among them:
Unless the structure of the larynx be understood, the
attack cannot be perfect.” Yet there were great singers
before anything was really known about the structure of
the larynx, and as for the “ attack ” —ye gods 1 think of
David s attack on Goliath, and deeds like that before
anatomy was invented. Seriously, Malibran, Rubini,
Lablache, Pasta, and the like did not worry themselves
concerning the “ structure of the larynx ” ; they sang be-
cause they couldn’t help it. I myself have known many
eminent vocalists whose laryngeal erudition was of the
vaguest. Faucelli, for instance, with a voice of nightin-
gale sweetness, and who sang like a condensed celestial
choir, never to his knowledge had any larynx—if he
had had he couldn’t have spelt it. I could cite
many others. Tamagno and Masini, I am convinced,
could not have told a larynx from a bar of soap. How-
ever, Mrs. Melba gives one piece of really valuable in-
formation : “Foreign languages are essential to the
singer, and are best acquired in the countries where they
are spoken.” Ordinary people would never have thought
of that, and would have tried to learn German at
Parramatta.
“Sr.”: “ E.O’F’s ” par (B. 9/4/*08) reminds me. I once
had a little experience as the leader of “ Omnes" behind
the scenes. It was a Single Tax drama yclept “The
Dictator,” written, I believe, by Max Hirsch. In the
great act wherein the Dictator orates from the drawing-
room window unto the mob congregated in the street, it
was part of the show that the crowd should yell out ap-
proval at his utterances. He started. As soon as we got
the first cue the unwashed and vulgar mob yelled
“ Hoorarrrr,” “ Horrr,” “Hear, hear,” etc. One of the
vulgar populace behind a side piece, thinking it
was really an election meeting, and that G. Reid
was speaking, started to sing out “ Loafer.”
He kept yelling out “Loafer!” through ‘the
whole blessed oration. The amateur stage manager,
property master and scenic mechanist angnly approached
the bloke and sang out to him to “ shut up, y’ fool ! ”
“LOAFER!!” yelled the bloke. “Shut up, will you;
they can’t hear anything downstairs.” “ LOAFER-R - !!! ”
screamed the turbulent one. “ Won’t you stop, you
blithering mule ? ” “ LOAFER !!!! ” was the loud reply.
Then the stage manager did his great act. He swung his
mighty fist and caught the screamer one on the jaw, and
the bloke subsided to the floor with a faint “ loaf .
When he regained consciousness he became angry. " Who
punched me?” he howled. “I did,” said the enraged
scenic mechanist. “ You wouldn’t stop singing out
‘ loafer,’ and I punched you.” “ Why didn’t you tell me
to stop, then?” “ I did, five times, you blank 1” “ Well,
I didn’t hear you. But what did you want to punch me
for?” The property manager looked hard at the bloke,
and just then the curtain went down.
“ O’F.”: The serio is an interesting study. Some-
times she has a voice, but usually it is just a
good, plain, robust, healthy shout. Her legs are
invariably magnificent. Originally well-formed and
plump, they have been developed and hardened
until they fill her stockings to bursting point. She
sings or talks a song as a prelude to a certain amount
of leaping about. The song isn’t an indispensable part of
the turn, but she has to do something while the orchestra
plays the tune. A song cannot be sung unless it has
words, therefore she is provided with a certain quantity of
remarks the same length as the music. The hearers
never allow themselves to be affected by the sad story—
it is usually a sad story—that the words unfold, because
they know from experience that, as soon as she is readv,
the singer will scatter care to the winds and throw herself
into the air like a kangaroo bounding over a suburban
terrace. Personally, I have no great liking for step-
dancing, and the sad songs never make me shed tears.
All the same, I would miss the lady. Her limbs are a
delight to the eye, and her healthy shouting is a fine,
vigorous, cheerful sound. As long as she doesn’t insist
on singing more than three verses, I can enjoy her turn.
“The Swimmer”: Was at a smoke-night recently
where each man, as he gave up his ticket at the door,
was handed four small pieces of cardboard, each of which
entitled him to one threepenny drink, and thus allowed
him to collect full value for the bob he had paid to
enter. When the drinks were supplied, the barman
took a ticket from each in his hat. Two rounds had
been served. Peace and good swill prevailed—when
suddenly Harmony broke with a crash. The chairman
rose, his eyes blazing with passion. “ Arf a mo’l ”he
howied, at the same time imperatively waving down a
young man who had just started to intone “ Would You
Care?” “ ‘ Arf a mo’,’ I say, and I’ll thank yer all ter
close yer blanked faces fer a minute. There’s a cow
sittin’ somewhere in this room that fer meanness and
treachery strikes yer ’umble as the limit. ’E’s just had a
drink on a cigarette card.” And thereafter there was con-
fusion and blasphemy.
THE PLAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I’m playing at the theatre,”
The splendid fellow said.
The simple people gathered there
Gazed on his noble head.
They loved to stand within the shine
That aureoled an actor fine.
They stood him drinks. “ And do you play
The hero grand?” they asked.
“ Or figure as the hostler gay,
By whom the Duke’s unmasked ? ”
The stranger gravely sipped his rum.
“ Ah, no,” he said. “ I play the drum ! ”

SOCIETY
PENEOLD'S
WINES
HE KNEW,
CYNICAL WAITRESS: “ What'll you have to
follow!”
DYSPEPTIC (haring a holiday): “Hell upon
earth."
One item only of the appalling Sunshine
(Vic.) disaster makes pleasant reading. Once the
rescuers arrived, all worked steadily and silently,
without fuss, without delay. The injured showed
splendid pluck and often splendid self-sacrifice.
But the aid was long in coming, and hopelessly
inadequate when it did come. With the staffs on
duty at night at Spencer-street, a well-equipped
ambulance train should have reached Sunshine in
less than half-an-hour from the first news of the
trouble. It actually took nearly an hour-and-a-
half, and, though repeated messages indicated a
dire'calamity, it contained only one doctor, with
13 trained first-aid men, 16 untrained assistants,
stretchers, 12 beds and pillows, 16 rugs, 24 towels,
six hand-lamps (a reokless half-dozen), and one
paokage of splints. There were neither axes, saws
nor sorewjacks to get the wounded out, and
neither the guards’ vans nor the station offices
boasted so much even as an axe. Incredible as it
seems, too, that ambulance train was stopped at
every station on the way out, and when it brought
back its first load of dead and wounded it was
kept at North Melbourne while porters went
through collecting tickets ! The department
wouldn’t even frank out to their suburban homes
passengers who had come through that awful
horror sound in limb but reft of purses and
money. Some had to borrow in order to buy a
tuppenny train ticket. The reverence of lait s
department for the regulations is very touching.
The deplorable thing is that it didn t have the
same reverence for its running time-tables. it it
had, the accident wouldn’t have happened.
* * * *
The stoic casualness of the Australian
transpired at Sunshine as usual. One lad of 18
lay with his head on his mate’s knee, smoking
cigarettes, and quietly waiting till the rescuers
reached him and levered the heavy carriage frame
off his broken legs. Another youth, with the side
of his face smashed and his eye hanging on his
cheek, asked the doctor if he could save the
organ. The doctor shook his head sadly. Ah,
well.” he, philosophised, “ I suppose it’a a goner.
Half-an-hour later he was dead. Another lay
with a crushed hip against the fence.
Finally an ambulance man reached him. “Never
mind me, old man ; there’s others worse than me.
But just put a pad or something under my back,
will you?” Yet even as the attendant lifted
him he fell over, dead from internal haemorrhage.
. * * *
Most of those who escaped alive from that
splintered Hell at Sunshine were dragged through
the wrecked carriage windows. Lucky for them
it wasn’t the Adelaide express. _ The new cars on
that inter-Stater are provided with only a narrow
door at each end, while across each window are
two strong brass bars. Attention was drawn to
this danger when the carriages were first used,
but Tait and Co. took no notice, though any man
with a fairly long experience of Australian railways
could have told them that it was the barring of
windows that caused very much of the unneces-
sary suffering and probably added to tlie loss of
life when N. S. Wales had its biggest smash out-
side Redfern station.
* * * *
“ Snell ” writes :
Interviews with passengers who were in the Ballarat
train, and went through the whole grim calamity at Bray-
brook (Vic.), betray a curious inability in man and woman
alike to comprehend such a catastrophe as a whole. Each
person I have spoken to has his or her own climax, the
one sight or sound that dominates everything else,
leavin»-'the rest of the picture vague, blurred, and dream-
like. "The woman who saw a hoy trying to climb up the
side of a carriage—“and I put down my hand
to help him, but he was dead ’’—saw little or
nothing else, could describe no more. A young fellow
“ heard a man screaming under the timber,” and to him
that was the tragedy. Another saw a youth under a
beam, “ and the lower half of his body was almost broken
off, but he said: ‘ Don’t mind me, I’m all right ! ’ Oh,
Go'd, he thought he wasn’t hurt!” That “he thought
he wasn’t hurt! ” was the burden of ttiis man’s story.
He tried to tell more, but was all confused. The poor
wretch with the fractured spine who thought he wasn’t
hurt was his one flame of fiery reality in the vast night-
mare. Everywhere you could pick up notes of
color, vivid, crimson, but nowhere a picture. The
rescuers found one woman secured in a sitting
position under a mass of fragments, suckling her infant.
Her injuries were very slight. But the most dramatic
incident of the disaster happened to Laffan, a fireman
who was hard at work among the rescuers when be dis-
covered two bodies under the debris. He called for
assistance. “ Here are two poor g'irls ! he said. When
help came, and the bodies were lifted out, Laffan recog-
nised his two sisters. Both were dead. Trie unfortunate
fireman had no idea that his sisters were in the wrecked
train.
m * * *
As one of the side issues of the Braybrook
(Vie.) smash, it was decided to hitch the defen-
dant engine to another train of the same weight,
and run it over the same bit of line at the same
speed, and see if the brakes would pull it up in
time. Which vaguely suggests the case of Muddy-
feet, the bay horse, which was said to have jibbed
on the 13th of the month, at the seventh mile-
stone on the road to Bottletree. His owner
didn’t believe it, so he took Muddyfeet to the
same milestone on the 13th of next month, and as
he didn’t jib then, the charge was held to be
absolutely disproved. #
“ Josh’s ” letter :
It is surely up to Victoria to lodge a protest against
the action of Railway Commissioner Tait, who is now
bustling about like a herd of ants going nowhere, and
“ investigating ” the Braybrook horror for all he is worth.
There are three possibilities in the Braybrook tragedy :
(1) It may have been caused by the neglect or
incapacity of a subordinate; (2) the system devised
by Tait may be so bad, confused and inefficient
that a great accident was bound to come sooner
or later ; (3) the power behind Tait (which is Bent) may
have made such impossible demands in the way of profits
that the system, however bad, ramshackle, and inefficient,
is the best that can be supplied at the price. But He
That Wears The Cigar In His Countenance has not only,
in effect, acquitted Bent and himself without trial, but is
acting as judge in the matter of the other accused.

the corpses. All the ghouls who were retained by
bereaved relatives and friends lined up in the

on the surface of things, and a voice begins to
clamor <k Gimme me cadaver, then lnvateii<n-
terprise undertakery becomes a horror.

A gruesome narrative was that of the crew
of the steamer Opland, which crawled into Port
Phillip a day or two ago. The vessel, officered by
Norwegians and partly manned by Chows, was
beating its way somewhere off Cape Nelson on the
night of April 17. A few minutes after midnight,
a Chow quartermaster just toppled over the iron
foc’s’le railing and disappeared. He didn’t say any-
thing, but left a relic in the shape of 10 inches of
pigtail, caught in a nut on the railing. Apparently
there wasn’t any row at the time ; but now other
Chowson board allege that the deceased was pushed
overboard by one of the officers. He had refused
to furl the bridge awnings, it was alleged, and
there was an argument and a scuffle —and, well
the Chow had gone overboard, leaving a streak of
black hair behind him. The officer interested
admitted the argument with Confucius, but swore
that he left the yellow man on deck toiling, or
supposed to be toiling, at the disputed job. As
the old tub was in ballast and was rolling heavily,
it is quite probable that the Chow did tumble,
over without assistance ; but, of course, the matter
has to be probed, though with Norwegians on.
one side and Chows on another, the chances are.
the investigation will pull up in a hopeless maze
of contradictions.
The resumption of Queen and Wexford
streets (Sydney) is causing the Chow to gather up
his Joss and other impedimenta, and find fresh
fields to contaminate. Seemingly, the Rocks
resumed area looks very green to the exploring
Chow. The other day a deputation told Trea-
surer Waddell a weird tale of happenings in
that locality—-of an attempted Chow invasion ;of
bonuses of from £5 up to £l5 offered by Chows, to
induce white people to give up their houses; of
white women who were ogled and leered at in
their back yards by the Joss agony ; of waterside
workers (for whom the Rocks should be an
eminently suitable place of residence) being
squeezed out by Chows, and forced to live in
distant suburbs, and pay ill-spared tram
fares to get to their work. Waddell was
more or less sympathetic with the Rocks’
desire to be white, but was careful that no
“injustice” should be done. Yet it is difficult to
say off-hand the exact sort of justice that is de-
served by the undesirable immigrant who made
Wexford-street a loathsome plague-spot on fair
Sydney. Perhaps a large stone-wall and a platoon
of infantry with ball ammunition would about fill
the bill; "failing that, strict segregation, as far
away as possible from the white centres, should be
insisted upon.
An old cottage in Argyle-street, Fitzroy
(Melb.), has put up something of a record ; it has
housed one couple for over 50 years. Alex.
Duncan came to Victoria in 1851, and in ’53 hung
out his farrier’s shingle in Fitzroy. On his
marriage, in April, ’5B, he went with his wife to
the cottage in Argyle-street, and the two have
lived in the little bandbox of a place ever since.
Theirs has been a dull, drab life. They have
never gone house-hunting, and battled with the
property-agent in his lair. Alex. Duncan has
never fought three rounds with the carter who
has broken all the crockery in the course of the
shift, and then given him "lip ” and tried to over-
charge him 10s. He never “ shot the moon’ with
the horse’s hoofs tied up in bags, and the rent
collector running behind clothed with curses
as with a garment. He never had wild
adventures with the linoleum that bags so
horribly in the centre when it is laid down
on a new floor. The pleasure of smelling
the incense of dewy morn from the top
of a van-load of furniture, with the hens tied in
front and the dog dragging behind, was never Ins.
He lacked the roving instinct, which made Drake
circumnavigate the globe, and Heraclius pursue
the Persian hordes across Asia Minor, and which
makes other men, with the gipsy blood in their
veins, rise up in the spring and remove from
No. 10 Boggs Lane to No. 17 Bagdad Alley.
A stay-at-home person was Alex. Duncan, and he
lost much fun in consequence. This writer, when
he was 13 years old, held bitter discourse at
6 a.m. with a cabman who objected to an iron
stove and a kitchen table and some sundries being
put in the four-wheeler and carried five miles for
3s.—the rest of the furniture had been shifted by
hand in the night. And the sunrise was crimson,
and the cabman’s language was a bright bay color,
and the cab horse was brown, and the whole
thing was a picturesque memory, and a color
scheme that will never be forgotten.

“ L.D.” (acidly) :
At Castlemaine, t’other day, a man leaned out of a rail-
way car as the train was moving off, caught hold of a lane
boy standing on the platform, and dragged him along
with the train. (This, it was explained afterwards, was
done in fun.”) The little lad on whom this humor-lover
played his joke fell between the platform and the foot-
board of the train, and, had it not been for the prompt
action of two women who caught his clothing and p -
vented him getting under the train, he would have teen
killed. There is many a man in this sad old world v\ nose
sense of fun is such that it ought to get him ten year
every time it is brought into play. The Castlemaine tra -
wit is a type whose over-developed comic sense merit
life-sentence any day of the week.

TWENTY-TWO KINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Monday Evening.—State Councillor Martin
has published a remarkable book upon Germany’s future,
contending that the maintenance of 22 monarchs, with
civil lists and separate governments, throws an enormous
financial burden upon the Empire. If the separate States
were swept away, he asserts, a saving of £50,000,000 a
year would be possible. —Daily Paper.
A king in his crown is an excellent sight,
He causes a deal of hilarity.
The commoner looks on H.M. with delight,
And says he is kept out of charity.
He’s useful to cheer and to open bazaars,
He’s covered all over with crosses and stars,
And jingles along with his gorgeous hussars,
A splendid example of charity.
Unemployed hoot at him,
Villains all shoot at him
Because he is kept out of charity.
A king with his Court is a glittering dream,
And awfully good to society.
He sends invitation cards out by the ream,
And cares not a fig for propriety.
He spills money carelessly over the land,
The value of it he does not understand ;
He hasn’t to earn it with brain or with hand,
So squanders it all on society—
Throws it religiously,
Hurls it prodigiously
All over the best of society.
One king by himself is an excellent jest—
A jest that we treat with civility.
He’s kept in a castle, and fed on the best,
And served with a winking humility.
But suffering Teutons to action are stirred
When kings roam about in an indolent herd,
For twenty-two monarchs are really absurd ;
They’re not things of any utility.
They are irrational
Jokes that are national,
And aggravate all the nobility.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

“ Stony ” : We are indeed, in some things,
a peculiar race. On the 17th of April of this year
many people arose at cock-crow and sallied forth
to signalise the anniversary of an age-old Tragedy.
It was done to sad music and many genuflections
and with the wearing of a great variety of fashion-
able clothes. Afterwards, the crowd came back
to breakfast and ate Buns—Buns which bore on
their sacrilegious back the Sign of that age-old
Tragedy. During the meal the bun-baker was
criticised, and silly jokes were made as to the
currants which a niggard hand had faintly scat-
tered among the breadstuffs. All through the
days those Buns haunted the eating-room, and the
jokelet, unlike the Buns, passed upon its weary
round, nor staled thereby. It is an old Family
Custom, this sacrilege of the Bun. Small good
it does; for in the very homes where the Hot
Cross Bun is a shibboleth and a fetich and a good
old English craze, too often there is a hitch in the
practical application of those principles concern-
ing which the Bun is to remind us. Their appli-
cation is too often left to a bunch of small-souled
psalm-singers, who sling talk about “ your betters ”
and the “responsibilities of wealth,” and who,
with a bun in one hand and a tract in the other’
and with a cup of weak tea at knee, speak warn-
ings to the young man upon the dangers of his
beery path, and advise him to vote the Prohibi-
tion ticket, even though Freetrade and Aunty
Sosh and the Marriage Tie perjury be blazoned
upon that same ticket in letters of mud. The
lessons of the Tragedy have gone astray a lot on
their way through the centuries.

By this paper’s Oldest Inhabitant :
3 VI If nl V cr ° J ss Geor S e ’ s River, opposite Sans Souci,
recently offered at auction by a firm of Sydney auc-
tioneers is a somewhat historic spot. In the early ’forties
one of the Laycocks built the old house, which still
stands with many modern additions. This Mr. Laycock
was an astute man. He bought Sylvania (not then so
known) bought another block of land at Bottle Forest,
and another block in the direction of Kurnell. By these
means he had the use of thfe intervening land in the
triangle. He sold Sylvania to Tom Holt, first N.S W
Treasurer under Responsible Government, and Thomas
made additions to the house and ornamented
the grounds. He also named it after Sylvania
daughter of Ewan Cameron, of Balmain, who
* Ho ' fc - original Laycock was a sergeant
in the New South Wales or Rum-Selling Corps. He
gained a position as quartermaster through his “ bravery ”
in slaying a rebel at Castle-hill in 1804. He was 7ft. high
and no male descendant of his was, or is, under 6ft This
Thomas Laycock owned two grants at Vaucluse, which
he sold to Henry Hayes, the convict baronet, who was
transported for attempted abduction. Hayes sold to
Captain Piper, who in turn sold to W. C. Wentworth. He
had other grants, amounting to thousands of acres, iust
outside Sydney ; but the glory of the Laycocks, as landed
propnetors has departed. Elias, the sculler, is one of
the descendants. This scribe met some great-grand-
daughters at the sale, but the giant stature was not in
them.

“ M.” : Something in B. (2/4/’08) about a
Sydney clergyman inviting immigrants to avail
themselves of “ ever ready coun-
sel and advice,” reminds me of
how unwilling the general run
of humanity is to accept any-
thing of the kind from the clergy.
I was quarantined with a Sydrtey
hotelkeeper’s family and household
during the plague scare. We thought
the thing rather a lark, and were in
quite high spirits. The fat cook
and one thin housemaid had been
pronounced plague-stricken, but as
they walked downstairs and took
their places in the ambulance in a
fairly cheerful mood we weren’t
really upset about them. Only the
hotelkeeper was upset. It ruined
him. However, when we reached
the jetty of the quarantine station,
a parson met us and said he hoped
to have the spiritual cure of us, or
something, during our enforced resi-
dence at the station. Panic seized
us. The barmaids, who had for-
gotten what religion they were,
crowded behind each other, making
frantic hissing appeals to anything
and everything. “Swear Pin a
Catholic ! Swear I’m a Catholic ! ”
they entreated wildly. We all
swore we were Catholics, right
down to the “ boots ” ; and the par-
son, with a disappointed look, fol-
lowed us up to the buildings. When
we reached the house a priest
emerged smiling earnestly, and the
parson had his revenge. “ All mem-
bers of your flock, Father O’Grady,”
he said, waving his hand at the
mob of us cowering together, panic-
stricken now indeed. There wasn’t
one of us who knew the Mass from
the Rabbit Act.
“ Wyung ” writes :
Melbourne Metropolitan Fire Brigade is a community of
“ handy ” individuals who could give any ordinary ship’s
company a long start. The men are nearly all skilled
artisans, and they are arranged in groups. Thus, one
station does all the brigade’s painting ; another does the
horse-shoeing and smithy work. Still another builds the
necessary furniture. There are stations that make
specialties of all the trades that are necessary for the
maintenance of the brigade. There is even an electrical
engineering and fitting department at the head station.
When the Postal Department wants a new switchboard
it calls for tenders, and, as likely as not, buys a German
one. When the M.F.B. needs a switchboard it makes the
thing straight away. I saw one, the other day, just
finished, and a magnificent piece of work it was. Asked
a brigade officer why he did not use the post-office plant,
for the brigade makes all its own signalling and telephone
appliances. He laughed, and said: “The post office is
10 years behind us—it would never do.” Physically, the
M.F.B. is a clever athletic troupe. The men are all kept
at the highest pitch of physical efficiency. They are
clever boxers and wrestlers, daring steeplejacks and
amazing ladder climbers. They have to be. But the old
men—a fireman becomes old in the middle of life—have
either to be kept on the strength till they die or turned
adrift. There is no pension fund. And the old men
are mostly kept on from sentimental considerations,
whereby the efficiency of the brigade is greatly impaired.
Not long ago an ancient was found dying in the street.
He expired on the way .to the hospital. That old man
had spent his whole life in the ranks of Melbourne’s fire
fighters. He had either to remain on the active strength
or go out into the unsympathetic cold. And he stayed.
He was rated on the active strength of the brigade to the
last day of his life. This sort of thing is the blot on an
otherwise magnificent system.
The Adelaide Tramway Trust has got on
far enough to accept a tender for constructing the
permanent way of the new electric lines, and thac
and the overhead work will be pushed on simul-
taneously ; though the contract for the latter part
of the job hasn t been made yet. By degrees
Adelaide is breaking itself in to look kindly upon
the whirling machines of the future, though there
are still people who have misgivings about them.
Itiver since most of Adelaide can remember, there
have been half-a-dozen different tram companies
working quite independently ; and now that the
Irust issues 14 penny tickets for a shilling, avail-
able on any penny section of any line, Adelaide is
beginning to really see that the old system was a
bit inconvenient—a great part of it only had vague
opinions on the subject previously. In adozen other
ways the Trust has already made a great improve-
ment. The cars, though as lumbering as ever, are
now moderately clean instead of beingindescribably
dirty, the horses are better kept, and a car now
rarely waits more than four minutes for a woman
and seven parcels that are puffing down a side-
street and looking as though they might be a
passenger if they got enough encouragement. It
has evidently been a hard job to break suburban
Adelaide of its faith in the patience of the tram-
car, but it is being done by degrees.
Yet in one branch of furious locomotion,
Adelaide is easily ahead of every other city of Aus-
tralia. The motor-car is so common a machine
that there is even a car-stand in Currie-
street, with an average of seven or eight vehicles
on the rank ; and when you ring up a livery stable
for a cab, it is becoming necessary to specify
whether you want it pulled by horses, or prefer
one of the sort that leaves a “bad smell behind.
Of course, the level Adelaide and suburban roads
suit the cars; but the cars don’t suit the soft
limestone with which the suburbs are in the
habit of building their tracks, and the suburban
councillor (only a “city ” in S. Aus. has aldermen)
is either ordering harder rock or is cursing vio-
lently about the dust, and threatening to make the
motor-car pay for it in some way.
“Groper” : Don’t tell me that the farm-
burner has had his day. Listen to this : Two men
in Perth assaulted and robbed an old man, and
the learned Judge, after scratching a bare place
out of his false hair, got a sudden attack of a long
dead disease. He gave one man twelve months,
and the other only five, because the latter had
fought in the Boer War. Yet the soldier who
would attack an old man would probably take
care to be out of the way when a young man w'as
shooting at him, and it might have been a fair
thing to give him 18 months, on the off
chance that he was a very bad discredit to his
country in the days when the Boer farm looked
like the last days of Pompeii.

“Vulcan” : Coaly Newcastle (N.S.W.) is
going to initiate the Commonwealth into the joys
of living in a Yankee sky-scraper. The building
—a hotel, eight storeys high—will be of the steel
skeleton type, clothed in reinforced concrete, with
roof garden, electric light and lifts, and similar
luxuries. A necessary corollary to this brand of
edifice is an efficient fire-fighting brigade. Coal-
opolis, though among the six biggest towns in
Australia, is about on a par, from the flame-com-
bating point of view, with the sort of ham-
let where the Oldest Inhabitant attaches himself
to his grandchild’s toy bucket, and dashes fren-
ziedly to the salving, what time the flames of
the doomed hostelry wherein he has caroused
since boyhood lick the midnight firmament. If
the new Newcastle structure does no more than
stir local enthusiasm on behalf of up-to-date blaze-
extinguishing methods, its hideousness will not
have been in vain.
The just-printed annual report of the
Government of Papua contains a story that doesn’t
do Queensland officialdom much credit. A Euro-
pean, who had a few years ago been acquitted of
murder on the ground of insanity, got into Daru
gaol again, and presently became very violent and
manifestly insane—among other things, he tried
to murder the Resident Magistrate. So the
unfortunate was put on board the Papuan Govern-
ment steamer with a guard, and taken to Thurs-
day Island, in the hope that the Queensland
Government would admit him to one of its
asylums. Papua offered to pay maintenance
charges, etc.; but Queensland officialdom slammed
all the doors in the man’s face, and the poor
wretch had to be carted back to Papua, to be
shut up in a police-cell—the only accommodation
available.
* * * *
After The Bulletin’s account
(16/4/’08) of the way Paris deals with its unem-
ployed, it is interesting to see how much better
the business is managed by Private Enterprise in
Australia. S. M. Herald (18/4/’08) summarises
the annual report of a local Night Refuge for men
for 1907. Thus, for a start: —
The men who fill the dormitory night after night, year
in and year out, are the castaways, as a rule, not of in-
dustrial distress, but of an irresponsible life. The com-
mittee recognises that it would not do to inquire too
closely into their lives and unhappy condition.
Which is an idiotic thing to say. Why not try to
sift the good from the bad, and secure for the
better men another chance ? But here is a fuller
statement of the practical working of the place :
During last year, 4397 different men were admitted
for shelter, each man on an average stopping four nights,
and before leaving each morning each man received a
light breakfast. (Compare that with the Parisian casual’s
hot supper of soup and bread before going to bed at
night, and his breakfast of ditto ditto in the morning, to
say nothing of the bath, and the two mattresses, and sheets
—real sheets.) During the winter months particularly,
the need of the institution is most apparent, for it is no
uncommon thing on cold, bleak nights for men to be
turned away because there is no room for them.
Strangers always have preference when the doors are
open, and sometimes the room is filled with men, the
majority of whom have never known its shelter before.
The principle thus adopted renders it almost impossible
for any man to obtain- more than a modicum of relief.
That partly accounts for the chronic unemployed
of Sydney. Instead of giving a starving man a
good supper, a bath, a clean change of clothes, a
comfortable bed and a good warm breakfast to
put heart in him to look for work outside, and
instead of helping him to do the looking, the rule
in Sydney is that the least possible help shall be
given, because the old idea about poverty and
disease, once so prevalent among fanatics, is still
cherished among the zealots who run these shelters.
THE LEPIDOPTEROUS SPREE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s an awful sound of buzzing in the air around the
town—
A buzzing which the residents would dearly like to
drown ;
It sounds like forty thousand bees about a flowering gum,
And fills the Federal atmosphere with devastating hum.
Strange insects whoosh and whiz about, they’re not a
moment still ;
They’re whirling over Deakin’s plate, and bothering old
Bill;
They’re falling into Chapman’s soup, and into Mauger’s
tea,
And generally keeping up a lepidopterous spree !
A lengthy insect, full of spite, flies straight at Deakin’s
head,
But misses him by half-an-inch, and hits the wall instead!
It shrills with wrath, and blunders back, and strikes
against a dish ;
And Old Bill says a naughty word—it’s wading in his fish!
Strange gnats and flies of every size buzz round with
might and main,
And fall into the Federal food to be fished out again ;
And with smeared heads and cluttered legs and gravy-
spattered wings * J
They whoosh about in insect wrath and buzz most direful
things !
They are an Insect Conference—they fill the Federal air
With thuds and hums and creaks and chirps and buzzings
of despair ;
Their whir and whiz just hinders business—that’s all
it does.
So the people of this continent will simply let them buzz t
N.B.W.
O. C. CABOT.
THE AMAZING ARISTOCRAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With a view to encouraging subscriptions to the
Linlithgow statue fund, the Argus is publishing local
reminiscences of “ dear Lord Hopetoun. Here is one
supplied by a Melb. banker :
“I found his Lordship, bare-headed, divested of coat
and waistcoat, carrying the gardeuer’sson, and just about
to start a race with three other youngsters.”
And here is an even more delightful anecdote betraying
the almost human attributes of a real live earl:—
“ His Excellency entered a tramcar near the top of
Collins-street. As there were but two empty seats,
opposite each other, he sat in one and his aide-de-camp
in the other, but, as chance had it, his Lordship
sat next a Chinese. The aide-de-camp, in dumb show,
offered to change seats, but by a strenuous facial grimace s
(on the far side), which looked very like a wink, the
Governor indicated that he would do nothing of the kind.
And yet a third, breathing charming insouciance and a
naive humor
“ His Lordship usually walked to church. With a friend
I passed him on one occasion. We raised our hats, and
so did he. We could not resist turning our heads to look
at him after he passed. To our amusement, he did the
same simultaneously, and waved his hand with a good-
natured acknowledgment.”
These incidents, we are told, “ illustrate the homely
camaraderie that endeared his lordship to everybody ” :
Oh, pleasing, strange, and quaint indeed,
These pretty little tales to read
Of how an earl in mufti can
Behave precisely like a man.
He lifts his hat the same as us,
He plays with youthful innocence—
And he a lord ! Mysterious
The workings are of Providence.
We’re tempted to believe that he
Ate bread and butter with his tea.
He wore these common woollen suits,
And walked about in leather boots ;
And sat unmoved, observers vow,
Within a tram, beside a Chow 1
Imagination totters when
We see the statement in cold ink,
That on a day well known to men
The earl unbended in a wink.
He winked ! We’d really like to know
Could human goodness further go !
Most wondrous though the ’mazing fact
That men not dippy, mad, or cracked,
Men sane and sentient can find
Some joy in piffle of this kind,
Can gasp incredulous delight
When any duke or earl behaves
Just like an ordinary wight.
Poor flatterers they are, these knaves,
Such acts in men should cause no fuss,
E’en though in apes they’re marvellous !
vie.

For the first time on record this splendid Australian
society (which has apparently made up its mind not to be
all-Australian any more) adds more than a million to its
funds as the result of one year’s work. Compared with
such figures, those of the boom years surrounding 1890
were small onions. In the tremendous boom-vear 1889,
for instance, in spite of the fact that the society averaged
over 6 per cent, interest, the total amount added to the
funds was only £742,312. And still, in spite of a 50 per
cent, increase in less than 20 years, Australia isn t wide
enough !
Certainly the society hasn’t, in some respects,
made the progress which it should have done, considei-
ing its quite unique advantages. The cost rate, on the
basis of premiums received, has been almost stationary
for years past. According even to the official reckoning,
the expenses amounted last year to a shade under 13 9
per cent, of the premium income, while so long as 40
years ago—in 1867—the rate was 12 per cent., though
that was rather under the average of the period. Even
compared with total income there is not the improve-
ment that there should be, remembering that a steadily
increasing amount of income is derived from cheaply-
managed investments: —
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1898 .. £9 4 0 per cent. 1903 .. £8 17 0 per cent.
1899.. 919 8 „ 1904.. 816 7 „
1900 .. 9 0 5 „ 1905 .. 815 6 „
1901 .. 810 0 „ 1906 .. 817 2 „
1902 .. 814 0 „ 1907 .. 816 5 „
The fact probably is that the A.M.P. had too good a
time. It suffered as many a mine has suffered—from the
ease with which the money came in. When a mine is
afflicted that way, bad habits grow on officers and work-
men alike—just as they grow on our savage brother Bill
who doesn’t have to work for his cocoanut. To make it
do its best, the society really needed another society
treading more closely on its heels. And there are plenty
of signs that it is getting what it needed most. For many
years past the report of the society has not been available
before May, and generally very late in May’. This year
it was in policy-holders’ hands by April 24. The alert,
aggressive Citizens’ —smaller, certainly'—sends its report
out in February, and probably that has something to do
with the extra hustle of the A.M.P.
But before we can tell whether expense rates
are low or high, it is necessary to find out how much new
business is being got in ; for a society that just sat down
and waited for its existing policy-holders to die, could
work for 4 or 5 per cent., or less if it were a big office.
Here, then, is a comparison running back 30 years. It
shows the renewal premiums (or receipts from old busi-
ness), the revenue during the year from new business,
and the total expenses. Then it is assumed that the old
business cost 7 per cent, to manage, and annuity' business
3 per cent., and the balance of the expenses is taken to
represent the cost of the new business :

Expenses are, therefore, a little lower than they were,
say, 10 years ago. But they ought to be a good deal
lower, for, on account of the great prosperity of Australia
recently, it ought to have been easier to rope in fat poli-
cies. The rate ought to have got down much nearer that
which ruled in the big boom period, represented above
by the two years 1887 and 1890. The A.M.P. has done
well. With all its advantages it ought to have done
better, and, now that it has aroused itself, it is very likely
that it will do better, unless the risky foreign business
spoils things.

The movement is all the right way—not towards

the public securities have increased from a poor
6 - 9 p.c. of the assets to 31’6 p.c. ; and behind

Policy loans go on shrinking ; and that is a good thing,
too, for when the policy loan comes in the real purpose of
insurance flies out. The assets taken into account in the
foregoing include, besides the insurance fund, a reserve
to provide for depreciation of securities (£125,158, as
against £114,988 twelve months ago). To this fund has
