year, in addition to £30,091 written off and charged
against revenue account direot. As against the £12,386
which has been added to the depreciation fund and
charged to revenue account, £13,786, being surplus on
properties sold, has been included in revenue account.

That is a fine procession of figures. S. Australia sticks
very close to its one bank, its Bank of Adelaide , and
there are very good reasons why it should think highly
of it. All through the troubles of 12 or 14 years ago,
when so many other Australian banks were breaking it
more or less gently to their shareholders that all their
capital was gone, this Bank of Adelaide continued the
even tenor of its 7 per cent, dividends. Since then,
things in S. Australia have improved in a remarkable way.
And it isn’t all attributable to good years. These past f our
seasons certainly have been better than usual, and prices
have been higher than usual; but, so far as the great
S. Australian business of agriculture is concerned, the
vastly increased use of artificial manures, and the exten-
sion of mixed farming—the latter coupled with increased
facilities for frozen-latnb export —have put a new face on
the State. The South Australian farmers had passed
through a good school, too ; they had had a desperately
bad time and had been forced into methods of very strict
economy. As a whole, therefore, the position not only
of the S. Australian farmer, but of S. Australia, is now
very sound. The farmer has paid off the local store-
keeper, the local storekeeper has cleared himself with
the wholesale merchant, and the wholesale merchant
isn’t so horribly polite to the bank manager as he was a
few years ago. From these improvements the Bank of
Adelaide, being so typical a S. Australian concern, must
have got a lot of benefit; and the benefit ought to have
extended further than the mere matter of disclosed
profits.
Yet the present balance-sheet figures show no
improvement on those of a year ago :
Mar., 1907. Mar., 1908.
Notes in circula- ,
tion .. .. £179,622 £185,540 Inc. £5,918
Bills in circula- „
lation, etc. .. 503,991 297,452 Dec. 206,539
Deposits .. 3,269,238 3,090,618 Dec. 178,620
Liquid assets .. 2,343,016 2,226,230 Dec. 116,786
Advances .. 2,256,414 2,006,688 Dec. 249,726
Premises .. 76,925 84,707 Inc. 7,782
But this bank does the business of the Government of
S. Australia, and the State’s large but irregular transac-
tions in borrowing and repaying often affect the balance-
sheet figures. Still, it is quite likely that there has been a
real shrinkage in advances, partly for the reason mentioned
just now. Further, notwithstanding the prosperity of
the State, there has been no very great amount of
new land settlement. It is the N. S. Wales and
Queensland land-subdivider that has been, flooding
South Australia with his advertisements, and imploring
the local farmer to get into country where the
raindrop isn’t so unusual. Though there has been
a steady rise in land values in most districts, this
competition of the other States has prevented much in
the way of a boom in S. Aus. ; which is probably another
reason why this bank’s advances haven’t moved up.
Later on, no doubt, the Bank of Adelaide will be thanking
its lucky star that it was so ; for there is going to be
trouble over the boom that has sent land up (in N.S.W.
particularly) to £lO an acre—in only moderate agricultural
districts too.
The Bank of Adelaide has no less than 62 per
cent, of its total debt to the public represented by liquid
assets. Under ordinary circumstances something like 35
per cent, is generally considered enough ; but on account
of this bank’s relations with the Government, it
is possible that the circumstances on any given
date are not ordinary. In any case, however, its
position appears to be strong. Its total debts are almost
exactly five-times greater than its capital and reserves,
and that is a very moderate proportion. And yet, in
spite of the unusual proportion of liquid assets (which, as
a rule, don’t earn very much), and in spite of the only-
moderate amount of public money of which the bank
has the benefit, the profits show £8 os. 3d. per cent, on
capital and reserves combined. The thing that seems
most certain from that is that the assets are an excellent
lot.

Of the half-year’s profits, however, £15,000 has been set
aside as “ provision for re-building and depreciation of
securities.” So far this £15,000 has been treated as a
reserve, and, therefore, has not been written off profits ;
but it is quite likely that it will not appear again, in
which case £15,000 will have to come off the half-year’s
profits to reduce them to net. Besides this special
appropriation, the usual £5OOO has been written off profits
to provide for depreciation of plant and machinery. Com-
pared with the present spring in profits the best high
jump at the Sydney Easter show was apoor performance ;
and there is nothing in the accounts to explain the how
or why of it. Various items compare this way
Mar., Mar., Mar.,
1900. 1907. 1908.
Debts to public £16,763 £17,777 £29,294
Properties, goodwill, plant .. 647,831 604,932 600,667
Public stock and deposits .. 92,896 183,761 237,212
Cash balances 61,727 31,638 50,103

Profits on brewings, rents, etc. 132,377 146,563 199,688

Nothing is said to account for the spring in profits from
brewings, etc., from £146,563 to £199,688. Times have
been good ; but they were good also in 1906-7,
and the improvement since then (if there has
been any) would hardly explain the increase shown.
There may have been recoveries of debts written
off as bad ; also it may not have been thought necessary
to write off as much profit as a provision for bad debts.
Anyway, the change is remarkable. In eight years, the
co. has increased its liquid assets by £142,692 and its
stock by £57,117; and meantime the co.’s debt to the
public has been increased by only £12,531, while no fresh
capital has been got in from the shareholders. Certainly,
“ properties, goodwill, plant, etc.,” have shrunk ; but it
is pretty certain that that has been less from sales than
from amounts written off out of profits. The position,
based on past records, is, therefore, very strong. (It
should be mentioned that the table above opens with
the first 8 per cent, dividend in recent years; for ten
years preceding that, 7 per cent, was paid. But it will
also be noted that the £150,000 or so added to liquid assets
in the past eight years brings in probably £6OOO a year,
equal to two-thirds of 1 per cent, on the capital.) Whether
the past is a good guide to the future in the whole-
sale and retail Bung business isn’t to be judged
from balance-sheets and revenue accounts. Lately
in N. S. Wales special Licensing Courts have been can-
celling licenses and throwing beer-pumps out of action.
But it has yet to be seen how brewery profits will be
affected in the long run. No doubt less beer will be
drunk when only nine pub doors gape than when 12
gape ; but the reduction in the quantity of beer swallowed
isn’t likely to amount to one-fourth. There will, however,
be a big reduction in expenses ; and the brewery will get
a lot of that advantage. Taken all round, the future of
the Bung business is obscure and uncertain, and that sort
of investment has become more or less of a gamble—with
no prospects certainly of any very sudden knock-out.

This society was born in 1886, in the days of Queensland’s
Great Continuous Drunk, and it bears the birthstain to
this day. At the end of its ninth year it found that it
had freehold properties, mostly foreclosures, which had
cost about £120,000, and were'estimated to be worth no
more than £62,083. An attempt was made to square
things in 1896 by writing the shares down from £l to
12s. 6d., and getting in an unpaid ss. per share. But
shareholders either couldn’t come up to scratch or had
no hope that anything would be left after the deposits,
debentures, and other debts of about £60,000 had been
paid ; consequently shares had to be forfeited whole-
sale, and of the 148,398 which had been subscribed, no
less than 59,235 have been ruled out, notwithstanding
that no attempt was made to get in more than half-a-
crown a share. Very little progress has been made in
the way of realising the assets. In fact, it is only during
the past three or four years that Queensland has been
able to hold up its head and remember that it had such
things as assets. Even now there is no demand worth
mentioning for city and suburban lands; Queensland’s
small boom has been practically all in the country.
Therefore the present position compares in only a
moderately satisfactory way with that of 10 years back

tell. Moreover, on present appearances, this generation
won’t be in the best of health when the final panning-out
does arrive ; for it has taken 10 years to work off £11,894
worth (book value) of assets, and £50,553 worth still re-
main on hand.

The principal recent work of this co. has been to freeze
the shareholders. So far as can be traced, no dividend
has ever been paid, and for many years past the works
have not been used, except at a few odd times, when
somebody has been got to lease them for a while. Twelve
months ago, with the rapid increase of flocks, it looked
as though the co. might be able to do something fairly
profitable; but while most of the other parts of
N. S. Wales have had a good time, drought has looked in
again on the Riverina, and there is no stock that would
pay to freeze. Altogether, in fact, back-country works
of this kind have been a ghastly failure in N. S. Wales :
those established at Menindie and Bourke, for instance,
were a ghastlier failure than even this ven-
ture has been. Though the list printed above
shows nominal reserves of £4667, there is pro-
bably a large actual deficit, because the amounts
written off—amounts really spent on repairs and main-
tenance —can’t represent anything like the depreciation
of the plant. To add to its troubles the co. has £15,000
of 5 per cent, debentures. These, which were renewed a
few years ago, fall due next year. Still the co. doesn’t
consist of men who need be worried over a little £15,000.
The Horsfalls, Austins, and others of that size are in-
terested, and they probably regard the works as some-
thing- that may come in handy some day, when the
country breeds more cattle and sheep than it knows what
to do with, and when it may be an advantage to have a
convenient pot to put them into. But it will probably be
many a long day before it won’t pay better to truck them
alive to Melbourne or Sydney, and deal with the surplus
there.

So, without deducting money spent on development and
charged to that account, the profit for the half-year was
£BB6, as against £1867 in the previous term. The form of
the accounts has been somewhat altered ; hitherto receipts
from coal sales have been distinguished from other
receipts. All, however, are now lumped together ; but
“ other receipts ” have lately not amounted to more than
a few pounds for rent. The position disclosed is not very
satisfactory : coal sales have fallen off, admittedly be-
cause some bords have been worked out. In view of that
admission, it is very doubtful whether the development
account can be regarded as a good asset, at any rate for
20s. in the £. The proper course, obviously, is to wipe
the item out by means of a regular charge upon each ton
of coal mined ; and if that course had been adopted, the
small profit shown during the last two terms would
have been further reduced. “ Flotation and preliminary
expenses, £906,” still also figures as an item among the
assets ; and it is surely time that that was written off out
of profits. At present the situation is that that much
capital is not represented by anything shareholders can
see or smell or pawn. However, the co. has a good area,
and it is now sinking and boring—the latter with the object
of getting the big East Greta seam which ought to be found
in this property. Thecapital is £42,573 in ordinary shares,
£20,844 in pref. shares, and £4OOO in debentures ; the co.
also has overdrafts of £2558. It began with too little
capital nnd too much debenture debt, even presuming
plain sailing ; but, instead of plain sailing, it struck fire
and other misfortune, and had to issue the pref. shares,
which have made a sad change in the outlook for the
ordinary shareholders. Nothing has been written off the
book-value of the freehold, in spite of the very con-
siderable extraction of coal, and it does not appear that
the plant has been written down to cover depreciation;
so it seems highly probable that the actual results are
not even as good as they look on the surface.

During the year the capital was increased from £15,000t0
£24,854, partly by declaring a dividend of 50 per cent,
and applying the money to a new' share issue, partly by
getting in a little outside cash. The effect of the change
is that old shareholders will have three shares instead of
two to play with ; moreover, a 15 per cent, dividend
can masquerade as one of 10 per cent., so con-
sumers won’t feel so annoyed about it. The
amount shown as written off last year (£375) is really
the amount collared by the canny Kidston by way of tax
on this so-called “ dividend ” of 50 p.c. ; to show it as
written off seemed the most convenient way of getting
rid of the item. Nothing else was written off to cover
depreciation or otherwise, though £522 was spent on
renewals and charged to revenue account. The co. has
been adding a little to its works, but at the end of the
year there was still a credit balance at the bank (£243),
while there was an overdraft of £1757 twelve months ago.
Still, as against the credit balance, there were sundry
creditors for £616. With the recent juggle a new form
of accounts has arrived, and those now presented, having
got the bad lumping habit, leave a good deal more to
the imagination than the old accounts used to. It is the
old business: in connection with the supply of public
necessaries and the profit it makes out of them, Private
Enterprise comes to see that it is safer to put as much of
its transactions as possible away in the dark—" out of
sight, out of mind,” it hopes.

It was the late W. R. Wilson who first went
along the east coast of Asia to find out whether Australian
lead producers couldn’t find a market nearer than London ;
and the trade, both in silver and lead, has grown so
vastly that nowadays the Broken Hill Prop, has Chinese
and other Asiatic customers for 6000 tons of lead per
month. Most of this is used up in linings for tea chests,
etc. ; but hundreds of tons go towards making a face
powder, prodigiously used by Chinese women. The
B. H. Prop.’s output of metals hasn’t been seriously
affected by the shrinkage in prices ; for the last four weeks
385,1380 z. silver and 7101 tons lead w’ere refined, but this
includes 105,2710 z. silver and 2755 tons lead from
purchased ores. Besides this, there were zinc
concentrates containing 2530 tons zinc, 97,9330 z. silver,
and 640 tons lead. Very little of this lead is, however,
paid for by the European spelter-makers, who, as a rule,
allow nothing at all for the first 8 per cent, of lead. Ine
co. is still enlarging its smelting output, and has lately
made a contract with the Sulphide Corporation for some
of its surplus lead concentrates.

ores on a large scale; The British Mine will economise

The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches ” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“Shaw’’Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by‘ the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has only one working part—the hammer —which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. It some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2£in. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [4

Whether the B. H. Prop, really isn’t earning enough now
to pay £144,000 a half-year can’t be said for certain.
Chairman Darling- says it isn’t ; and it ought to be re-
membered (but often isn’t), that though the co. paid
£288,000 last half-year, the disclosed profits were only
£lB7/42 net. Further, lead was £l5 18s. 9d. per ton on
Novei. her 80 (the end of the half-year), and silver was
2s. 2fd. standard, whereas the metals now are worth
about £l3 7s. 6d. and 2s. 0 11-16 d. The shock the market
professes to get when these mines don’t pay regular
quarterly divs. simply proves that even now it hasn’t got
them in the right perspective, but still views them from
the recent boom view-point.
* * * ■*
“Watcher”: The Zinc Corporation’s plant at
Broken Hill has now begun to produce on a commercial
basis, and the results are said to be satisfactory to the
management. On the principle that a sprat is better
than no fish, that may be so. But, on the work at
present being done, the profits, if any, must be small.
Take the Corporation’s published figures. The Block 10
tailings, which the Corporation has treated, totalled 4790
ton 9, assaying 17 p.c. zinc, filoz. silver, and 4 p.c. lead,
whio'h produced 1359 tons zinc concentrates assaying 44
p.c. zinc, 13oz. silver, and 7 p.c. lead, and 58 tons of lead
concentrates assaying 50 p.c. lead, 35£oz. silver and 10
p.c. zinc. To start with, then, the tailings treated con-
tained 814.3 tons of zinc, and of this 597.90 tons were re-
covered in the zinc concentrates. The recovery of zinc can
therefore be set down at 73.4 p.c. Of the 191.0 tons of
lead contained in the tailings, only 32‘48 tons were re-
covered, equal to a recovery of 16’9 per cent. The lead in
the zinc concentrates cannot be taken into account as
recovered, because it is not worth anything to the Cor-
poration. The zinc contained in the lead concentrates is
also of no value to the Corporation, as no payment would
be reoeived for it; in fact, such a high percentage of zinc
in a lead concentrate would considerably increase the re-
turning charge on the concentrate —at the rate of perhaps
Is. per unit for all over 10 units. Even the philanthropic
B. H. Prop, would hardly offer better terms than that.
Therefore the only zinc that can be regarded as recovered
is contained in the zinc concentrate, because that
is the only zinc the Corporation will be paid for. Of
course, the silver contained in the zinc concentrates will
be worth a little ; the smelters would probably pay for
about half of it at. So, as half the silver will be
paid for, only half of it can be said to have been
recovered, and this, together with the silver con-
tained in the lead concentrate, gives a recovery of silver
of 84 per Cent. Looked at in this way, the result is not
anywhere near as brilliant as the Corporation’s bald
figures would suggest.
° * ■» * *
If something doesn’t happen along very soon in
the way of a buyer for the Broken Hill South’s surplus
output of concentrates, that co. will have a big white
elephant to keep in its stable. The new mill, just com-
pleted, is a fine, up-to-date structure, but, so far, there
isn’t anything for it to do. The old mill is working to
only about half its capacity, and even then it turns out
more concentrates than there are buyers for. In its
predicament the South has one consolation. A new con-
centrating mill always takes two or three months to pro-
perly adjust, so the dull time ahead won’t be all wasted.
r • * * *
The desperate efforts made by the B. H. Junction
North to drag working costs down from their elevated
position threaten to stir up a hornet’s nest. For a long
time past miners and truckers have shaken a deprecatory
forefinger at the man who sets the contracts in that mine,
but up to now the aggrieved ones’ only resource has been
to “ pull out.” That meant irregular tonnages for the
mine, but it also meant lower working costs. Unfortu-
nately, the situation arising from this state of things is
likely to jeopardise harmonious relations between
owners and men all along the line of lode.
* * * *
At Wyalong (N.S.W.) all the properties are
again open under the new working conditions. More
outside attention has lately been directed to this field,
and already Sydney cos. control such properties as
Barrier, Homeward Bound, Brookman’s, and, lastly, the
Klondike, where they are making extensive preparations
for following the reef down in the deep country. The
syndicate is not having very good luck with the Called
Back, but intends to continue development work. The
Operator lease is being reshuffled by the Mines Depart-
ment, another scramble for possession having recently
taken place. This time there are some dozen applicants
for it.
- * * * •
For a mine that was awarded columns of gush
in the boom time, the Cangai, near Grafton (N.S.W.),
is still managing to produce mighty little in the way of
results. The last week’s work (three furnaces) showed
141 tons ore smelted for 31J tons matte. But the pro-
duct was of such poor grade (39 per cent.) that the
copper contents were barely over 12 tons. Cangai may
be building up tremendous reserves ; if it isn’t, 141 tons
is a ridiculous output for a mine that scatters remarks
concerning 7ft. faces of solid ore almost as freely as if it
were another Mt. Morgan or Lyell.
* * * *
This year the want-of-firewood trouble at
Lloyd’s mine (N.S.W.) has started very early in the
winter. Manager Corbould’s idea last year was to build
a cheap tram to get out into the timber, the furnaces
having cleared nearly everything that is combustible in
the neighborhood ; but mine and market both went back
on the co., and when the directors looked into the till
they doubted whether there was the money there to do
it. So things drifted on in the old w’ay, except that
every month the fuel got more remote, and now supplies
have failed again, and the stoppage of work is once more
visible in the distance.
* * * *
The people and papers that boomed the Light-
ning Ridge opal-field so outrageously, and hurried scores
of men out into the remote far west (N.S.W.), probably
feel a bit ashamed of themselves now. For weeks past
practically no gem has been found. The blanks in opal-
mining at White Cliffs were bad enough; at Lightning
Ridge they are simply murderous.
» * * »
London and Western Australian Exploration
Co., which buys mines and other delusions, and also
dabbles in shares, had a bad time last year. Receipts,
principally dividends on investments, totalled £8556 ; but
the expenditure was no less than £33,325, made up chiefly
of loss on other speculations, £13,419 ; abandoned
options, £6628 ; and Australian expenses, £9414. Thus a
loss of £24,769 was shown. The book values of the co.’s
two principal investments—the “ Sink ” Corporation and
the Lancefield—also showed a heavy shrinkage at the
time the accounts were made up ; but instead of writing
them down to their then market valuation, the directors
provided a reserve of £lOO,OOO for depreciation. This
they hope will be extinguished by and by ; and so does
Ihe Bulletin, though it has mighty little faith. The co.
recently gave Australia the cold shoulder, and is now
prospecting for suitable properties in the Malay States.
Meantime the five directors continue to divide £lOOO in
fees, and spend an additional £2OOO in London office ex-
penses, and if on past records shareholders think they
are getting value for the money, they must be a very
easily pleased lot.

* * * *
Boulder Main Reef Co. (W.A.) is thinking of
applying for Government assistance to deepen the main
shaft from 1900 ft. to 2500 ft. The management is vain
enough to believe that one or more of the Boulder shoots
will be struck at that depth, but that is an opinion which
quite a number of people don’t share. Meantime, the
mill has re-started, and the old tale that sufficient revenue
will be earned to pay working expenses and nearly cover
cost of current development is being told. Whether, in
Spite of this announcement, the working capital will
rapidly melt away and render another reconstruction
imperative remains to be seen. The chances are that it
will.
* * * *
The local press still praises the Coolgardie
Redemption (W.A.). The latest puff states that it “ pro-
mises to develop into one of the best propositions in
Coolgardie”; but, as practically all the shows there are
derelicts, that is not saying much. The Redemption
really belongs to the played-out class of mine so common
in the West, that would only have prospects if everybody
worked for nothing and no one charged anything, and
somehow that sort of man takes a lot of finding.
* » * *
Barrambie Ranges (W.A.) has put out £30,000
of the £90,000 worth of gold which was said to he in sight,
and is now grubbing round to find the rest. The flicker
of hope it lives on is that it may hit gold at 200 ft. In
true Victorian fashion, sinking has been postponed until
the mine is pretty well at a standstill. Rock drills in
such country should have been the first thought. With
the eyes of the directors fixed on the big gold estimate,
that detail was overlooked, although driving and sinking
were costing just about 50 percent, more than they should.
If the Board had any pluck, and any faith in the mine, it
would call up some capital and complete the equipment.
No. 2 crosscut will soon tell whether the gold lives to
200 ft. If it does, shareholders should put on their biggest
hob-nailed boots before setting out to make a radical
change in the Board.
* * * *
Electric pumps and other oddments do not help
the Duke and Main Lead Consols, Maryborough (Vic.), on
the road to health, strength, and wealth. As with all
deep-lead bleeders, call comes on top of call, so £70,000 of
capital and gold have gone, with nary a sprat
returned to shareholders. There is plenty of
Mark Tapleyan congratulation from directors that
remunerative work is at hand at No. 2 shaft. With the
gutter dipping below the level of the main drive, and a
forest of timber needed to log up the wash drives, some-
thing more than the 13dwt. dirt being got will be wanted
to show any margin of profit. The ground is too green to
give the best result, but the co. is one of the hard-ups,
and must keep going. After all, the mine lies round No. 1
shaft. Work there is a bit further on than six months back.
There is some nice wash, but it has all the patchiness
of the Duke lead, and that has proved fatal to the Duke
United near by. Still, Duke and Main Lead shares hold
their own with a bit of judicious propping.
* * # *
David Syme dipped pretty deeply into mining,
but as a rule he didn’t let his name appear. His family
has not the same reserve, for J.H. toots the horn over the
Argo dredge and other shows. This Argo has been a dis-
tinctly costly Victorian show. Now a rift in the cloud
appears, as better gold is in the wash. But costs run to
6id. per cubic yard, so good dirt must come to pay divs.
ft * * *
Briseis Extended (Tas.) has a vast hole in the
ground, and a neat little gap in its banking account. The
claim is about the most doleful of the Tasmanian tin
boom relics, not omitting the Cybele—more doleful still
because of broken promises and unfulfilled expectations.
In all, £27,000 has gone in plant and work, and the half-
year’s operations show a loss of £948; the actual cash
position of the eo. is nearly £5OO to the bad. Another pro-
mise of good things ahead is now held out; the manager
says that when he lowers the pump to the bottom
he will get good results. If he does not, the directors
should let in the Ringarooma River, and moor the barge
as a monument to wasted effort. The best tin should be
at the bottom ; the cost of elevating should be light ; so
Pennington has a chance there. But what afterwards?
1k fc * *
Zeehan is in crape over the Tasmanian Smelting
Co. As Premier Evans refuses to help the co. other than
by a reduction in freights, the plant is to be shut down.
This hits half the mines under the belt, and throws a big
body of workers idle. If the proposed Rosebery smelter
were up, things would not be so bad ; but it isn’t, and that
ends the matter for the present.
* * * *
The feature of recent developments at the North
Lvell has been the proving of ore bodies 50ft. wide and
270 ft. long on what may be termed the ground floor of
the 850 ft. level. Ore reserves have largely increased, for,
except as to a few feet above the 850 ft. level, some of
these big blocks are practically solid up to the 700 ft. level.
But with irregular bodies conservative estimates have to
be made. The best development at 1000 ft. has been the
downward continuation of No. 23 stope ore body. This
has been opened 'soft. wide and 40ft. long, with good,
even-grade ore showing at both ends. It is this develop-
ment that has kept the Lyell crowd in good heart.
Prospecting is going on vigorously to pick up the down-
ward continuation of No. 20 stope ore body, but it seems
none too easy to find.
* * * *
New Golden Gate, Mathinna, gives
gold-mining a needed fillip by striking what seems to bp
a new make of stone at 1900 ft. —the deepest at
which payable stone has been got in the island. lids a
little early, in fact, to say that this lot is payable ; but
the quality (24dwt.) is ail right, and only the quantity
remains in doubt. , ,;
* * * *
Queensland still shivers when it considers its
gold returns. Last year’s were chilly, but the total out-
put for this March was only £151,164, as againfet £168,792
in March, 1907. Gympie’s fall is greatest ;>its total out-
put was a poor 32630 z., or less than half that of the pre-
vious March. Apart from the Etheridge,! which includes
the new Oaks rush, Mt. Morgan is practically the only
field that holds its end up.
* * * *
Aldershot Works, run to a by the
English-owned Queensland ' Smelting Co., found the
mortgagee on the doormat last week. The solicitors for
the debenture-holders took possession of the works, and
it looks as if they will find the- security of very little
value. It is understood that the Rothschilds are the
debenture-holders. The Pinnacles mine, outside Broken
Hill, once in the hands of the Q. S. Co., was also mortgaged
to the Rothschilds, so that the whole concern appears now
to have been lost by the co. Aldershot Works are badly
situated and badly laid out. Two or three times in the
history of the co. Australian brains and energy nearly
pulled the concern out of the mud, but after assuming
each time a little air of respectability and cleanliness, it
dropped back into the ditch again. This time it seems a
certainty it will stay there. It’s a pity, for Queensland
wants a smelter badly ; but the Aldershot Works cannot
hope to fill the bill successfully. High treatment costs
will always render it an impossibility from the producer’s
point of view.
* * * ■*
Mount Elliott (Q.), which got its tramway Bill
through, is looking exceedingly well. The oxidised zone
is gradually being replaced by sulphides. At 280 ft. the
ore is partially mineralised, and averages nearly 15 per
cent., copper, with better gold values than have been
hitherto met with. Sinkingisbeing rapidlypushedforward,
and the shaft is half way down to v the next level, where it
is expected that the ore will be sufficiently mineralised to
warrant smelting being, started on a good basis. The
original intention of the co. was to treat the oxidised ore
for black copper, but the prospect of abundance of
sulphide ores will make the smelting proposition a much
simpler one. The furnace will not be blown in until these
sulphides are properly opened up.
* * * *
J. Bowie Wilson, who took H. P. Seale’s place
as mine manager at Mount Morgan when the latter went
up to the post of assistant general manager, has resigned
from the job. For the present the mine is apparently
looking after itself. Mount Morgan is practically held
together by its brainy young men, and what would happen
to the mine and undertaking generally if Seale were to
vacate, those in the know hesitate to say.
A man who is used to making tallies cuts up the
population of the Oaks rush (N.Q.) this way: Making
good money, 200 ; making wages, 300 ; carrying their
tools about, 500. You can hire dry-blowers on the field ;
but most of the dirt is carted to water, and that licks up
12s. a ton. The field has one advantage: “tucker” is
fairly cheap—the Q.M.E. Co. is selling beef at 4d. per lb.
“ The best little rush since the Palmer,” an old battler
describes it; “but it’s not big enough for even the men
who are already on it.”
• * « *
Some permanent good may come of the Oaks
(Queensland) gold rush, which so far has yielded probably
6000 oz. of gold. Some of the men are turning their
attention to the unbroken stone, and one or two payable
leaders are being folkwed. A few years ago the Einas-
leigh copper mine, in that district, sent a fair-sized parcel
of ore to Cockle Creek, and over and above 22 per cent,
copper it was found to contain 3oz. silver and 12dwt.
gold per ton. That auriferous stone will ultimately be
found in payable quantities in the neighborhood is as cer-
tain as anything in mining can be.
• * * *
A deputation of members for mining districts
urges Queensland Minister for Mines Blair to establish a
State battery and State smelter. Miners reckon they are
not getting a fair deal from the battery ow'ners,
who, they urge, are the buyers of the product. A
State battery could safely be undertaken by the Govern-
ment, especially if it is to treat tin stone; but
a £3OOO State smelter at Mareeba, as asked for, will pro-
bably be a vehicle for losing money. Miners undertake
to put up a third or half of the sum if the Govern-
ment will go the other half. The suggestion is a
fair one, but will probably end in loss to both
parties. If the Government goes in for smelting,
it must be prepared to spend a heap of money
in doing the thing properly; and the miners on their
side must be prepared to guarantee a certain tonnage of
material, so that the scheme can be worked out on
business lines from the inception. To build a smelter to
treat nothing in particular is a wild undertaking. It is
like putting up a butter factory before one is assured that
there are cows to supply the cream, or grass to feed the
cows. If the miners have got the ore, let them go to the
Government and say “ We’ll guarantee you so much
copper.” Then the industry starts right away as an
industry, and not as a rank speculation.
* • * •
The recently-reported manganese-cobalt find
near Gympie has been quickly followed by another at
Pittsworth (Q.), where a clay formation has been dis-
covered carrying a small band of mineral containing- both
these metals. The percentage is very small, however,
and the manganese is therefore valueless ; but cobalt is
undoubtedly present, and as the metal is worth some-
where near 50s. a unit, it may be worth while to consider
the advisability of concentrating the ore. There should
not be much difficulty in obtaining in this way a product
containing about 5 per cent, cobalt. Whether there is
sufficient ore available to justify this treatment remains
to be proved.
* * * *
Charters Towers has now jumped to the con-
clusion that the long extension of the famous Day Dawn
reef 150 ft. or so over the boundary of Mills’ United is a
reef distinct from the Brilliant with which it had been
associated. It was the improved look of the reef as the
level was extended that caused the flutter in shares. If
the reef maintains its value of £2 19s. 2d. per ton for an
average width of 3ft., the New Brilliant Freehold will
have to sink its shaft and open out on the Day Dawn
shoot systematically. That is why the Towers is jubilant;
it means renewed life for a field rather down on its luck.
They are accustomed to see these shoots of mineral
stone with a flat underlie pass from one lease to another ;
but the speculator generally gets about two years ahead
of the event —as, for instance, in the case of the New
Brilliant Freeholds. Even now there are punters taking
a long shot at Brilliant Block or East Mexican shares. A
while back the latter were in great favor because the ex-
tension of the higher levels of Mills’ United Day Dawn
shoot was thought to be a certainty.
* * * *
South Glanmire and Monkland, Gympie (Q.),
has got an improvement in its No. 2 reef at the 1100 ft.
level ; but manager Jewell, in his quiet way, mentioned,
a few days since, that the gold was not so good as the
appearance of the black slate floors had led them to ex-
pect. ,1 South Glanmire needs something good, for shares
had, .a, neck-breaking drop a while ago. A Queensland
Government official was recently sent up to examine the
old Gympie mines that have sacked so many hands. The
resurrection of an old mining camp is rather a big order,
but the Government is going to do what it can by helping
iV legitimate ” prospecting. The East Oriental and Glan-
mire offers encouragement: at the 2174 ft. level the for-
mation is large and the ore can be cheaply mined ; the
average so far has been about lldwt. The mine keeps 20
head going. It may be only a temporary enrichment of
the reef ; but, even so, it is something to go on with.

Mount Molloy (Q.), which made the shocking
miscalculation regarding the cost of its tramway, and then
drifted along until the Q. N. Bank talked a quantity of
very plain English to it, proposes to raise the wind by
issuing 50,000 pref. shares of £l, at a discount of 10s.
That is to say, the shares are to be £l, but shareholders
are only to pay 10s. for them, and they are only to be
preferential until the 10s. has been repaid. There will be
no difficulty in getting the money, especially as the rail-
way ought to put Molloy in the way of doing a good cus-
tom-smelting business when times are opportune.
No less than 105 more coke ovens have either
been just completed or are nearing completion at Bulli
(N.S.W.). For a few 7 years past N.S.W. has been supply-
ing all its own coke requirements, yet 20 years ago it was
alleged that “ Australia couldn’t make coke,” and it
probably wouldn’t have seriously tried to do it if Dibbs
hadn’t arrived with a stiff Protective duty. There was
then just the same howl about “ penalising tjie mining
industry ” that there is to-day ; yet to-day coke costs less
than one-fourth of w-hat the imported coke used to cost,
and Protection, instead of “penalising the mining
industry,” makes it possible to smelt ores which were
formerly left untouched. And it isn’t good coal w'hich is
being used up either ; it is the small stuff which in most
cases was formerly thrown on a dump and treated as
profitless waste.

There is no better medicine for babies than

Are Moustaches Fashionable ? One is com-
pelled to believe so when he sees the number of men—
reputedly the leaders in men’s styles—wearing- this
pirsute ornament. And to the man who cannot grow one
this must be annoying, and often times a disadvantage.
Still there is hope for him. “ Capillarine’’—prepared
from a noted dermatologist’s private formula—is guaran-
teed to produce a strong, handsome moustache where
none can be grown at present. It is harmless and un-
detectable in use—very forceful as a moustache grower—
and has a successful career of 25 years behind it as a re-
commendation. Messrs. Craig and Aitken, of 654 George
Street, are the proprietors. " They post “Capillarine”
free, anywhere, for 10s. 6d.*

THE FUNERAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They are carting him along,
And it seems a trifle wrong
That Billy, rightful King of Merriniee,
Should be going home to rest
With his badge upon his breast,
And not a soul to sing corroboree.
In the dawn of Sixty-Three
Such a strapping buck was he,
And his nulla made his foeman’s noddle ring ;
And in sundry tribal rows,
And in battling for a spouse,
Did he prove himself as every inch a king.
But his bitter bane was Rum,
And whene’er his soul was glum.
He liquored free to glad his bosom tearful ;
And whene’er his soul was gay
He would put some more away,
Just to keep his heart a-feeling doubly cheerful.
In the savage days of yore
He was savage to the core,
And would dine upon a comrade now and then •
But as times grew dull and tame,
He had turned to other game,
But he never ceased to love his fellow-men.
Though his dusky mates had gone,
Still he lingered on and on,
When the whites of land and living had bereft him ;
But to-night he’ll soundly sleep
In a narrow bed and deep,
In the only plot this callous world has left him.
Doff your hats and let him pass
To his couch beneath the grass ;
He was Majesty, though sable, when we knew him ,
And what’er his failings be,
He was King in Merrimee
Till another Sable Monarch overthrew him.
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
A BORIGIN[
ALITIES
A while ago Resident Magistrate Jiear
made what is alleged to be the first regular inquiry
ever made concerning the Barau tribe in Papua.
The Baraus are nomads and an extraordinary little
lot. Naturally, on a first acquaintance, Jiear
couldn’t find out very much about them ; and
amongst the things that he couldn’t find was a
single case in which there was more than one
child living of the same parentage. Jiear can’t
say for certain, but the practice seems to be to
smother every child after the first. Probably
there isn’t more than some truth in the yarn ;
because if two people only leave one child behind
them, that tribe pretty obviously must have been
a tremendous tribe to start with, or it hasn’t been
carrying on the cheerful business very long.
A DEBATABLE QUESTION.
LEWIS: “ I see the doctors are disputin' about
which is the best side to lie on in bed, the
left or the right.”
LONNEN (the curtain-lectured): “If they're like
my missus they'll find there's a great deal to
be said on both sides."
“ Camp-Oven ” : I had the collie pup with
me when we sighted a huge porcupine scoffing
ants. The pup immediately waded in, whereupon
porky rolled up pincushion-wise. I had great
difficulty in separating the pair for the second
round ; but after I had made the separation,
helped by a hardwood rail, the pup showed no
anxiety to again leave his corner. Then P.
Cushion started to roll down the hillside. After
smashing Postle’s down-hill record to smithereens
he cannoned against a sapling, and effected a neat
pocket shot into a neighboring gully. The pup is
off “pork.” Coming inside the house he even
shied at the door-mat.
“Itan ” : They are talking rain records in
Bananaland now, since the great sheets of rain
have been swilling the dry west and turning the
valleys into rivers and lakes. But the storms of
Outback are not in it compared with the moisture
that occasionally happens to the coast. At Nam-
hour, a decade ago, 120 in. fell in three months.
Everything went blue-mouldy—clothes, boots,
books. While this steaming was going on, a
drought was cracking the black soil of the Darling
Downs, and breaking the hearts of the cockies.
But weather is a strange, unaccountable female
altogether. One recent morning, Watson’s Bay,
down Sydney Harbor, got nearly 2in. of rain
within a couple of hours; yet when the ferries
landed their cargoes of dripping brollies at the
city, dust was playing about the Quay, and
making fun of the water-carts.

“ R.N. ” writes :
Am no “ theatrical manager ” of the type who wailed
and whirled so polysyllabically re buckjumping
(B. 26/3/’08), yet the other night I felt parlously inclined
to agree with that verbose person in re the cruelty of the
business. Understand that I don’t disapprove of the
game where the horses are willing buckers. It then seems
to me to resolve itself into a fair go between man and
beast, where the only genuine annoyance the latter may
he said to suffer is that of having 12 or 13 stone
of masculinity adhering to its spine. But on a cer-
tain night last week things were far otherwise.
About half the horses that were led into the ring refused
to buck up to anywhere near the form of their riders.
For their docility the poor brutes got bad gruellings with
cutting-whips—which brand of whip, in the hands of a
Waite or a Dempsey, is an agonising weapon. No
rational, unhysterical male objects to seeing professional
horsemen tackle genuine outlaws like Snips, Cooee, etc. ;
hut the spectacle of the tame, weary, bogus outlaw, who
has to be lashed about the head before he will begin his
outlawry, arouses pity, and an overflowing measure of
sickened disgust in regard to his assailants.
* * * *
“ W.J.A.” : In the Warrego, in dry times
when the water is low, tremendous cod-fish are
found in the deep holes. How old they are no
man knoweth. Their eyes are deep sunken, their
skins lined and leathery looking, and they are
very hungry. A shearers’ cook caught one of
them a little while back, and as the boys didn’t
care to take it on, he benevolently presented it to
the church bazaar to be raffled for at Is. a time. The
bazaar was some weeks ahead, so they tethered
the cod by the top lip, and flung it a few frogs
occasionally to go on with. It was eventually
won by Dooley, a Roman Catholic, who, in a fit
of generosity, donated it to his own church, which
also contemplated a bazaar. As it w r as a good
asset, it was again put on a diet of frogs, with occa-
sional grasshoppers, etc. In due course the second
bazaar eventuated, and a shearer was the fortu-
nate winner. Then they adjourned to the hotel,
and had a “ Tambaroora ” for the fish, and finished
up by being fired into the road at an early hour.
As by this time the fish was thoroughly impreg-
nated with frogs, grasshoppers, lizards, and the
like, and ceased to be a payable asset, they de-
cided to sell it to the Chinaman. I don’t know
how much it realised, for I wasn’t present when
they cut the money out in beer, but I have the
word of the yellow Pagan who ate it that its flesh
was “ welly goo’.”

“ Cict ” : I£e“Polstan” (B. 2/4/’08) and
the frilled lizard’s frill. Wherefore art thou
“ Polstan,” and why art thou so ? You carefully
exude the mis-statement that the reptile neither
uses its Elizabethan tucks and flounces (why not
call it a Susannahan decoration ? ) as a storehouse
nor yet as a bank, and in the next sentence you
orate thusly : “The frill is used as a receptacle
merely in which to store rations for future refer-
ence, or use, or consumption, or something.”
That was my contention, and you further back it
up by narrating your experience when holding a
post mortem on the animal, where you quote the
contents of its frill as insects, etc. As to your
concluding clause, wherein you assert that its
folds are only there for the convenient stowing
away of the apparatus when not in use, what’s its
apparatus, anyway, if it’s not grasshoppers?

THE OUTRAGE.
BYSTANDER (referring to infuriated

“ Pewter Plate ” : I have never seen any-
one run to print over the unusual habits of some
Western District (Vic.) lakes ! There is consider-
able individuality about some of them. Take, for
instance, Bullen Merri and Gnotuk. A strip of
land about 150yds. wide keeps them from mixing.
Gnotuk, which is saucer-shaped and slightly fresher
than the Dead Sea, rejoices in a water level 26ft.
higher than its shamrock-shaped neighbor, Bullen
Merri, which nestles up against its bosom.
Bullen Merri, by the way, means, in abo-
riginal lingo, as near as possible, decree
nisi, and obtained its name through an errant
lubra who leaped from the tribal dock one night
on the occasion of a great divorce trial at the
Gnotuk assizes. She fled into the dark and gloomy
wave and did the trudgeon across to the camp fire
of the loved but lone co-respondent, who belonged
to a neighbor tribe on the opposite shore —two
miles off. The lady wasn’t pursued because the
tribal John Hops were afraid their uniforms might
wash off. Gnotuk protests at times against the
presence of a white man’s burial depdt on its best
bank by emitting, without any warning, the most
abominable stench. The origin of this hefty
flavor no one knows—or else hasn’t told. It
doesn’t emanate from weed or anything putrify-
ing on the beach, as some reckon, because the
smell smells as bad in winter as in summer. The
water of Gnotuk is so salt that a dead dog never
gets any deader, so long as it keeps out in the
brine, and doesn’t lie too long on the water’s
edge and bask in the sun. And if you dangle your
legs for an hour where Gnotuk laps your boots,
said boots will emerge covered with an incrusta-
tion like unto limestone. Fourteen years ago
Gnotuk subsided hurriedly in one act sft. with a
roar that shook its banks, and frightened a young
couple philandering near by. The two were so
scared that they got married next day. But per-
chance some other Abo. bloke would like to tell
that yarn ?

“ Edyson ” : The prim gentleman in the
corner had not been invited to join in the conver-
sation, but he did not merely join in—he took it
over. He had a distinctly decorous appearance,
and from his sober garb and his grave manner I
had placed him as a parson of a sect that was not
strict in the matter of neck-wear, nor about the cut
of a collar. “ Your conversation, gentlemen,” he
said, “recalls to my mind the distressing error of
judgment that prostrated poor M‘Cubbin. M‘Cub-
bin was a good man, an agriculturist of great
industry and wide experience, who had been
tempted into a wild-cat mining scheme, and was
left an almost penniless stranger in a strange land
within a week of arriving on the Barrier. But
M'Cubbin was a man of courage. He went north,
where land was available, with the intention of
taking up 1000 acres, and starting life afresh. He
selected on a curious upland where the soil,
though a trifle dry, seemed most promising, and
set to work. He toiled with remarkable assiduity,
put in a crop, and, to his great joy, saw it sprout-
ing in the course of a very short time. Conceive
M‘Cubbin’s frame of mind on awaking one
night to find his hut tumbling to pieces about his
ears, and his farm positively disintegrating.
M’Cubbin actually fell right through his thousand
acres of arable land. Striking a rock in his descent,
he lay unconscious for five hours. When he
recovered he was on a barren hillside, and
the whole stretch of scenery was foreign to him.
The poor man was simply heart-broken, gentle-
men, on learning that he had selected a
dust storm temporarily delayed in its descent on
Broken Hill, and had fenced and cultivated 80
acres of it. Excuse me, but your feeble experiences
of dust storms extorted this story from me.” And
then we all opened our mouths to speak, and shut
them again without breaking the silence.
“ Camp-Oven ” : “I struck the champion

a sovereign that he could not increase his load bv
an extra sleeper and still perform the feat. The
speculator lost his quid. To vary the proceedings,
two kerosene tins were brought out, filled almost

“Rapids” : Be account of the hermit-dog
(B. 12/3/’08). It reminds me of the method
adopted by a South Coast (N.S.W.) selector some
years ago. No well-defined boundary marked his
holding, but a surveyor, following the track
brushed by his assistant, had roughly marked its
limits. A belt of felled timber followed by a
running fire ; a blackened waste sprinkled with
seed and then clothed with soft green grass ; a
barn-shaped slab-house with bark roof—such were
the steps which marked the development of his
selection. The little grass plot which had to sup-
port a saddle-horse and a few poddies, the
foundation of his herd, was a great temptation to
the wallabies which swarmed in the scrub that
bounded two sides of the clearing. So the selec-
tor organised a canine army of sentinels. Tied
to trees about 50yds. apart, and whenever pos-
sible commanding a wallaby-track, these fellows
took up their duties at sundown, and they were
an indispensable garrison. Much barking and
tail-wagging greeted the selector when he came to
release them at early morning. Then they set off
into the scrub to run down something for break-
fast.
* * * «
“Macfcavish,” who is evi-
dently an imaginative Scot, remarked, in a recent
“ Abo.” par, on the weirdness of the fox’s cry.
The fox gives a short, sharp yelp when hunting,
and a snappy snarl when discussing family
matters ; but for eeriness neither note is a patch
on the dingo’s wail, or even the howl of the com-
mon hoiise dog in low spirits. Reynard often
sounds his hunting song under a tree where fowls
are roosting, and a storm of startled chooks
usually drop from their perches in sheer funk.
This proceeding argues thoughtfulness on the
fox’s part, but there are contradictions in his
character. One is a habit of stopping to locate an
unusual sound, which habit often gives a fox-
shooter an easy shot. Another equally foolish
trait is a weakness for a lair in a tree with only
one entrance to the hollow. Generally it is a
standing tree so inclined as to be easily climbed,
with a hole about 20ft. up, where a limb has been
broken off. The marks the fox makes passing up
and down advertise his domicile as plainly as a
signboard, and, when discovered, the animal has
no hope of escape unless the individual who
ascertains him is of small size and nothing very
great in the way of enterprise.
THE SPIRIT OF DEFENCE AT BILLY-GOAT FLAT.
“ J.S.W.” : On a Riverina station I went
for a drive last week with the boss. Two weary,
dejected mokes drew us across miles of grey
plain, as bare of grass as the cranium of Charles
the Bald, to one of the tanks. Here was camped
a curious specimen of humanity—short of legs,
long of arms, filthy beyond description—a casual
dwarf, whose duty it was to drag out sheep that
had become bogged in the tank. “Good day,”
said the boss. “Day, Boss.” “How many to-
day ?” “ Seventy-nine, Boss, so far. ” The heaps
of skins, the mournful, long-drawn-out “ Coor,
Coor” of the crows as they dropped or rose
heavily, the sickening smell and the barren waste,
and the stumpy, professional dragger-out were a
picture. Another small, dirty tent was half-
hidden in a bit of brush. “Is that part of the
dwarf’s camp?” I asked. “No; a rabbiter’s.
He went away to get married this week.” “Good
God! Is he coming back?” “Yes, he brings
h's bride with him to-morrow or the next day.”
I hen I fainted and remembered no more.

“Sr.”: “Your crowd’s a nice lot, aren’t
they ? Y’ father did a year. You orter talk about
my people ! Y’orter ! Why, yer own brother
took yer down fer four poun’s. You orter tork
about my people ! ” It was the same old com-
batants fighting for the same old household
championships, and using the same old arguments.
“ Yes,” she declared, right at the front gate, and
addressing the people who peeped behind the
window blinds. “Yes, why yer own son’s aloafer
and booser like yerself. Go round and see Mrs.
Brown ! Go on, yer drunkard !” And then she
started to weep. Frequent “shut ups” sounded
inside the house at intervals. Finally he came
out to the door with a bucket. “Ef yer don’t
shut up, yer big mad fool, I’ll bang this water over
you!” “You dare!” replied the big, stout
amazon to the little shrimp of a hubby who stood
with his bucket at the door. “You dare! Jist
do it! See what I’ll give you ! Go on ! Throw
it, y’coward!” “Damn me, I will!” shrieked
the wizened hubby, and with a mighty heave he
poured a Niagara over the big, fat, complaining
woman. She didn’t scream. She couldn’t speak
with the shock. All she did was to cough and
wipe her face. For two solid months they’ve
been as quiet as lambs. Nary brawl or fight.
“Joe Billycan”: I agree with “ Mun-
dadji ” (B. 26/3/’08) that man’s noblest friend
prefers to choose his own burying ground. I
remember at the beginning of one very severe
winter an old, casual neddy was continually
hanging round a camp that he had selected, in-
stead of being with his cobbers, as was his wont.
The old bloke noticed it, and opined that it
wouldn’t be a bad lurk if we brought him in and
gave him some corn, as he reckoned the old fellow
would come in handy in the spring, getting in the
crop. It will thus be noticed that the old man’s
sympathy was of the useful (to himself) kind. But
though the ancient equine would eat the corn, he
would immediately mooch back to the camp, with
a suspended head, and sleep there all day, wearing
an expression on his dial likethatof an old leg-weary
gladiator thinking of his departed glory. He
“threw his seven” a week later—from senile
decay, I think; anyhow, he was old enough to
have a vote. We buried him under the shade of
the old apple tree.
“The Rager ” : Anyone ever hear of
Muldoon? Muldoon, like Blackguard Brummy,
Black Andy, Bill the Miner, and Cooper’s Creek,
was a famous character and celebrity on the rivers.
He was the only man that was ever known to
drink right off a bottle of “carriers’ rum ”
or painkiller without winking. Em-
brocation and turpentine he swal-
lowed at one gulp—and asked
for more ! As for beer, well,
he would drink it out of a
bucket. He feared neither
gods, men, nor dogs—
only blue devils!
Snakes, cats, rats,
and spiders he did-
n’t mind—if they
were dead. It was
a common thing for
him to polish off a
loaf of bread and
two pounds of half-
cooked steak and
then rise up and
say, “ Thank God
for that snack ! ” No
mate could travel
with Muldoon long,
as it meant starva-
tion—for the mate.
The mate would do
the cadging, and
Muldoon would do
the eating. When
at Big Yanko, Mul-
doon stowed away
the largest meal of
which we have any
record. He got out-
side of 27 rock-cakes,
the same number of
chops, a bottle of
pickles, two loaves of
bread, a tin of mar-
malade and three
pounds of vegetables.
The shearers had to
hunt him in the end, as
he was running the mess
account up too high. If
he had remained there
much longer they would all
have been in debt. So they filled his ration-
bags and turned him adrift to look for another
fattening paddock. After he had taken his de-
parture, the cook discovered that be had stolen a
whole newly-killed sheep and all the yeast.
* * * *
Up in Papua Judge Murray and the
magistrates are struggling with the eternal dress
problem. They have long since come to the con-
clusion that the habit of wearing clothes is killing
their Papuans, yet they know that their Papuans
would rather be killed than be out of the white
man’s fashion. It started this way. The white
man can kill with great suddenness and mystery ;
therefore the white man lives up two flights in the
human kennel. But the white man wears clothes ;
therefore when the Papuan wants to put on dog
and climb up a storey, he must wear clothes too.
But as he can’t get enough clothes to go round,
Billu’s clothes to-day are Jimmu’s clothes to-
morrow ; and so the gorgeous shirt wanders through
the village and doesn’t get washed anywhere on the
voyage. Now, it is bad enough to wear clothes
to-day, and go protected only by native innocence
and a shark’s tooth to-morrow; but worse still is
the habit of passing the clothes around, seeing the
number of lung-diseased subjects that they come
in contact with. The consequent increase of pul-
monary trouble is something awful; in the past
two years some tribes have lost no less than 20
per cent, of their numbers—mostly from this
cause. At the start the Papuan magistrate tried
to discourage clothes ; but the missionary frowned
horribly, and, anyway, apart from the missionary,
the plan didn’t answer. Consequently, nowadays,
the Papuan magistrate reckons among his minor
duties the education of the natives in the rational
use of shirts and other unnecessary garments.
* * * *
“Naye-Baal”: The gecko turns up on
Kangaroo Hills, North Queensland, as the latest
Australian claimant to honors and notoriety, and
is assuming fearsome proportions in debate. The
affirmative party alleges that he (gecko) is a true
chameleon, and the opposition says he ain’t, and
that anybody who says he is is a plain ass with
long ears and a frill on his tail. The chameleon-
ites are a growing power, and the conservatives,
maddened by-desertions from their ranks, have
become bellicose. On the policy laid down by
The Bulletin’s pet chameleon I have taken a
decisive yes-no stand. We know the gecko in
many shades, but the schism comes in on this
question : Is the brown or dark yellow saurian of
the evening the identical bright green gecko of
the morning, and does he alter his hue to accom-
modate himself to his environment ? I am seeking
to have the matter settled by a Commission of
Bulletin Aboriginals, for the row is driving
capital out of the country.

“ Cict ” : He the multifarious names tacked
on to some of the N.S.W. towns. Was commis-
sioned recently to collect an account from
Adelong. Address supplied me was “L. Jones,
Adelong.” Struck Tumut, and inquired the
route. lnquiree asked if I wanted Adelong,
Adelong Crossing, Main Adelong, Middle Ade-
long, or Upper Adelong, and then sat back
calmly to observe the effect. Visited the lot ulti-
mately. Each included several houses and a
school; and most of them a pub, church and post-
office. They were easy to find, as they one and
all slept the peaceful sleep of stagnation on a
common creek—that of Adelong to wit. The
most elusive thing about them proved to be
Jones, who still evades me.
THE AFTER-DINNER APPLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Cole suggests the substitution of apples for tobacco,
inasmuch as “good apples are a delicious, health-giving
food, while tobacco is not a food, but a disease-giving,
offence-giving, and demoralising poison.” It will be
remembered that somewhat similar opinions in regard to
tobacco were held by the late John Alexander Bowie.—
Argus, mi'oB.
When Bill, the teamster, seeks the shade beside the billa-
bong,
To boil the billy and to pass the bully beef along
To Jim, off-siding ; when, beside the crib-fire, smoking
blue,
They pull their ancient cutties out, and fill ’em up anew,
Not here do loaded boughs bend down, with apples by
the score,
To scatter juicy joys on those who smoke big pipes no
more ;
There is no other pleasure
Than smoke to bless their leisure—
Last time they saw an apple was some eighteen months
before 1
When, rib and jowl bedraped in fat, the steady diner sits
And, platter after platter, gives his ten-course dinner tits ;
When as, the final nectar rolling kindly on his tongue,
He chaffs his pink companion, who is golden-tressed and
young—
When, fed and wined, he sprawls, and spreads his heels
upon the floor,
There’s room in his economy for smoke, and nothing
more !
He has no strength to grapple
In earnest with an apple—
“ Cigars !” Just hear his comfortable, super-fatted roar 1

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Davey Weir, the 96-year-old Dunmore
Lang emigrant who helped to open up North
Gippsland in 184 d, died recently. He bequeaths
three generations of sturdy descendants to the
country of his adoption.
Deceased British Prime Minister Campbell-
Bannerman had points. No game was preserved
on his estate at Belmont Castle. His old carriage
horses he declined to sell. When out of date he
sent them to the country, and pensioned them off
on green grass for the balance of their lives. His
wife, when dying, said, “He never gave me an
unkind word.”
Warden Harry Graves, of Sydney, stock
and station agent, who passed to the pastures of
Elysium last week, had been connected with
pastoral pursuits longer than most men now living.
His half-century’s experience served to teach
him as much as it was possible for a human being
to know in regard to the sheep as a money-spinner.
Deceased came of Limerick (Ireland) military
stock, and afforded a model of unbending Toryism
to all who ran across him.
SENATOR SYMON.
It has apparently been demonstrated to
the satisfaction of his accusers that Canon Nash,
of Geelong (Vic.), did nothing worth I mentioning
after all. The Argus congratulates the Canon on
the re-opening of a future for him. That is all
right in its way, but the Canon has lost a very
interesting past, and the aura of Byronic wicked-
ness which imaginative people wove about him
has burst. He was darkly suspected of being a
pirate. Now it proves that he is only a preacher.

Died, at Maryborough (Q.), on April 18,
aged 76, George Negus, who established railway
carriage works in that towm, and built the first
passenger cars made in thb Northern State.
A noticeable absentee from the Royal
Commission on Beautifying Sydney is J. D. Fitz-
Gerald. Why was he not included 2 No other
citizen has done as much as he to force to the
front the question of a Greater and a More Beauti-
ful Sydney.
William Higgs, Queensland’s democratic
ex-Senator, has got a Government billet in
Sydney as director of the Queensland Tourist
Bureau and Intelligence Department. His job is
fixed for two years, by which time the Senate
elections will be round again, and his State may
be weary of Sayers and Ohataway and Sledger.
The death of Harry Foran, at St. Vincent’s
Hospital, Melbourne, is announced. He was aged
45 years. County Clare, in Ireland, was his birth-
place. On arrival in Victoria, some 20 years ago,
he obtained an appointment in the Public In-
struction Department. Leaving the service he
moved on to Sydney, and began life as a loud and
unstable variety of politician, the Domain being
the chief scene of his eloquence. He contested
many Parliamentary seats, with no success. A
couple of years ago he returned to Melbourne,
where he agitated some more.
“Silas” : The other day Magistrate Meekin
of Melbournetold an old road makerof myaequaint-
ance, 66 years of age, gone in the back, sft. Bin.
high, and weighing about eight stone, that he had
years of hard work in him yet, and declined to
risk giving him the old-age pension without a
birth certificate. A week later I saw that Meekin.
a substantial man, portly, and in his prime, had
given notice of his resolve to retire on his pension.
And it is not a 10s. pension that is due to Mr.
Meekin mild, you can bet.
One of the best known men on Sydney’s
water front is master-mariner Joe Weston,
of Balmain, who has owned for vears the
queerest-looking lot of sea-going craft that
travel along the N.S.W. coast. The recent strike
spelt trouble to Joe Weston. When the North
Coast beer supplvran out, the Sydney brewers char-
tered his fleet of two to carry beer to the parched
districts. The Sophia Ann (flagship) had just suc-
cessfully landed a full cargo of XXX at the Tweed
River, and was thumping out over the bar on its
way for more, when it became disabled. In trying
to get back into the river it developed into a total
wreck, and a big loss to Weston. The local Sal-
vation Army held a rally the night after the
disaster, and publicly thanked the Almighty for
wreaking just vengeance on the beer-carrier. The
success of the meeting was marred, however, by
a carping sister, who remarked that she thought
Providence would have shown more sense by
wrecking Sophia Ann before the lady landed ail
that beer.

Since George gave up bossing the W.A.
Ministry as Commissioner for Railways, and
essayed cockying at Olive Hill, J.’ T. (or
“Jaunty”) Short has assumed acting command of
the Westralian lines. This gentleman has cut down
the staff, the expenses, the service, and every-
thing that an axe could get at, and what prevails
now is known as the Short (sighted) policy. Over-
time has to be cut out in enforced idleness, and
it seems likely that soon everything will be cut out
except the occasional accident.
“ Ifcan ” sends a memo. :
There were some things at the Sydney Easter Show
worth looking at. One exhibit was a collection of
farm products sent in by two Paddington boys, son of a
chimney sweep, who went on the land seven years ago so
ignorant of the business that they could not use a plough,
and almost too poor to buy one. They were then 11 and
13 years of age. With splendid pluck, these Prevost
Bros, tackled a 140-acre block of mountain land near Moss
Vale, and fought ignorance and irnpecuniosity until they
got both enemies under their feet. Then they began to
make headway. Now they offer a lesson to Australia, for
their collection at the Show would have done credit to a
State farm, where the management had an expert staff
and the run of the State Treasury. There seemed to be
no end to the variety of stuff produced on the small hill
farm, and it has a fair supply of live-stock as well. Also
the youths reckon they are only beginning, and hope,
after a few more years of bogging in, to do greater things.
Two more Federal Labor members are set
down for matrimony at an early date. The
Laborites who have entered the married state
since becoming members of the Australian Parlia-
ment include MeDougall, Fisher, Needham, Catts,
Russell, and Fraser. The roll of Deakinites,
Reidites, and corner men only tots up Keating,
Hume Cook, and Motor Kelly. Yet it is some of
these latter who spout about the Labor policy
being destructive to the home.
The profound kindness that Victoria heaps
on its helpless poor, viz., six months’ gaol, lately
fell like the gentle ton of coals from heaven on
ex-champion runner Albert E. Bird. Bird, who
is now 76 years old, had his share of good and ill
fortune before, in his old age, the usual Victorian
charge brought him once more into publicity.
The Court made severe remarks about the absence
of receiving homes for such cases, and this
moved Premier Bent (who is an ex-sprinter
himself, and was once a famous swimmer and
balloonist) to say indignantly that there
was no need to imprison a man because
of poverty, and to “express surprise” at
the magistrate’s ignorance of any refuge but gaol
for a penniless totterer. Well, if Victoria has
any other refuge, it is about time Bent told the
magistrates where it is, for Victoria’s helpless and
impecunious have been going to gaol for quite a
long time now. In Bird’s case Bent is doling out
a trifle from the “compassionate allowance”
which he controls, so Methuselah, the runner, will
dodge gaol after all.
The confused tale of this paper’s Oldest
Inhabitant:—
John Pascoe Fawkner, the founder of Melbourne, who
died in 1869, left but little of this world’s goods. He
made a will, however, of which Mr. G. B. Hailes, an old
colonist and old friend, was the executor. Mr. Hailes
died some years ago, but old Johnny’s will was before the
Court last week, after 40 years, when an order was made
to “ pay out” to certain beneficiaries. John P. Fawkner
left a widow, but no offspring. The widow married
a political barrister who had been a bookseller—
J. J. Walsh. Apropos of the founder of Melbourne.
It is not generally known that Fawkner’s party
was saved from slaughter by Derrimut, a native.
Mr. Fred Hailes, son of J. P. Fawkner’s executor, has
shown me an ancient photograph of Derrimut lying dead
in the Melbourne Hospital. The photo is by Davies, who
had a studio opposite the Theatre Royal. On the back of the
photo in the founder’s handwriting are the words : “ The
last of the chiefs of the Boonorong tribe, Derrimut, died
26th April, 1864. He preserved the lives of. the whole of
the first colonists when about to be massacred by up-
country tribes ; 300 were assembled together at‘Mel-
bourne in 1835. —John P. Fawkner.” Fawkner had six
copies of the photograph struck off, one of which he gave
Hailes, senr.

Bonyfchon, of the S. Aus. Advertiser
has lost his general manager, Robert Cooper wU
broke down while S’langdon was away in £
land last year, and died last week from a hope
less nervous disease. For 30 years Cooper had
been most of the skilled business brain of the
Advertiser ; Bonython was still plodding along
on the staff when he joined the paper, after an
English training as an accountant and an Aus-
tralian training in breeding jumbucks in the Port
Lincoln district. It was a pretty tough struggle
they had in their earlier days, so Cooper got into

would probably have lived a lot longer (he was6ll

Corpse item from “ F.C.” :
The Vic. Licenses Reduction Board’s recent innni™
into South Melbourne’s drinkeries concerned
Hotel, among others, and brought up memories of an olri
enme. In ’B4, J. B Slack, a wealthy miser who lived
alone in the house where the Star shines now though h
shone next door then, was found dead, his throat e, t
from ear to ear and a razor in his left hand Though U
was shown that Slack was not left-handed the hi*
Coroner Youl and a jury verdicted “Suicide” S«i
months later, William Barnes, then a compulsorv res
dent of Pentridge Stockade, babbled fatuously about
Slack s death and the interested authorities inquired
further. Slack s body was exhumed, and it was found
that the cut in his throat ran from right to left which
tended to show murder instead of suicide. Anvhow
Barnes was duly hanged in May, ’B5, and thus he realised
at length the folly of opening his mouth and letting the
wind blow his tongue about.
“ Minko ” : Anent Darling, the self-styled
m 7 low °. r late) y ™ Melbourne
(U. A/4/ 08). this fantastic person daily harangued
delighted crowds and distributed pamphlets for a
while in San Francisco streets, attired only in an
armless shirt, opened down the front, and a pair
of frayed trousers, the latter garments terminating
above the knees. The “nachur” man was un-
molested by the police until a few days prior to
the annual convention of Knights Templar,
when he and several other Ishmaelites were haled
before the beak on a large, general charge of
“ being characters calculated to undesirably affect
the visitors’ impression of the city of San Fran-
cisco.” Darling was discharged on entering into
a promise to remove himself and his wardrobe to
Honolulu forthwith. Darling was leading man
of his year at Stanford University in physiological
research, and an athlete of some note.

A WORD OF ADVICE TO A KING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Wednesday Evening.—The police at Lisbon
have discovered a plot to murder King Manuel at the
opening of the Cortes.
The Republican newspapers advise King Manuel to
remember the fate of his father and brother.— Reuter,
SU/ltl’oB.
When the bombs are hurtling round you, Man-u-el,
Making peaceful Lisbon seem a perfect Hell,
Produce your memo book ;
Open it, and have a look
At the fate which, erst, your relatives befell.
In the joys of regal life in Portugal,
’Mid the pleasing strife of clique and of cabal
You will probably forget
Your own forehead once was wet
With red gore spilled by a son of Belial.
Always brood on your progenitor’s sad end ;
It will buck you.up, and wonderfully tend
To make you spry and cheery
In the presence of the eerie
Anarchist, with cans of dynamite to vend.
Never put the hideously blood-stained knife,
The smoking gun, the crash and scream of strife
From your mind, my youthful king—
By which practice you will bring
To yourself the blithe and perfect Cheerful Life.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.
DULY QUALIFIED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In 1886 Sir Thomas Gibson-Carmichael married the
daughter of Captain Albert Nugent, E.N. He is in-
terested in natural history, and has written various
papers upon centipedes and spiders.—Argcs re new Vic.
Governor.
He was a quaint and shabby bloke,
And, while his air was dreary,
His atmosphere, you’ll grieve to hear,
Was singularly beery.
He came up through the dust and smoke
Of Melbourne on a loping moke.
An eye opaque and bleary
Shone in his old and faded head.
He wished to see the Gov., he said,
About a little tlie’ry.
Upon the nice vice-regal mat
Encamped the dusty rider.
He turned his sleeve, and made believe
To pluck a purple spider,
And then a pink one. As he sat
He counted fifty in his hat.
He pushed the portal wider.
“ Come out, you loafers ! ” thundered he.
“ Of insects for his Ex’llencee
I’m general provider.”
He tore his whisker out like weeds,
Which didn’t seem to thin id.
He stuffed it in a pannikin,
And said he with a grin : “ It
Is half a pint of centipedes.
I’ve more to meet vice-regal needs.
I’ll catch ’em in a minute.
For spiders, mister, how is that?"
The Gov’nor peered into the hat.
Said he : “ There’s nothing in it ! ”
The bushy rose and murmured “ Fie !|
I’m only Brown, from Biam.
I ain’t well-bred, I ain’t well-read,
And just now deadly dry am ;
But on this point I qualify.
’Bout centerpedes and spiders I
Particularly fly am. •
And let me say you’ll never be
Well up'in natural his'tbree
Till you’re as drunk as I am! ”
VIC.
