POLITICAL
POINTS
It looks as though China were making
ready to give back answers to Japan. According
to some accounts, the Chinese army is reaching a
high stage of efficiency, and the Chinese arsenals
are turning out guns equal in quality to those of
the best European works. When Australia
wanted some defence “ material,” the other day,
one engine firm offered to supply the goods from
China at a rate far below the English or Austra-
lian price. Of course the decision of Minister
Ewing was to manufacture in Australia ; but the
Chinese offer shows the progress that the Yellow
Man makes towards efficiency in warfare.
YEA-NAY.
An almost futile attempt to portray a notorious
political twister, as described by Deakin.—(Vide
daily papers.)
Britain’s so-called Freetrade Government
meets with one disaster after another. Winston
Churchill, going up for re-election after being
appointed a Minister, at Manchester, where
the original Freetraders came from, has lost his
seat. It is a rare thing for a Minister to be thus
thrown out, for any electorate feels a pride in
being represented by a member of the Cabinet,
and the conversion in two years of a majority of
12U into a minority of 429 is serious. Then at
Dewsbury, where the rags are sold, Churchill’s
colleague only got in by a majority of 1516— a
grievous falling away from his 3810 majority of
two years ago. And at best he is a minority
candidate who only crawled in on a split vote.
There is again talk of Premier Bent recon-
structing his Cabinet. He hasn’t yet passed every
member of the House through it, but he has gone
a long way on that road. Since he became
Premier, on Feb. 16, 1904, he has had three Lands
Ministers (Taverner, Murray, and Mackey), two
Agricultural ditto (Murray and Swinburne), two
Water Supply ditto (McLeod and Swinburne), five
Chief Secs. (Murray, Gillott, Mackey, Langdon,
and Peacock), and about as many honorary Minis-
ters as cared to take the billet. Now it is broadly
hinted that he wouldn’t lose much if he dropped
Sachse, Cameron, McLeod, and “honorary”
Pitt.
A queer story comes down from Papua to
the effect that a Japanese schooner lately sailed
into Samarai, and proceeded to land its crew
without first getting pratique. When the autho-
rities came down on the skipper, he told them
through an interpreter that he didn’t savee
pratique, didn’t know what a clearance was, and
hadn’t a scrap of paper on board to show what, his
vessel was, where she belonged to, who he was, or
what right he had to her. He said he was beche-
de-mer fishing, and had just run over from Thurs-
day Island, where he had been living for years.
There must be some queer, shambling goings-on in
Torres Straits if that Jap skipper’s account of him-
self was only half true —if, for instance, a brown
Asiatic can get possession of a schooner without a
scrap of writing to show for it, can leave the port
without a clearance or any formality, and go
wandering about beche-de-mer fishing, or making
believe to doit, and landing men unceremoniously
anywhere and anyhow.
Bigjohn Forrest lent his vast intellect to
the Bendigo Easter Fair, and in the course of an
oration expressed something like sorrow to hear
that Bendigo municipality was practically out of
debt. Then, according to a report of the pro-
ceedings, he scattered this wisdom :
They should float a large loan, so as to be able to carry
out extensive works that appear to be necessary. Many
people were averse to borrowing, but it was not the mere
fact of borrowing, it was the awful expenditure of
borrowed money that led people or municipal bodies into
trouble.
Think of the vast value of the information that it
doesn’t matter if you only borrow, so long as you
don’t spend. As a further subject for some of
these deep reflections, The Bulletin suggests that
Bigjohn should point out to some Rechabite lodge
that it doesn’t matter how much beer you buy, so
long as you don’t drink it. The Rechabite will
probably want to know what’s the use of buying
beer in that case, and Bigjohn will get interesting
when he proceeds to answer the question.
“ Galleryite ” writes :
The lately-closed session of the Queensland Parliament
was a remarkable one. It began on March 3, and during
its seven weeks’ existence twelve measures were passed,
besides Appropriation Bills and eleven Railway Bills.
The dozen measures were : the Land Surveyors Act,
Factories and Shops Amendment Act, Election Acts
Amendment Act, Old Age Pensions Act, Technical In-
struction Act, Wages Boards Act, Inspection of Boilers
and Machinery Act, Sugar Works Guarantee Act, Consti-
tution Amendment Act, Parliamentary Bills Referendum
Act, Land Acts Amendment Act, and Referendum (Bible
in State Schools) Act. The start was inauspicious. The
Government was unable to elect one of its supporters
either as Speaker or Chairman of Committees,
and had eventually to support the candidature of
a Philpite and a Laborite respectively for those posi-
tions. The Address-in-Reply was of an uncommon
character. It was practically a censure upon the Gover-
nor for his unconstitutional acts. These time-wasting
matters took up a fortnight, and then the House settled
down to work in dead earnest, and put up the above per-
formance, which must be an Australian record for legisla-
tion. Beginning with Tuesday, Wednesday, and Thurs-
day as sitting days, Friday was added, and subsequently
Monday swelled the list, and when, in the last week, the
House couldn’t sit on Friday (Good Friday), it sat on
Saturday, beginning at 10.30 a.m., and it didn’t knock off
till 1 o’clock on Sunday morning. It was a hard-working
session, and memorable for swift Democratic legislation.
“Mate”: Be Australia’s Navigation Bill
(B. 26/3/’08). Don’t see why the British Board of
Trade should “suggest alterations” in it. It’s
only going a step ahead of that Board in the
direction it prides itself most on taking— i.e., im-
proving the conditions of our sailors. The Board
enforces its regulations on all ships flying the
British flag when it can get hold of them. For
instance, some few years ago an Australian regis-
tered ship went to an English port. It was
British built, and not more than five or six years
old, but the regulations as to accommodation and
ventilation had changed since it was built.
It had to be brought up to date before
the Board would let it * leave port. Yet
it wasn’t trying to do a coasting trade,
and it was never likely to see England again.
Also its provisions were examined, and some of
them condemned. They were hauled ashore and
destroyed ; the skipper’s offer to take them to sea
and dump them was coldly declined. No doubt
the Australian shipowner considered this a griev-
ance, but he was not consulted. What we need
is a respectable navy to back up the “hot, firm
words of Deakin.” The British shipowner has
very strong objections to the American Naviga-
tion Law ; but the British Board of Trade doesn’t
dreatn of embodying them in “suggestions” to
Uncle Sam.
“ Solitaire,” from Maoriland :
The Church Gazette again: “No! Labor will not
further Christianity by preaching its doctrines from the

teaching and principles, then they are being preached as
it is, and if they are not the pulpit is no place for them.”
This reminds one of the old Mahometan and the Alexan-
drian Library. “ Are the books antagonistic to the true
faith ? Then they are pernicious. Destroy them ! Are
they in accord with the Koran ? If so, they are not
necessary. Destroy them ! ” And they were all destroyed.
The Church Gazette is beautifully solid on the idea that
it is teaching the whole gospel and the pure gospel; and
that its preachments can’t be improved upon in any way.
If Arbitration laws are Scriptural, then the Church is, and
always has been, strongly advocating them, even though
it might sound as if it were cursing them. And if the
Church doesn’t advocate them then that proves that they
are unscriptural. For though the Church, when you get
down to details, is only the Rev. Jones and the Rev.
Smith ; yet Jones and Smith are infallible, and they know
everything, and any proposal for the improvement or
enlightenment of Jones or Smith is superfluous, not to
say impertinent. In fact, you can always find out for
certain if a theory is true bv asking the inspired Jones,
and if you are bewildered by conflicting political creeds
the one sure remedy is to lay the matter before Smith.
Hollis, M.L.A., speaking of the recent
rush of railway accidents in N.S.W., complained
of the rigid, wooden and indiscriminating
methods of so-called “discipline.” As an in-
stance he quoted the case of a man who was fined
a whole day’s pay for staying at a watering
station one minute over the time allowed for
taking in water. The minute was occupied in the
adjustment or repair of a portion of the mechan-
ism of the engine, but next time that driver will
know better, and will let the thing rip. Discipline
must be maintained.
Joe Cook’s latest was an hysterical deliver-
ance when Bill Lyne proposed to agree to some of
the Senate’s tariff amendments which tended to
raise the duties passed formerly by the Reps. Joe’s
idea was that those duties were adopted by the Reps,
as an honorable compromise, and that Bill had no
right to agree to the Senate action in raising them.
Bill, in reply, asked Cook if he would stick to the
alleged “compromise” by voting against the
Senate’s proposed reductions of certain duties.
Cook couldn’t promise that he would, and Bill
just said “Well!” and Joe said “ Well!" and
Bill said “WELL !”
The uncouth Bent, of Victoria, announces
that “ the State Cabinet has decided to keep in
its own hands the superintendence of the arrange-
ments for welcoming and entertaining the
American fleet when it arrives.” It can only be
hoped that before that fleet arrives some one
will have taught Premier Bent some sense and
a little quantity of manners. The idea of
Bent’s waistcoat being the first thing the visitors
see on landing, and the vision of Thomas, with a
comic song about “Jack Jolt” in his mouth, and
a large cabbage in his right hand, asking the U.S.
admiral if the ’ot weather makes him sweat; and
the prospect of Bent and Deakin shoving each
other on the landing steps —it is too awful. This
country wants to give its visitors a good impres-
sion. It will exhibit Deakin with some pride, and
it reckons five of the State Premiers will pass in-
spection, but it wants Thomas Bent to go to
Birdsville or Hergott Springs till the event is
over.
“ Eiddal "on a great Victorian irrigation
work :
The Waranga-Mallee Channel will pass through some
queer country, and, unless the Water Supply experts
have made a tremendous allowance for soakage, the
whole scheme, as far as the Waranga Basin storage
supply' is concerned, is likely to prove an expensive
fiasco. The steam scoops are at work on a big contract
approaching the Campaspe, and toil away without the
previous interposition of the plough, the big dredge-like
affairs pulling up fill after fill of sand-drift. Stuff that is
so soft as that means soakage, unless the bed of the
channel is cemented at a cost that would have staggered
Cheops even. The experts seemingly think that a coat-
ing of silt, naturally applied over the bed of the channel,
which will be composed of stone chips, will prevent this
disastrous soakage. Time will tell. But one thing is cer-
tain : the hundred miles of channel way to the Coliban,
which measures a chain wide and about 7ft. deep (a
pretty good river, in fact) will just about deplete
the Basin first filling. A big dam 6ft. deep and
about 150yds. long and a chain wide —part of the channel
scooped out in advance for water storage for the use of
the contractor’s camp —was filled from the Campaspe
Trust’s channel. In less than six weeks the water had
nearly all soaked away, and that was on one of the
soundest bits of ground yet scooped. What will the per-
petual sponge-like soakage be in the sand-drift country?
A humorous feature of the business is the presence of
Yankee irrigation expert Meade, who is roaring up and
down the country telling of the advantages of irrigation.
The country doesn’t want to hear general whoops and
remarks about the wetness of water and such-like, nor
does it crave for loose eloquence and generalities. What
it desires is a plain, flat assurance that the channel won’t
leak.
Pleasing slab from N. F. Spielvogel’s
lately-published book, “A Gutnsucker on the
Tramp” (G. Robertson and Co.): —
I have had to defend Australia from most abominable
calumnies. Such questions as “Why would you not
allow the Six Hatters to land in Australia ?” “ Why did
you put the Six Hatters in prison?” “Why did you
refuse to allow Indians to save themselves from drown-
ing?” “What about the Petriana?” My denials were
met with copies of Australian papers, confirming my
opponent’s argument.
It will probably be 20 years before Australia
recovers the good name which the Argus and
other Tory liars stole away. In the Petriana
Myth days there was an idea that a large section
of Australia might be terrified into abandoning the
democratic party by flooding the English papers
with incredible falsehoods about the party’s doings,
by thus filling England with a hatred and loathing
of Australia which would necessarily find ex-
pression in the English press ; and then by widely
circulating here the English press expressions of
horror, disgust, and detestation, in order to show
Australian democrats what an unbiassed outer
world thought of their policy. But the Australian
democracy went right on its way, and Toryism
gained nothing but the unutterable disgrace that
naturally attaches to the kind of person who
would sell his mother at auction and spend the
proceeds in beer.
From “ 0.,” on a situation which is still
hanging fire: —
Would the Governor-General have sent for Lyne had
Deakin persisted in resigning on the Webster vote? The
question is useful only as indicating what might or might
not happen in a similar contingency. When the head" of
a Government retires, all the members of the Govern-
ment automatically disappear, and there is no longer a
Minister in existence. All are mere members of Parlia-
ment. On that basis, the Governor-General might
send for Lyne, or Reid, or Fisher, or any other
member who was likely to command an effective majority,
or who seemed to deserve a trial. But there is
another matter to consider. Inasmuch as a Cabinet is
constitutionally assumed to work as a unit, an adverse
vote which causes the Prime Minister to retire affects all
the members of the Government. If the cause of the
vote were purely personal, the retirement of the Prime
Minister might justify the summoning of a colleague to
take his place. But if the vote relate to policy or admini-
stration, sending for a colleague of the retired Prime
Minister is exactly the same as sending for the retired
Prime Minister himself. Of course, if the Governor-
General could be persuaded that Deakin didn’t retire on
account of an adverse vote, but through weariness or a
boil on the back of his neck, then the sending for
Lyne would be in perfect order. Failing that* it
seems that Webster should have been sent for as the man
who made the trouble ; or Fisher, as the leader of the
party to which Webster nominally belongs. Personally I
think if Deakin ultimately goes out it is the duty of the
Governor-General to send for Webster. The spectacle
of Webster forming a Ministry, and offering George Reid
the position of Postmaster-General, is a thing which
would enrich the world with mirth and make the desert
of dry politics blossom like the rose.

PICTURE OF A FEDERAL CRISIS, TAKEN
BY OUR OWN PHOTOGRAPHER ON
THE SPOT.
“Excelsior” writes from Queensland re
the Federal capital:
I believe “Indaba” in your last issue has struck the
right note. Australia is to be a great nation. Instead
of the present six States —three of unwieldy size
—it should, and will, he divided into many more. Already
each State is pushing its railways inland towards the
centre, and it is a logical conclusion that eventually these
lines will all meet somewhere on the North-and-South
transcontinental line, which must come before long. As
other States are formed from Geraldton, in the West, to
Tow'nsville, in the East, each State railway will converge
to pretty near the same point, and, granted that a place
can be found, say, in the Northern Territory (and I know
it can be found) with suitable climatic and other condi-
tions, that is where the future political capital of Aus-
tralia should be. I should like to see this question
thrashed out, and by people who really know something of
Central Australia, which is certainly not the desert we
were taught to believe it 50 or 60 years ago.
LINES ADDRESSED TO JOHNSON, M.H.R. FOR LANG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was never contemplated by the Premiers’ Conference
that the Federal capital should be anywhere in the
vicinity of Dalgety. . . . Victorian politicians say, bang
the agreement ! “ Why,” they ask, “ should New South
Wales have the capital at all?” . . . Twofold Bay is
scarcely belter than an open roadstead, exposed to the
full force of the heavy winds that blow and the heavy
seas that roll. . . . But, can anyone imagine any fairer
prospects for sites for a Federal city than those afforded
by the areas lying between George’s River and National
Park. The magnificent beach of Cronulla is included in
this area.—W. E. Johnson, brokenly, in Sydney Tele-
graph, »/4/’0S — and most other mornings.
Bear with me, Mister Johnson, for my brain
Is (through your fault) in a wild, hopeless whirl;
I scan your letters—o’er and o’er again—
About that Site, avid for Truth, the Pearl.
Tell me—who am confused — ivhen did the Con-
Ference of State Fright Premiers decide
To ban Dalgety ? Eagerly anon
I’ll publish what you now right deftly hide.
Disclose the wicked politicians that
Cry “Do not give Noo South a site at all” ;
Give names ! The villains should be levelled flat.
They are dishonest, and deserve to fall.
Last year Australia’s chosen site you viewed
(See Hansard) as a “ gem ” it were a “ vile
And dreadful crime to steal ” ; how you have slewed
Right round! You hold it “bleak,” "poor,” “not
worth while.”
The Bay of Twofold, which you raved o’er erst,
To-day for some dark cause you choose to damn.
The Port you once upheld is now Ma’s “worst”—
A place beneath purloinment by sane man.
Out of this coil one salient fact alway
Stands out; your protest-drum you do not bang
Anent Cronulla Beach —O Johnson ! say,
Is this because Cronulla is in Lang?
N.S.W.

A HEAP OF MISERY FROM
INDIGESTION.
Weak Stomachs need Tone and Strength.
Sydney Woman had a bad bout of Indigestion ;
Headaches ; Heartburn ; Drowsy and
Listless ; Stomach Strengthened by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.
Indigestion is an everyday complaint. Nine
people out of ten get it some time or other.
Meals taken hurriedly or at irregular intervals,
food eaten too fast, or other little causes, not
noticed at the time, bring on a heap of worry and
misery in indigestion. Sometimes it’s the promi-
nent symptom in people who are bloodless. The
stomach simply becomes too weak to do its work.
Headaches start, the sight of food sickens, and
what little is eaten doesn’t nourish, the heart
thumps, sleeplessness and a depressed feeling sets
in till life isn’t worth living. To cure indigestion,
strengthen the stomach. This is what Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills do. They have cured indi-
gestion in men and women when every other
means failed. They sharpen the appetite and
enable the one who takes them to get nourishment
from every bite of food taken. If you’ve got
indigestion look to your blood and be cured, as
Mrs. Pierce was.
“I have suffered tortures from indigestion for
years, practically from girlhood,” said Mrs. John
Pierce, 177 Pyrmont Street, Pvrmont, Sydney. “I
never had any appetite, and what I swallowed lay
on my chest like a cold stone. My shoulder blades
would ache and I’d nearly feel suffocated. The
pains lasted for some time after each meal. My
head ached till I was nearly crazed, the pain
being up both sides. Every little while there was
a buzzing in my ears and I’d get quite dizzy. I
had no strength. All day long I was drowsy and
listless. My tongue was always coated' and I
had always a vile taste in my mouth. Often I had
some very bad attacks of vomiting. The doctor
told me my indigestion was chronic, but I got no
benefit from his treatment. Nothing did me any
real good till I read about Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. I read about them in a little book, so I
thought I would give them a trial. After the
first box I was able to eat my meals without fear
of suffering afterwards. I began to sleep better
and to get a little stronger. Before I left off Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills I was fully restored to health ;
every trace of the indigestion had gone, and I
have never had it since. lam thankful to say
that I am now in very good health.”
You must insist on getting the same kind as
cured Mrs. Pierce. Sold by retailers and the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Australasia, Ltd., Syd-
ney, at 2s. 9d. a box, or six boxes 15s. 3d., post free.

MAY NUMBER NOW ON SALE
The Lone Hand
BEGINS its second year with the strongest number yet produced.
With the aid of the lessons of a year, the Editor claims now to
have gauged the feeling of the Australian public, and to be able
to supply them with the sort of Magazine they want.
That the sort of Magazine they want is the sort which the press
the world over, in its criticisms of " The Lone Hand " declares to be
about the best sort in the world, is a good compliment to Australian taste,
** The Lone Hand " for May starts off with a humorous cover by
“ Hop "; a characteristic bush picture (in three colors) by Fred Leist
follows; and Norman Lindsay helps to make the number memorable
with another of his Great Stories, dealing with Gautama's life.
THE CHIEF STORIES are by Randolph Bedford, Kathleen
Watson, M. M. Phillips, and C. A. Jeffries. Ambrose Pratt's great
serial, ** The Big Five," develops its interest, and tells of heavy
fighting in the Northern Territory.
THE ARTICLES keep up to the usual high standard of interest.
Acting as our Special Commissioner, Lieut.-Col. Antill went recently
to Australian Papua to investigate the conditions there, and gives this
month his conclusions. Beatrix Tracy continues her powerful articles
on Women in Industry with " The Chorus Girl," a bright and frank
description of theatrical life from the inside. Blamire Young writes
on Longstaff's newest picture. Louis Esson gives the first of his
impressions of Asia. Rene Clement (Paris) writes graphically of the
history and development of the motor car. " Byrock " has a topical
article on "The Great Rider," comparing our Australian buck jump
riders with great British masters of horsemanship.
And there is half a score of other great features.
Yes, May will be a memorable number.
Buy it; decide that you must be a steady reader, and send a
year's subscription direct to the office or to your nearest newsagent.
The |one Hand
214 George Street,
SYDNEY.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydnky, April 28, 1908.
My Dkar Artnt, —
Most of the people who ought to be bitten to
death by wild lambs have gone back to their
homes in the Back of the Beyond. The Agricul-
tural Show brought them to Sydney. The closing
of the same has sent them back. They bumped
into one like Tait’s trains, owing to a habit they
have of walking in one direction whilst looking
in another. The be-Rovalled show was a great
success, both as regards fatstock and gate receipts,
and the riding of Mrs. Stace and other skirted
ceutaurs in the jumping contests will hang in one’s
memory gallery until some other riders and ridden
do the rocket-oum-grasshopper act to greater
advantage, if such a thing be possible.
At the St. Vincent’s Ball, Mrs. John Hughes
set, “ Coal,” was decidedly first favorite. It was
entirely of girls in black sequined frocks over silk,
long black gloves, and tiaras of jet, illumined
with tiny electric bulbs. They carried miners’
safety lamps as they emerged from a representa-
tion of the inside workings of a coal mine.
Next was the “Butter set,” headed byMesdames
Toohey and Kenna in frocks of buttercupsilk (short,
and turned up to show buttercup and white striped
petticoats), white fichus, bunches of clover on
their chests, the same flowers in their white mob
caps, and carrying little pats of butter on platters.
In between the acts they retired to a dairy which
was set up on the right-hand side of the dais.
The best-looking girls composed the “ Oranges
and Lemons” set, for the fruit industry, and,
appropriately, they wore large hoop earrings
A la Dago. They danced very little, but were
content to look nice on their stall, standing in
tiers, with the “Bells of St. Clement’s” dangling
in front.
About half-time the vice-regal procession pro-
cessed round the hall to view the sets on their
native heaths, and Lady Northcote said nice
things to everyone concerned.
The majority of the new frocks were built over
silver and gold tissue, and the head-dresses were in
the same line. One decoration was the body of a
bird of paradise with its whole tail dangling over
the back of the wearer’s hair. The galleries and
ballroom were crowded, which makes for financial
success, and the honorary secs. (Mrs. Barlow, Mrs.
Watkins, and Miss Glasheen) could not desire
anything better.
Her Bx. Northcote, at her ball on Tuesday
night, might have been representing the effect of
sunset on polar ice. She glimmered in white
satin, blazed with diamonds, emeralds and silver-
tissue, and glowed softly yellowish as to under-
skirt. The new ladv-o’-war Poore looked as light-
hearted as a sea-gull. Also she was as white as
one—hair, face and frock. The youngest Madden
girl, and the youngest Manning damsel (Trixie),
and a Currie Elies charmer were debutantes.
Premier Wade’s consort is making a toast-and-
rose effect with a glad gown she is wearing at
evening happenings and circumstances. Report
says it is silver tissue over rose glace, but brown,
rather than silver, seemed the transparent en-
closure.
Was it Wilde or Shaw who wrote that too few
clothes and too much color always showed that a
woman was getting desperate ? He—whichever
he it was —meant the drop of nitric acid for
unmarried women. But at certain balls
that rolled tins week, married women, and
grandmothers at that, gave fearsome bone
and suet and brisket expositions. At the
St. Vincent’s those in the gallery got —well,
it was a shame to take so much for so little money.
The older some women get the more spinal but-
tons or hump-fat they seem desirous of exhibiting.
One well-known dame displayed as much of her
parchment back and front as she dared, and com-
promised with transparencies down to her waist.
Another showed a back view that more than any-
thing resembled a side of beef from one of the
prize stock at the recent show. As if anyone—
man or woman —wanted to gloat upon unlimited
ancient fat, or an indefinite quantity of middle-
aged bones. Still if they gave these exhibitions
in the sacred cause of Charity, they meant, well.
Charity uncovereth a multitude of shins, as some
wag remarked.
Thirty thousand persons—an official estimate —
witnessed the Sydney Cup, at Rannwick, Easter
Monday. Both vice-regal boxes were filled with
two sets of Excellencies and suites and guests,
shaded black, violet, brown and grey. The color
schemes of this year’s fashions are like Swinburne
poems.
The smart debutantes of this Resurrection time
may be said to be emancipated, let alone out.
They chipped the shell of the conventional “ in ”
at the Matrons’ ball on Monday night. On
Tuesday night they came further out into
the electric light at the Government House
Ball. On Wednesday several of them came
out some more at St. Vincent’s ball. On Thursday
night a clutch of the bantlings were at the
E. W. Knoxes’ dance given for the debut of Miss
Janet Knox, and on Friday night the whole
brood was at the Union Club ball. Truly, they
have emerged, and their outness is complete.
A new social feature of the just-finished race
festivities was the Brobdingnagian theatre party.
The movement began with a comparatively paltry
affair for 30 persons, donated by a dame who
already had a pile of generous entertainments to
her credit. The idea caught on. It reached its
climax on Saturday night, when a host and
hostess turned blandly up at Her Majesty’s with a
three-figure tally of guests in tow. If this sort of
thing is going to develop into a fashion, J.C. W. will
have to take serious thought. His most constant
Saturday night patrons are the simple leg-and-
sing-song-loving children of Israel, who live sur-
rounded by hard cash, cold fried fish, and
diamonds in Maeleay-street, Potts Point, and yet
remain withal Peris in regard to the Society front
gate. These will assuredly be sorely annoyed,
if they find—as was the case last week—every
decent seat pre-empted on the evening whereon
they have for years been accustomed to go forth
and glitter publicly, and be altogether happy.
Already there is heated talk in Bethlehem, and
round about Jabesh, and Hazor, and Ramoth-
Gilead, and the talk is mostly of the Society
matron, who wants to buy her theatre-seats in the
bulk, being charged extra for taking a quantity,
instead of less, as is the commercial wont.
Hobart’s “ season ” just about finished when the
Pah’f’l led the fleet out of the beautiful Derwent
and on back to Sydney fortheEaster worship of the
horse. After that the Tasmanian maiden shut
herself within doors to re-cultivate the peach-
bloom that tradition insists upon her struggling
after. By way of waving a good-bye, the snow
gathered on Mount Wellington and tossed out white
arms that stretched right down to “the Springs.”
A recent back-comer tells me that the Hobart
tripper of a few years back would be liable to be
bushed to-day if it were not for the very much
alive Tourist Association. No longer is the Fairy
Bower the stopping-place for everything that
isn’t a human antelope; drags run up to
the Springs, and a good track leads on to
the pinnacle of Mount Wellington ; and you don’t
have to go even that far to get the
only Commonwealth view of winding water
that seriously threatens to knock our own bit of
Harbor off tiie top perch. Tasmania still dodders
along many of its old ways, but it has learned
quite a heap of things about tourist traffic and
accommodation.
But Adelaide, which in so many things resem-
bles Tasmania (without Tasmania’s snows), has
learned nothing. If the ocean-boater drops off
there to see how pious the city really is, he risks
a desperately dull wander unless he has friends to
take him in hand. Mostly, however, he doesn’t
drop off unless the weather is something extra in
blue-and-gold, because as yet only one of the four
big ocean mail-carriers uses the Outer Harbor.
The boats of the other three just haul up some-
where out in St. Vincent’s Guif, and you go shore-
wards in a bobbing launch. Sometimes, if it’s very
rough, and you must land, you get dropped on to the
bobbing launch in a “ bosun’s chair ” or a basket.
It rests with the gods whereabouts in the launch
you drop. Arthur Searcy, President of S. Aus-
tralia’s Marine Board, was once being landed in a
bosun’s chair from the old R.M.S. Rosetta, and
by an unfortunate frolic of the gods he was dropped
down the funnel of the launch. He was a great
advocate for Harbor improvements after that.
Yet now that S. Aus. has got them, most of the
ocean liners won’t use them —all owing to some
base money considerations, I’m told.
The Captain Walter Lumsden, whose cruiser
was rammed by the American liner St. Paul,
on Saturday afternoon last, is well known to
Australians. He married Melbourne’s Miss Fitz-
gerald a while back, and took her away
to the Scottish castle he is understood to own.
Why a man with a charming wife and a castle
wants to go trapesing about the high seas and run-
ning the risk of violent colds, sudden death, and
sea-sickness is an unexplained and unexplainable
mystery to the female mind. The captain—as is
the invariable convention in these circumstances
—was the last to leave his vessel; and even
then he was loth to abandon her entirely.
The cable shows that when she overturned he
adhered to her keel with a pathetically earnest
adhesiveness, and there abode till removed by the
crew of the St. Paul. It is a lesson to all woman-
kind on the sort of thing that happens to the
husband who goes down to the sea in cruisers.
Don’t pray now for a goose that lays a golden
egg—just supplicate for a chook that will lay a
regular supply of the common, yolky sort. Down
in smelly Sussex-street last week that sort was
bringing 2s. 4d. a dozen wholesale, and there were
hardly enough to make two puddings in all the
market. A produce man, whom I implored to
give an explanation of the disaster, said that it
was partly owing to S’Africa and partly to the
high price of wheat and stuff. A horrible sus-
picion floated in upon me that the warrior in
S’Africa had got so confirmed in the habit of com-
mandeering poultry and wringing their necks that
when he returned he went on doing it. But what
the produce man really meant was that a while
ago N. S. Wales foolishly sent away tens of thou-
sands of frozen fowl, and then fowl food got so
dear that it wasn’t worth while incubating any
more fowl. So Sussex-street sells eggs at 2s. 4d.,
and when you want one with your fried bacon the
hotel manager pushes his head in the doorway and
frowns horribly.
Miss Madge Mclntosh and Miss Gunn, of the
Meynell and Gunn (etc.) Co., have got themselves
a furnished house at Milson’s Point, with a tennis
court, and a garden, and a cat, and a vine over a
porch, and a blessed damozel of their very own to
ask “ Wot’s for the butcher to-day, mum ? ” Assets
like these are not to be had in the closer settlement
area of grand hotels.
Mrs. Geddes-Crawford (widow of a Sydney
journalist), who is interested in immigration and
other patriotic subjects, will give a lecture in St.
James’ Hall on Thursday evening next. She will
give her impressions of travel in the British Isles,
America, Italy, Argentina, and Maoriland, besides
recounting her experience of two years on the
land, and several months’ acquaintance of the
billets of housemaid and nursemaid in Australia.
It sounds an attractive mosaic, and, if successful,
will be the first of a series. Then Mrs. Crawford
will go to India to further pursue the matter under
discussion.
For the Easter camp this year wet weather
mostly ruled, and this kept visitors away to a large
extent. Yet Tommy’s girl didn’t seem to have
her ardor very much diminished, and she spooned
with Tommy during his hours off, on the cold,
dank flats around Liverpool, in about the usual
numbers. The artillery deserted their usual camp
at National Park and began training at Heathcote,
trekking afterwards to Liverpool, where they were
brigaded with the Infantry and the Light Horse,
and participated in some highly theatrical show
manoeuvres. These were lost to visitors, owing
to the fact that they were held many miles from
everywhere in a wild gully between Liverpool and
the coast. Woman’s gentle influence touched- the
camp of Mars, however, on Thursday night, when
the regimental messes had for visitors at dinner
the nurses of the Liverpool hospital.
Wonder why our women folk don’t take to
camping-out as a holiday attraction now and
again. In Victoria mixed parties under canvass —
with, of course, proper chaperonage —are fairly
usual; and the tent-dwelling idea has so much
caught on that a great canvas hotel is one of the
summer attractions at the Lakes Entrance. In
N. S. Wales, where there are 1001 beauty spots
that can only be well exploited by tent-dwellers,
girls seem to leave the delights of camp life to
their brothers. Yet there could be no more
healthful, delightful and comfortable way of put-
ting in a week than under canvas. Seeing the
soldiers’ tents helped to convince me of this.
The tent, properly pitched and drained and pro-
vided with good equipment, has all the comforts
of a house and few of its worries.
Young George Castles, brother of the wonder-
ful three Castles sisters, joined the “Lady Madcap”
Co. in Hobart, and the tenor voice he lifted
proved the laddie worthy of his relationship to the
three lassies.
Miss Donnelly, a sister of Mrs. Agent-General
Coghlan, was married last week at Waverley to
Mr. Seafortli Mackenzie. A beautiful name is
Seaforth Mackenzie, and suggestive of the Scotch
Highlands, and the kilts, and things like that.
A Sydney presswoman, part of whose daily
grind consists of compiling lists beginning
“ Amongst those present,” lately started to leave
out the name of a married lady who is making
herself conspicuous with a Tertium Quid.
The lady sent T. Quid to the office to protest
against the slight offered to “the best-dressed
woman at the races.” The curious thing is that
he went and protested as directed.
Mr. Gresley Lukin, editor of the Wellington
(M.L.) Post, is in Sydney on a holiday, staying
with a married daughter, Mrs. Saclier, at North
Sydney. Bishop and Mrs. Julius, also of the Un-
stable Isles, will be here this week.
There was no Captain Leslie Wilson, A.D.C.,
at last week’s festivities. His father —who was
the Colonel the son is one day going to be—died
recently. A big section of Sydney will extend
its personal sympathy to this young man, who is
the kindliest, as he is the most talented and tact-
ful, of his type yet seen in these parts.
A persistently-rumored engagement is that of
Miss Nora Dangar, one of Sydney’s prettiest
girls, and daughter of H. C. Dangar, M.L.C.,
to a relative in a far land. The maiden
left aboard the Mongolia this week on a visit to
the Abbey Dangars in India.
Another pair of engagements : Miss B. Bab-
bington, of Parramatta, of one of the First
Quality Families, to a Mr. Bobertson, of Hay;
and Miss Gwen Tooth to a son of the late Dr.
Bundle.
The latest Australienne to success considerably
in the Cold Country is the little Manly soprano,
Miss Hilda Mulligan, the sister of solicitor Mr.
“ Jack ” Mulligan. She was one of the lucky
ones whom Melba told to acquire a knowledge of
French and Italian, and then get away to Europe
and just keep on singing. If she sang long
enough her voice would do the rest. So Miss
Mulligan stole away to Milan and to Paris, where
she learned the languages in between singing.
Then she hied her to London, where she sang
before Moody Manners, who promptly engaged her
for his grand opera co., and, after sundry re-
hearsals, promoted her to be leading soprano. Last
month she appeared in Scotland with the co., and
wild-eyed men in kilts threw away the whuskey and
the haggis and drew the claymore to do her honor.
The performances were frightfully delayed owing
to the Scotsmen threatening to turn on the bag-
pipes unless Miss Mulligan sang her numbers on an
average of three times each. At Perth, where
she appeared in “La Traviata” and “La
Boheme,” she drove the press to “fine writing,”
and when an Australian girl can drive the Scotch
press to fine writing it is proof positive that she is
something out of the common. After the tour of
North Britain the co. will shift to England, and
if the little Manly girl can create the same im-
pression in the big provincial cities she is a made
woman.
Maoriland visiting bowlers will go back to the
Dominion with a freshened knowledge of
“ancient” Australian history. They and the
Victorian bowlers, and ladies accompanying
them, went by tram and steamer to Kurnell on
Friday, and listened to a historical speech from
Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan. Kurnell is that bit of the
Cosmos on which Captain Cook planted the piece
of English bunting that practically wrapped Aus-
tralia up as Bull’s property. The picnic, which
started at ten in the morning, and was given by
Mr. W. Macleod, President of the N.5.W.8.A.,
was attended by over 250 visitors, who absorbed
with equal enthusiasm food and historical narra-
tive. At La Perouse, en route, the party saw the
monument, watched the boomerang-throwing, and
inspected the cable-station ; and the aborigines
showed their shell-work. At lunch, the President
of the Victorian team, Mr. Charles Wood, and he
of Maoriland, Mr. Waddel, responded cheerfully
to the N.S.W. President’s humorous wailings ac
the lickings Captain Cookland had got at bowls
from the Maorilanders. “ Knowledge by suffering
entereth,” says the poet; but the picnickers, full
of nourishment, tradition, and information about
bowls, and cables, and Cook, and shell-work,
wouldn’t read it the poet’s way.
Yet another understanding between marine and
pastoral products has been arrived at locally, for
“the marriage will shortly take place ” of Miss
Eglantine Campbell, daughter of Hon. “Willie”
Campbell, and one Dudley North, 8.N., of
H.M.S. Powerful. The prospective groom is
a lieutenant, and has a colonel for a father;
against which distinction the lady sets off a
maternal grandparent, who, in his golden
middle age, was a C.B. and a K.C.M.G. ; and a
great-grandsire (Sir Bichard Bourke), who, in the
intervals of being a British general, misgoverned
N.S.W. somewhat.
Miss Mary Bobinson, who has been pianist to
Sydney Philharmonic these many years, and has
otherwise done much to earn the gratitude of the
musical, is to have a benefit concert in the Cen-
tenary Hall on May 6. The musicians will help
with a good programme ; the public should help
with a good audience.
West’s Pictures move from the Glaciarium into
the Town Hall to-night (Monday).
The Kapurthala rajah, who married Delgado,
a famous Spanish dancer or something, last month,
has been rushed like a spring bargain sale by
London Society mammas on each successive visit he
paid to the City of Smoke. This potentate is about
35, looks 50, weighs anything from 15 stone up.
wards, and owns enough diamonds to tip up a
coastal steamer. He is no snide plutocrat like his
fellow-countryman, Dhuleep Singh. Thatis known
beyond question. He rules Kapurthala, and
wrests therefrom a massive income. It is a
particularly bitter blow in the circumstances that
he should now be numbered among the Lost
Niggers of Debrett.
“ Mario ” writes from Brisbane :—
The air is dark with threatening of balls. The General
Hospital animal will hop upon the sloping boards of the
Exhibition concert hall on Thursday night, when the full
weight of the vice-regal foot will be applied at 8.30 pre-
cisely. On May 4 the Commercial Rowing Club will come
along, and this year this well-manned function lias been
selected as the principal opportunity for flappers, who
will burst forth in the usual chifiony cloud in great
numbers.
On Thursday afternoon there was the usual struggle of
the be-f rocked sex round the tables of the Queensland Hor-
ticultural Society’s annual show of chrysanthemums and
dahlias. Mrs. Cowley, wife of the ex-Speaker, herself a
successful gardener, said the usual nice things from the
platform, and said them very prettily, so that she reallv
deserved her bouquet, tied with the colors of St. George
of which royal and honorable society A. Cowley is top
dog.
No vice-regal light was shed upon the proceedings,
as her Ladyship is too busy these times shaking up into
unwilling action the weary Mothers’ Unions, where a
lot of distrait and ill-gowned house-fraus congregate to
drink tea, and listen to such words of wisdom as fall
from the mouth of the determined president, or are
poured forth by some fluttered curate. One day last
week, the Government House horses got nerves ori their
way home from an entertainment of this sort, and gal-
loped over a small Italian “ Fish-O” on the banks of the
river road. The damage done was trifling.
The Children’s Hospital, that wonderful place that some
day is to have a proper sanitary system, an infants’ ward,
and a diphtheria ward that is not of rotten wood and
swarming with microbes, has been getting itself into
high disfavor again this week. On all sides come com-
plaints of distracted parents, who allege neglect of their
infants—in one case leading to a diseased bone, and in
another to a loss of 24 hours in treating a serious
case, which delay, it is said, almost cost the little
patient’s life. But the hospital is thick-skinned and
accustomed to complaints, and if there is any right way
of setting the machine of investigation and reform in
motion, no one seems to have discovered it.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

The Royal Bank of Australia Limited
Head Office : MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
FORTIETH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 39th April , 1908.
THE Directors have pleasure in placing- before Shareholders the annexed balance sheet and statement of accounts
for the half-year ending 31st March, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £4941 6s. 9d. brought from last half-year) amounted to .. £19,957 12 5
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz. :—
To pay a dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum
To transfer to the reserve fund (making it £37,500)
To defray the cost of alterations to the Melbourne premises..
and to carry forward the balance .. .,
During the past half-year the directors have succeeded in obtaining a share of the State Government banking
account on the same terms as the other banks, and this, in conjunction with the general expansion of the business,
has necessitated somewhat extensive alterations in the head office premises, the cost of which is provided for above.
The Melbou rne business continues to grow on healthy lines, and the two branches are satisfactory contributors
to the profits of the hank.
The Directors acknowledge with pleasure the good work done by Shareholders in the past in bringing business to
the bank, and they hope for a continuance of the same friendly assistance in the future.
. Bv Order of the Hoard. H. T. WILSON, Manager.

I, SELBY PAXTON, General Manager of the Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited, do solemnly and sincerely
declare—
That the reserve fund amounts to £120,000, and the accumulated profits to £19,063 14s. 7d., and are used in the
business.
That the accompanying statement and balance sheet of the bank is, to the best of my knowledge and belief, true
in every particular.
The names, addresses, and occupations of the persons who are directors of the company at the date hereof are :
The Hon.. Thomas Skene, of Melbourne, land owner ; Thomas Russell, of Malvern Road, Armadale, gentleman ; John
Johnston Smart, of Orrong Road, Toorak, gentleman ; James Moloney, of Collins Street, Melbourne, barrister and
solicitor ; Bowes Kelly, of Melbourne, gentleman.
And I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions
of an Act of the Parliament of Victoria, rendering persons making a false declaration punishable for wilful and
corrupt perjury.
Declared at Melbourne, in the State of Victoria, this 15th day of April, one thousand nine hundred and eight.
Before me, ROBERT S. INGLIS, J.P. SELBY PAXTON.
Melbourne, 14th April, 1908.
To the Shareholders of the Colonial Bank of Australasia, Limited.
Ladies and Gentlemen,—We beg to report that we have audited the accounts of the Colonial Bank of Australasia
Limited for the half-year ended 31st March, 1908, the assets and liabilities of the branches being taken on the certiti
cates of the managers and accountants, and that the accompanying balance sheet is, in our opinion, correct. We
have also to report that during such audit we have not observed or become acquainted with any breach of the “Com-
panies Acts ’’ committed by the company, or any director, manager, employee, auditor or shareholder thereof, and
that, so far as we are in a position to form an opinion, the balance sheet and accounts have been drawn up in accord-
ance with the provisions of the “ Companies Acts,” and present a correct view of the state of the company’s affairs,
and that all our requisitions in regard to the shareholders, as well as the private balance sheet, have been duly com-
plied with. We are, ladies and gentlemen, yours fathfullv,
JOHN BISHOP, F.O.P.A.)

correct view of the state of the company's affairs.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melboubne, Apbil 27, 1908.
My Deab Yabbik,—
Elsie Moore, the plump beauty who once was a
caryatid in the chorus of J.C. W.’s opera co., was
married to her millionaire youth in N’York last
week. She changed her state with pomp on
Tuesday—so she cabled to her chums, the pretty
Butler sisters. And those ladies distributed the
glad news forthwith. The bewitching Elsie was—
amongst other things—a sister of the late Lady
Pratt-Winter, and quite successful on the
American stage —in certain r6les which demanded
a pretty face and an appetising figure. Her
husband'—whose name escapes me for the moment
-—has sufficient dollars to make half-a-mil!ion
sovereigns or more ; also he has a pair of heavily-
gilded progenitors, who think their new daughter
the nicest ever. Apropos to Mrs. Elsie, a wise
man said that, to be a theatrical success in the
U.S.A., she had all desirable qualifications—a
femininely-fair front, and strong masculine back-
ing. (Which are difficult to obtain simultaneously,
by the way.)
Another comic opera girl, lucky enough. to
possess those same potent weapons against
failure, was Ida Barnard. She’s making good
use of them. She is appearing in London in a
tolerable part and a gorgeous frock. Also she
makes her appearance from time to time at the
smartest Anglo-Australian functions that occur.
The female traveller ’twixt Melbourne and
Sydney is beginning to get her dues from the
inter-State railway authorities, whereat she is
gushfully grateful. No more is she thrust into
six square feet of stuffiness, with three more of
herself, and left to swelter there the night long,
from about nine o’clock, just because a com-
mercial traveller in the main car wants to go to
bed early. Now she’s got all to herself the
largest and most comfortable compartment
she can want (even if she numbers only four).
The compartment has chairs and tables, and even
ash-trays for her contentment, and is ever so much
nicer than anything provided for mere men. This
departmental detail has an essential significance.
It’s yet another proof that the era of Woman is
close upon us. Now, both men and women write
for her almost exclusively (surely no men
find pleasure in the unending succession
of stories of passion, padded with baby
Tailors strive to cut skirts rather
than "rousers. Artists paint portraits of
feminine prettiness, rather than of masculine
genius. Political newspapers give Mrs. Woman
a couple of pages of cookery recipes to herself.
And, at latest, insensible creatures like railway
commissioners exert themselves for her comfort.
Truly, if the importance of being feminine is
recognised and appreciated at this rate, in a de-
cade or two it’ll be worth while being womanly.
Now, I am told quite definitely that there is not
an atom of truth in the various yarns current
about Alfred De'akin and the Syme estate. The
Prime Minister does not benefit by a single
penny from the will; he is not to be in any way
associated with the future management of the
Age. The fact that Syme and Deakin were for
many years close personal friends, and the
thought that it would be a very good use
for a dying millionaire to put some of his money
to —that of recouping part of the financial loss
that the honest politician has to suffer for his
patriotism—probably inspired the yarns freely
current in Melbourne as to Deakin’s good luck.
But they have no basis of truth, which is a pity.
This year, when a murmured idea floated about
that Governor Talbot (who is also a soldier)
might like to join the Easter encampment, the
authorities demurred, and politely but fussily
hustled the idea out of perspective. It wouldn’t
be comfortable enough for His Tallness—better to
let him come when the band played “God Save.”
But Talbot and an aide descended quietly on a
neighboring hotel, to stay unofficially through the
Easter fray. The C.-O. of the artillery dis-
covered him, and invited him to mess at his camp.
Talbot sat on a camp stool and yarned about his
49 years of campaigning, and proved himself such
an out-and-out good fellow that the camp embraced
him. His kit was hauled over from the hotel,
and Talbot took up his quarters in the C.-O.’s tent
for the whole Langwarrin camp.
One of the many bungles after the ghastly Sun-
shine accident: The forepart of the Ballarat
train was uninjured. It was detached and sent
off with its passengers for Melbourne. People,
live and strong and kicking, hurried through to
the terminus, while the injured bled their lives out
among the wreckage. No one thought of turning
the well people out to help, and packing the
injured in the waiting train. Melbourne Hospital
had ambulances waiting at the station long before
the injured arrived. Whenatrain full of quite whole
excursioniststurned up, the waiting doctors danced
with fury. A relief train was despatched with
medical aid, and took two hours to reach the
scene. The next train from the wreck, carrying
injured and uninjured and the Ghastly Dead, was
hauled up at North Melbourne with a demand for
tickets. A callous guard shouting “Tickets
please ” to a train full of over-wrought people
who had spent the last few hours in Hell !
Even after the arrival in Melbourne, suburban
trains were held up for over two hours. After an
experience of six hours’ horror, three poor un-
escorted girls arrived at St. Kilda suburban
station in the grey chill of five o’clock early
morning. A porter demanded tickets. They
were not producible, having been dropped on the
field of the accident. The relentless official sent the
hysterical girls back to the ticket-office to get
fresh ones.
Mrs. Charlie Wallace and her sister, Mrs.
Parker, sailed for London on Tuesday. The
divorced beauty departed the scene of her strife,
looking very charming—brighter and plumper
than she has been since a year or so after her
marriage. She may go on the stage if oppor-
tunity offers, or eise go into one of the great
dressmaking establishments, and there learn to
put her artistic ideas into high-priced gar-
ments. Mrs. Parker will endeavor to sell some
songlets she has strung together.
The Mongolia deprived this State of the Butt
couple on Tuesday, and also deported Dr. Charlie
Ryan, who goes to Bullopolis to join his clever
wife and see his successful son. Surgeon Tom
Fitzgerald and his Nightingale of a daughter go
round as far as Fremantle by the same vessel.
The Butts, of course, were inundated with gush
and more tangible souvenirs, all of which they
graciously accepted. It might be better, perhaps,
if the enthusiastic mob which dances attendance
upon immigrant artists was more attentive to the
best of our local folk. But, obviously, a famous
name is a more valuable asset in Australia than
fine art.
The Marshall-Hall-Pinschof coterie talks of
building a great concert hall on Prince’s Court,
and popularising good music. It’s a negligible
plan. We’ve yet got to get good music estab-
lished in a small way, and good people to support
it. Anyhow, a clan like that one on Eastern
Hill isn’t fated to be our Mentor in music. Its
following is composed of two-thirds partisans and
one-third music lovers, which disproportion is
encouraged and maintained by the leaders of the
blind folk.
Hjalmar Jeremias Josephi presents two gifted
Australiennes and their music for Melbourne’s
edification in a while. Our Una Bourne joins
with NoraKyffin Thomas, of Adelaide, in a piano
and violin recital. Una, of course, is always an
exquisite little artist. Nora is a prize plum out
of the Joachim pudding, and fiddles like one of
the original angels who have never had their
technique tainted by an earthly apprenticeship.
The Rurr,fords gave a farewell reception at
Menzies’ on Friday. There was a steady stream
of paw shakers from 4.30 till 6.30. The Madden
lady, pale and quiet and alone, motored along for
a five minutes’ stay ; she covered a novice-white
frock with the dead black of a silk motor coat, and
accented the funereal note with a black hat bent
over a white face. Jovial John Forrest looked
small in comparison with the Butt lady; his
rotundity rades his height to a close finish. The
Forrest fady circulated her sweetest smile. Mrs
Daniels came with Mrs. Roberts. Daughter Bee
warmed the dull grey of the day with her auburn
locks—the hair that Fox painted in his most
successful portrait. She wore an adorable coat
of ’possum-colored cloth covering a lacy green
silk frilly frock, and topped the hair with brown
gear. The three youthful Tait matrons were smartly
tailored. Mrs. Rumford clothed her magnificence
in black lace and white, and Joy and Toy and
Boy and Coy were in blissful evidence. Daughter
Joy was fantastically fitted out as a pink silk
Juliet with her hair snooded in pearls, while Baby
Victor waddled about like a miniature George
Reid—his fat legs wobbling a Yes-No in his
attempts to walk, and his small rotundity clad in
tight white satin. It was a homely, friendly party.
Emelie Dyason, whose father digs ducats from
Golden City mines, rushes hilariously about Ben-
digo waving a contract. She is engaged as sole
pianiste with the Kubelik company for the whole
Australian tour. Miss Dyason possesses the true
Bendigonian harmony bump. Papa Dyason is a
musically-eccentric mortal, who turns his house into
a sort of refuge for orphan pianos. One drawing-
room possesses six grands, and twelve fiddles, an
odd ’cello and a trombone.
Louie Mueller, who warbles with the Kubelik
co., is the pianist’s most intense chum. The two
made their earliest mud pies from the same clay.
Clara Butt said she’d seen “ Peter Pan ”
twenty-one times in London, and she “would not
miss the Melbourne first night if she had to stand
at the back ! ” The management, however, found
the lady a box to fit her. It was a little tight at
the back seam, and the skirt was skimpy, but she
got into it.
Puccini, the Florentine composer, has just
woven a golden net of song-music round a Louise
Mack poem. Mrs. Louise-Mack-Creed is in Italy
now.
Mark Hambourg, of the Lion Head, approaches*
With him come Mrs. Hambourg and the Baby.
Baby Hambourg is busy laying down a mosaic of
schemes to wipe out the triple triumph of the Butt
Babies off his own bat.
Miss T. “Peter Pan ” Brune talks gaily of her
last New York visit. She stayed an hour’s jour-
ney from the city, with an adored sister, and
“ worked hard ’’ —went to the theatre every night
for study. She met Kyrle Bellew of the Legs.
He was working with Dan Frohmann.
Miss Noalles, the Victorian girl who toured
with the Brune co. so much two years
ago, is married in New York to a brother of the
Dress-Clapp sisters, and lives in the cunningest
flat with a Baby Clapp to play with. The Tittell
sister has her husband’s smoking room packed and
lined with Maori curios, collected for the Brune
lady by Maoriland Tourist Donne. The sister
tells visitor folk that the greenstones and the spears
and the deer-heads come from “ Down-There.”
Tittell says she often heard the term in New
York for Australia and Maoriland. Mebbe the
N’Yorkers expect the inhabitants to come up
served with flames and lava sauce.
Our sensational medico —or rather the one
whose sensationalism was most recently dis-
covered — is disappointing the uncommonly
righteous of this city in a most shameful fashion.
The kindly souls had forecast a dreadful future
for him. He was to wander from door to door
seeking patients and finding none. His case was
to be a Lesson to swift and unprincipled ado-
lescence; a Horrible Example to doddering eld.
However, by present indications, it is going to be
nothing of the sort. He has not to seek patients ;
they seek him. It is a fact that his practice has,
if anything, increased since the conclusion of
“The Case.” Churchgoers, when they get sick,
seem to lose all enthusiasm to avenge the Lord’s
fractured enactments, and think only of getting
hold of the best man to put them right again. It
is a paltry view, and enough to make a Sabbath-
school dominie say “ Blow ! ” quite irritably.
Miss “ Marina ” Murphy was most hospitable
to the cocoanut-oiled Fijians when they cricketed
here, so in a recent descent upon this city, the
fulness of the Brown Ones’ gratitude expanded
into a serenade, which struck the poor lady at a
horribly unaired hour one morning. The irri-
tated serenadee draped her head in a shawl, and
shivered in a clutched bath gown while she
thanked them forcibly. They spread themselves
with grins of delight all over her prize chrysan-
themum beds, and left the reek of their oiled
bodies on every verandah tile, and they wailed for
hours in the hope of coflee or resurrected Bun—
but the savage-surfeited lady hid her hospitable
head under the eider-down. Now, the child-like
savage wonders vaguely at the variableness of
the strange White.
The Jubilee of the Anglican Grammar School,
begun three weeks ago by jovial Old Boys, has
been elongated to a furious carnival by the Small
Boy and the Weedy Youth. Regattas and Fights
and Bun sprees made a golden dream, regretfully
relinquished on Saturday night with a final
Dramatic Frivol. The Jubilee jamboree has
embraced the Small Girl of local schools who has
rushed into an abandoned dissipation of New
Hair Ribbon for the enslavement of her bun-
filled boy.
The psychological inwardness of Hair Strings
has never appealed so strongly to me before.
“ Looks pretty ? ” snorted, one fourteen-year-old
feminine in my hearing. “Why, she’s worn the
same hair ribbon three times to Evenings, and her
stockings never match the color. Huh ! ” and she
tossed her scarlet-crested head scornfully, and
viewed her lanky scarlet legs with blissful superi-
ority, while she ostentatiously displayed the
infinitesimal fraction of a curve cut in her “semi-
evening ” dress.
The India brought a “frightfully fascinating”
(to quote “Peter Pan’s” Wendy) freight to
Toorak this trip. The Fairbairn’s one ewe lamb,
Alison, comes triumphantly home from the Coral
Strand for a Ti-tree trip, with Captain hubby in
tow, and Baby Balmain and its ayah in the lime-
light. Prof. Spencer’s wife and daughters were
also India passengers—returning after two years’
journeying. The girls have learnt French-as-she-
is-spoke at a Parisian pension, while Mamma
played about and took them for occasional jaunts.
Softgoods Butler and his imposing dame once
more add to the gaiety of show audiences. They’ve
motored all round the British Isles and over half
the European continent; they’ve climbed moun-
tains and slid down again with toboggan, and put
in a record rapid trip. The Butler baby-boys stay
at school in Paris, with Auntie Flo Wilson within
coo-ee.
Chapel-street, P’ran, the Mecca of the careful
housewife, the factory tart, and the people of no
importance, was lifted from its sordid gloom one
afternoon lately by a vision of Clara (there was only
one in Melbourne just then) and her devoted
Kennerley. They drove slowly along. Clara, as
she leant back, smiled lazily, and buried her
massive chin in the plateau of violets that decor-
ated that noble chest where the “Lost Chord”
might be found. Clara always reminds me of the
“Arabian Nights ” beauties. (I had a copy and
wasted my youth reading it in the hay loft.)
They had names like Ballyboudour, and had “the
bath ” with a great escort of maidens, and were
so modest they swooned whenever they saw a
man ! But they were beautiful, like Clara. And
as Kennerley smiled and talked to his wife
(as if he had just been introduced and was anxious
to make an impression), with his beautiful brown
eyes alight and the setting sun shining on his sage-
green hat, the heart of the careful housewife con-
tracted with sudden envy, and she didn’t seem to
care whether she bought beef or horse, for the old
man never looked at her like that. And the
little “factory rat” sniffed and murmured, “Did
jer see’er feather?” And when Clara was gone
the people of no importance returned to the
eternal two-and-elevenpenny flannelette blouse,
and the lame boy in the chair pinned the corner
of his rug round his faithful dog, for public atten-
tion had returned to him once more.
An interesting exile sends me the following
from London :
Our new Governor General, Lord Dudley, is recog-
nised by the cream of the Hub here, as in the Irish
capital, as one of the social ornaments of the hour.
He will be found more affable than any viceroy since
the late Lord Hopetoun’s time ; his wife, after a “ meteoric
career,” shines as one of the tactful, accomplished, cute,
but ingratiating graces of double-bevelled circles.
South Australian Agent-General Jenkins grows in the
girth two yards around —another Ho-Jolly Jenkins ! . . .
Mrs. Rupert Clarke circulates at Monte Carlo. . . .
Squatter Niall’s long figure perambulates Bond-street. . .
Hugh Ward buzzes round Piccadilly. . . . Jennie Brennan
rushes from town to town inspecting the panto twirlers.
The world is still young enough for a boy and
girl romance to bloom here and there in its
gardens. The heroine of this story is a fair,
slender girleen who bids fair to be one of
Melbourne’s Society beauties. Though not yet
a debutante, she has spent a fair section of
her 18 summers in the hot-house atmosphere of first
nights and cafes, hansom cabs and rinks, but
always under the careful wing of mamma, whose
ambitions for her lovely daughter were boundless.
But sometimes Cupid’s call is stronger than that
of Society, and, like the little girl in the fairy-tale,
she ran away and got married to a young lover
who had just left school. The pair were found in
an East Melbourne boarding-house, and last week
the little bride was whirled off to England, leaving
the little husband to break his little heart on this
side of the world.
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
The Fox-Robertson wedding made the expected stir,
and a ripple or two over. The Rumford-Butt company
was right in the thick of the commotion. Mr. Kennerley
Butt gave the bride to her Benedict, the great Clara
distributed smiles to the elect, and both were photo-
graphed in the bridal group. Secretary Ibbs best-
manned the bridegroom, and accompanist Godfrey
helped organist Arthur Otto with his large obstreperous
instrument. The fully-choral Voice breathed at St. John’s,
which was garbed in a moderate amount of vegetation ;
and the subsequent reception occupied the Lyric Club,
where there was more vegetation, along with tea and a
beauteous absence of speeches. The bride wore a grace-
ful garment of white ninon, with soft lace and chiffon
fluffed about the bodice, and a white picture hat. Her
bridesmaiden, a young Koeppen-Wendt damsel, was in
palest pink glac6, and carried a floral staff ; and there
were two white-robed infant train-bearers (tbe bride's
small son and an infinitesimal niece). Mrs. Runiford was
resplendent in bright blue ninon de soie, panelled with gor-
geous Limerick lace, scattered over with silver butterflies.
Her hat was black and white, with the inevitable plumes
overoneear. Mrs. KoeppenPorter,mammaofthebride,was
garbed in black silk, with a violet toque, and she carried
some of the real variety of modest vegetable in her
bouquet. The pair are spending the first week of the
honey period in “ the Hills,” and then they join the Mon-
golia at Largs on April 30, and sail as far as Perth with
the Butts.
Another wedding of the week was that of Speaker
Coles’ daughter Annie and lawyer Willie Gunson. It
occurred quietly at Glenelg. The Attorney-General Prop-
stings, of Tasmania (Mrs. Propsting was the eldest Coles
daughter), came over in a body. The honeycomb in this
case is also being gathered in our weary, frequently-
mentioned hills.

There died in Adelaide, last week, aged 66, Dr. William
Campbell, who had been practising in South Oss for over
30 years. He had long been honorary consulting phv
sician at the Children’s Hospital, and was a truly chad
table man in a quiet way. Also he was a considerable
authority on Art, and possessed a fine collection of nio
tures and china. Mrs. Arthur Rymill is one daughter
Bland Holt, at our Royal, is having houses that even
Julius Superfluous couldn’t rival. On Easter Mondav eve
enough population was turned away to fill every other
place of amusement in Adelaide, and “ The White
Heather ” had to be kept on for an extra week.
The Chrysanthemum Show on the lawns of the
Children’s Hospital clashed with the Butt matinbe •
therefore the attendance was small and shorn of frill’
although the shekels were all for the pet charity. Two
marquees housed the exhibits, and a “Continental”
wailed through the evening hours. Their Exes, went in
during the afternoon, and president John Lewis shook
them gratefully by the paw, and gave Lady Le Hunte a
nosegay. Miss Hague won the chief dinner-table prize
with pink begonias and ferns.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
Sydney’s old friend, Gilbert Parker, ap-
pears to have graduated from Freetrade through
Preferentialism to solid Protectionism. His last
speech in the House of Commons attributed the
ruin of the Kent hop industry to the dumping of
8000 tons of American hops in the United King-
dom all within a few weeks. It is obvious that
if such unrestricted imports may ruin a genera-
tions-old industry, they can most effectually pre-
vent the establishment of a new one. Horatio
Gilbert Parker was of the uncompromisingCobden
school while lecturing and journalising in Aus-
tralia, so that there is hope for others of his ilk.
John Davis, Owen Lewis, and Thomas
Rooksby, members of the crew of the ship Irene,
which reached Queensland with a cargo of immi-
grants on February 14, 1858, are still going strong
in Brisbane. They were photographed together
the other day. For the first year or two after
arrival, Davis was engaged in the Bananaland
coastal trade. He gave it up for river work,
chiefly as captain of tug boats. In this risky
work—in those days the navigation of the Bris-
bane River, even now no easy task, was like
looking for a clue to last year’s murder—he never
had an accident. Lewis became a road and
bridge contractor in Central Queensland.
Rooksby got a job at the Bulwer (Moreton Bay)
Pilot Station. Three years later he became
master of the lightship Rose, which used to boh
horribly up and down at the Brisbane bar. He
put in seven years there, when he was appointed
to the charge of the newly-built Bustard Head
lighthouse. He stuck to that job for thirty-three
years, and then retired.

Interstate Mail
Steamship Lines
I
S hinted in the
previous article
on the subject,
the N orthQueens-
land Winter Tour
opens up a series
of excursions, etc.,
of exceptional in-
terest to the traveller. It will
now be the purpose of this article to
indicate a little of what is to be seen. As
suggested previously, Cairns is generally the
goal of the northward-bound tourist; but there
are other towns along the route which will repay
inspection. Sydney was treated in an earlier
article, so it will suffice to pass it by without fur-
ther comment. Brisbane is the next port of call.
Prettily situated on the Brisbane River, it is well-
equipped with facilities for rapid conveyance to
any part of the environs. The best and cheapest
of these facilities is undoubtedly the excellent
electric tram service, the journey on the
Toowong line through the Western suburbs, as an
instance, being particularly delightful. From the
tram terminus a short walk brings one to Mount
Coot-tha, from which a magnificent view, embrac-
ing all the country between Ipswich and
Sandgate, may be obtained. One might men-
tion the seaside resorts of Sandgate and Redcliffe;
one might refer to the Eagle Farm Racecourse, to
the Bowling and Golf Clubs which welcome the
Southerner with a large, glad hand —but a mere
catalogue of names grows tiresome. Within the
city of Brisbane itself are many private and public
buildings, such as the Treasury, the G.P.0., His
Majesty’s Theatre, which have their counterparts
in southern capitals. The city is also adorned
with extensive parks, the Botanic Gardens on the
river bank at Garden Point, being notable on
account of its collection of tropical plants.
After leaving Brisbane in one of the steamers of
the Five Companies, one commences to experience
the delights of the sea journey, which returned
travellers have described in a language rich in
superlatives. One passes through the Whitsunday
Passage, with its wooded islands rising precipi-
tously from a sea that is like a big blue jewel cast
down indifferently. One takes a trip into fairy-
land, via the Hinchinbrook Channel. One sees,
on either side, rugged mountain slopes covered
with tropical verdure; island upon island,
tree-clad to the summit, passing by change-
less and beautiful —but one may not
dwell on these things lengthily.
But now the traveller finds
himself at Rockhampton, which
is 43 miles up the Fitzroy
River, and which is reached
by transhipping in Keppel
Bay. The city, which is the
outlet for the productions
from a great extent of pas-
toral and mineral country,
is memorable to the visitor
as having the world-famous Mount Morgan
gold and copper mines in its vicinity. The
mines, which yield, per annum, about three-
quarters of a million sterling in gold and cop-
per, are also celebrated for the completeness of
the plants used. For visitors interested in mining
developments these mines will have an undoubted
fascination ; while the engineer— indeed, the
person without any pretensions to knowledge on
the subject—will scarcely grudge the time spent
in an inspection. Again, the new blast furnaces
and converters, said to have a capacity of
10,000 tons per month, will have a special attrac-
tion for the expert. Of course, one may spend a
whole day advantageously in looking round Rock-
hampton itself. Its Anglican and Roman Catholic
Cathedrals, of a fine order of architecture, by the
way; its many public buildings; its Botanical
Gardens should all inspire more than a feeling of
indifference; while Emu Park, should also be
mentioned. Likewise, the spacious Olsen’s and
Johnson’s Caves, situated about 16 miles from the
city, should not be omitted from the itinerary of
the tourist who desires to be thorough.
Northward again is Mackay, on the Pioneer
River, and one of the principal centres of the
cane industry. Here one may observe the differ-
ent processes whereby the cane is crushed and
manufactured into sugar. The cane season does
not commence until about July; so the stranger
from the South will in all likelihood see the in-
dustry in its most flourishing conditions, and,
moreover, watch his own countrymen at work in
the fields. Naturally, Mackay in other ways is
well adapted for a short stay ; but space demands
just a passing comment. Its panoramic views and
the orchards in its vicinity secure for it a gradu-
ally increasing popularity.
Now comes Townsville, the commercial capital
of North Queensland. Townsville, about two days
by sea from Rockhampton, is the port of a large
area of mining and pastoral country, and also de-
rives much benefit from the cane and fruit-growing
districts. It is in no way meanly built; some of
its buildings, such as the Supreme Court and the
Custom House, may be said to be equal to
any similar edifices in Queensland ; while
its harbors afford to the deep sea traffic
accommodation of a high order. Cleveland Bay,
on the shores of which the city is built, is indeed
one of the finest anchorages on the Queens-
land coast. At Townsville the traveller en-
trains for Charters Towers, which, distant
about 80 miles, is the centre of goldfields,
said to be the richest in all Queensland. To give
some idea of their mineral wealth, it may be stated
tnat for one year (1906) the yield was 205,632 fine
ounces, to the value of £873,469. Since its dis-
covery, in 1872, the growth of the industry has
been wonderful, and it now supports a thriving
population of 25,000 inhabitants. No one who
visits Townsville can afford to overlook Charters
Towers, the town itself, irrespective of the various
mining properties, being a good illustration of a
mining town under sound municipal government.
#3?
Lastly comes Cairns, which is' best reached by
transhipping at Townsville, and proceeding per
Kuranda via Hinchinbrook, Lucinda Point, and
the Johnstone River. The town squats in a
wild tangle of jungle on the shores of Trinity
Bay. Cairns is the point of disembarkation
for excursions to the Barron Falls, the
fame of which is a byword in the mouths
of men. The rail journey, through miles of tropi-
cal vegetation and forest growths, is inexpressibly
beautiful. The traveller climbs up the wall of
mountain and, comfortably ensconced in a railway
compartment, gazes into mighty depths with
eternity at the bottom. But the journey is only a
preliminary to what is to follow. Annd a dense
tropical jungle the river hurls into a gorge eight
hundred feet below. Through the rising and
ever-changing mists the cataracts gleam, torn
into foam. But beyond the Falls at Kuranda—a
journey of a few hours hence by rail is the magni-
ficent Atherton Scrub. In all Australia there is
nothing so wildly beautiful as this. Here flourish
great cedars, rosewood and satinwood trees, ban-
yans, ferns, and orchids ; flowering vines climbing
the trunks to the height of quite a hundred feet —
all completing a picture grand and almost savagely
alluring. Beyond—but a journey of a couple of
hours —is Lake Eacharn, once a volcano, but now
asheetofdead blue water in the mountains. Then,
a dozen miles distant from Atherton, lie Herberton
and its tin mines, said to be equal to Southern
Tasmania in their prevailing climatic conditions.
Again, branching off at Mareeba, the railway goes
northerly to Chillagoe with its mines, its rocks,
and its wonderful limestone caves. One may
travel further on to Mount Garnet and to the
Innot Springs, declared to pos-
sess medicinal proper-
ties; and thus conclude
a trip that will be a
precious recollection
in after years. The
North Queensland
Winter Tour must
be made sometime—
if Queensland is to
be understood. That
time is now !
