The Inky Way.
THE EVENING PAPER “SUB.”
[Fob Tub Bulletin.]
He calls us out at dead of night,
He wakes us from our troubled sleep ;
VVe blink and dress by candlelight,
And in the cold grey dawn we creep
(While he, impatient, waits and stews)
To glean the latest murder news.
He sends us to the deadhouse, too,
Where festive microbes prowl and lurk,
To hear the evidence so blue,
That tells of misery and murk.
He will not hearken to our views,
But only snaps “ We MUST have news.”
He sends us to police-courts drear,
Where tales of villainy are rife,
And where we see the curse of beer
Stamped on the bruised and battered wife,
And strain our ears lest we might lose
Some scrap of lurid, spicy news.
Confound this hard reporter’s life,
And hang the cry of “ Boil it down !”
And curse this never-ending strife,
A plague upon i.,e “ sub’s” grim frown ;
Blast these perpetual interviews !
DAMN this eternal yelp for news !
MA0R1LAND. H. E. BOON.
* * * *
G. L. Jessop, the mighty smiter of the cricket-field
(when he hits the ball), puts in his winter months as
judge of a Limerick competition. His latest award gives
£13 Os. 8d. to a line about a pantomime clown, whose
“ hits cause the misses to frown.” The words “ hits ” and
“misses” are both in inverted commas, and there is a
heavy black line under each word as well. They do ram
their jokes home in London.
* * * *
“The Age will be my monument,” David Syme said to
one of his sons just before his death. Syrne’s will, just
up for probate, shows the Age entailed —from father to
son —through generations of Byrne as far, as entail will
reach. The house of Syme will not easily die out. David,
the King, begat a great family, and about him rose many
sons and'many grandsons. And his monument he has
built himself, one great stone upon another, with the
sweat of his brow and the rude strength of his own labor,
and a fierce whip for the less willing in his working gangs.
* * * *
Asquith, the new British Prime Minister, indirectly
gave T. P. O’Connor, the editor of a London evening
paper the chance to write the most taking contents-
poster in journalism, It was in connection with the
Parnell Commission, which all Britain, and London in
particular, was watching at the time with frenzied in-
terest Asquith was acting as junior to Russell (after-
wards Chief Justice of England) who was cross-examining
the forger Piggott, and he pointed out to his leader
that the word “hesitancy” was spelt “hesitency”
in the damning letter which Parnell was alleged
by the Times to have written. Russell asked Piggott
to write a number of words—among them, “ hesitancy.
Piggott who had already been cornered and discredited
a score of times by the great barrister, fell in yet again.
He spelt the word “ hesitency.” He left the court, and
next morning failed to turn up, nor was he in the witness-
box that afternoon. He had vanished. And “T.P.’s”
contents-boards came out the same evening in flaring
capitals with the news
The Man who Hesitetes is Lost.
* * * *
The London Tribune, the paper which was started with
a blare of Freet-rade trumpets, two short years ago, as
the “official organ of Front Bench Liberalism,” has died
the death of the average new London daily of late years.
With S. J. Pryor, one of the most capable of British
journalists, as editor, and the financial backing of
Thomasson, M.P., a wealthy cotton spinner, it was
reasonable to expect that the Tribune venture would
succeed. But it didn’t ; instead it has lost £390,000.
The deceased journal was the only penny London
morning sheet advocating those vague things which are
known in Fogland as “ Liberal principles.” Other dailies
which have been started and proved rank and deadly
failures of very recent years (since the uprising of the
Daily Mail, that is to say) are the Majority, the Daily
Courier, the Morning, the Sun, and the Echo. The
much-boomed Daily Paper bust with a loud detonation
almost immediately it was launched. Freetrade England
is a remarkably bad place to start a Freetrade newspaper
in these days.
* * * *
W. E. Smith, Sydney, has issued a Local Government
Diary. It is for use by mayors, aldermen, councillors,
and the like, and gives a useful item of information for
each day in the year. Thus on Tuesday, April 21st (1567), so
the reader learns, “ the Spanish Armada was destroyed,”
while on the 11th of the same month (1893) “ Mo'sman
was incorporated.” This work caters with a sure hand
for the it<jn municipal mind whose range is simply
immense.

“Crosscut”: He was the only other customer in the
pub, and I asked him to help me wash down some of the
dust. He was willing. Between gulps he said he was a
journalist, and had just terminated a short engagement
of three days with the bi-weekly Wild Dog Banner. On
the first day the tow n was visited by a clerical person of
enormous tonnage, who was announced to give an address
on “ The Value of Cheap Phonographs as a Civilising In.
fiuence on South Sea Cannibals.” My friend had
to make a two-column report. The day was warm,
and having no desire to hear anything more of
phonographs than he already knew, he dropped in at
a pub by the back way. The bar parlor was cool, and the
beer still cooler ; and the air outside had melted with the
heat and was running down the street. So he lingered
and had a few more. When he eventually arrived at the
meeting place, he found the hall locked and everybody
gone. But he had heard the same “ light” drone somere-
marks on the subject a little while before, so he
gave full rein to his imagination, which had been
well oiled during his stay in the pub, and churned
out two columns of eloquence about cannibals and
phonographs. The report was duly published, and
the “ Old Man ” went to the length of complimenting
him on the way he had handled it. Flattered, the new
reporter “ shouted ” for all Wild Dog. Next morning he
picked up “ our contemporary,” which intimated that
Canon Worryboy, having left the notes of his original
address behind him, had lectured instead on “Little
Lessons We Can Learn By Watching Beetles.” On the
opposite page appeared a scathing attack on the depraved
Wild Dog Banner for its senseless hoax, “ which, we ven-
ture to assert, will go near to ruining what circulation it
still has.” My friend left Wild Dog hurriedly ; and he
told me, with tears in his eyes, that he thought another
beer would give him strength enough to resume his
journey.

“ The Swimmer ” : Most of the Maoriland dailies have
such a lot of surplus space that they send out staff men
to report all the little tin-pot council meetings. Maori-
land pressmen are accustomed to the duty, but the strain
has driven imported pressmen to drink Maoriland heer.
One Rawdon—or something like it—a new-chum English
pressman with a haw-haw manner, was turned loose to
report a shire council meeting shortly after he joined the
Auckland Star. For three hours the council furiously
discussed whether it should have a new drainpipe at the
rear of the shire hall. Shortly before 10 o’clock Rawdon,
who had slept uneasily for the past hour, awoke with a
start to a recollection of the iniquitous liquor law which
closes the Auckland pubs at 10 sharp. The council was
still in the thick of the discussion. Rawdon waited until
an aged local legislator had finished his great speech on
the sacredness of the trust of public funds, and then ap-
proached the president. “ Your Worship,” he said
courteously, “ what is the price of this—haw—drain-
pipe? ” His Worship glanced at him in surprise. “ About
7s. 6d., I suppose,” he said. Rawdon seized the Mayor’s
hairy fist, and delicately slipped three half-crowns’into
his palm. “Let us bring this—haw—discussion to a
close,” he said. “ I want to get away.”

The Bulletin has received a copy of the
Paraffine Paint Co.’s “No. 6 Interchange,” and learns
from it that mighty changes are taking place on the tops
of the buildings that are springing up all over Sydney, as
the result of Federation and Moderate Protection. With
the introduction of Paraffine Paint, P. & B. Concrete, and
Malthoid, the flat roof is rapidly becoming an institution,
and the city dweller is beginning to make a paradise of
ferns and pot plants out under the stars where he can sit
and smoke at nights, and breathe pure fresh air far above
the jangling* streets. Hitherto, the flat roof has meant a
leaky house, now it means merely a set of rooms cool in
summer, warm in winter, and dry as the proverbial bone,
and a private garden out under the sky where noise and
dust do not come.*

SPORTING
NOTIONS
N.S.W. struck disaster on the bowling-greens
last week, when Victoria crushed it by 124 points to 84,
and Maoriland rolled it up with 95 to 78. But
the crowning disaster for the combined Australian
teams came on Saturday, at Ashfield green,
when the Maorilanders proved victorious by 11(1
to 114. The green, being slightly heavy after the wet
I weather, favored the invaders, who are somewhat tnore
accustomed to damp greens than the Australians, and as
its dampness did pot affect its order in any way, the
Maorilanders were in their element. It was a great
and magnificent struggle, and for once the bowlers
threw to the winds the fine reserve and stoical forti-
tude that usually characterise the ancient game, and
whooped and roared applause as each succeeding shot
sent the pendulum swinging now this way and now that.
Close as the victory was, it was unexpected, and came as
a surprise to most bowlers. The visitors had a good time
while in Australia, and among the pleasant features of the
trip was a day with the President, when they all went to
La Perouse arid thence by launch to Kurnell, where Mr.
E. W. O’Sullivan filled them up with history while
the President poured sustenance into them. The bowlers
declared themselves delighted with both operations.
* • * •
Points from Onkaparinga, Adelaide’s Easter
Monday picnic race-meeting- the Club having no other
fixture in the vear. Stakes given for the day, £2350,
including a £iooo steeplechase. Passed through the
totalisator, £20,633, of which the Club retains per
cent, and the Government another 2£ per cent. The
steeple winner, Tarpon, owned by R. M. Hawker, a local
sportsman, paid a dividend of £5 165., and the second
horse (after the cheerful S. A. custom) returned his
backers £4 16s. Oamelot(Tradition-Circlet), brought over
from Melbourne to win this event, and backed down to 6
to 4 in a field of 13, broke his back in the third round of
the long and trying 3|-mile course.
At Ararat (Vic.) the other Sunday, parson J. J.
Spalding (no relation to the Private Secretary) preached
from Isaiah, chap. 63, verse 1: “ Who is this that cometh
from Edom, with dyed garments from Bozrah ? this that
is glorious in his apparel, travelling in the greatness of
his strength ? ” The congregation gave a good guess. It
rushed from church, formed a syndicate, and backed
Dyed Garments for Sydney Cup, and Dyed Garments,
“'in the greatness of his strength,” got home ahead of the
field. Why don’t men go to church ? Well, if the Church
was always as accurate as this in its tips they would.

Some A. J.C. fragments : Tartan has started in
34 races, of which he won 13, ran second in five, third in
six, and nowhere in 10. Of £17,771 stake money, N.S.W.
reps, gathered the big whack (£11,964), despite that the
two richest things (Doncaster and Cup) fell to visiting
nags. Westralian owner, P. A. Connolly, got away with
£2298, and was the biggest individual winner ; youthful
trainer-owner of Malt Queen (Harry Brennan, son of
“lucky Jim”)was second in the list with £2007. Vic-
torians carried £lB3B home, and Queenslanders
£1671. Most successful of 19 sires represented
were Wallace, Malster, Mikado 11., Positano and Grafton
—two wins each. The 24 races were won
by 16 jockeys. F. Hickey, D. Callinan, and Clayton
secured three each, and A. Hood and J. Smith (Vic. cross-
country rider) two each. The biggest of the few good
winners was P. A. Connolly j atop of his Cup pile he
scooped considerable over Poseidon’s Cumberland Stakes
and other events. Among unlucky owners M.L.’s bowler-
sport, Bill Lyons, who assisted materially in the Apologue
coup last spring, figured prominently. His well-per-
formed Waipuna, heavily backed, ran second twice-
coming just a bit too late in City Handicap (Wednesday),
and, though turned the other way about at the start of
the Place Handicap, was only beaten by a neck.
Though Randwick is, as a rule, a place of milk
and honey for Patrick Moses, probably at no time in the
A.J.C.’s long history have results been so persistently
against punters as on the latest occasion. The most
favorable day of the four from a backer’s view was the
last, and even then his natural enemy left the battle
ground with a fair margin to the good. The one handi-
cap in the long list that fell to a favorite was the Cup,
and once only did a second favorite (Tangaroa, in the
Rous Handicap) triumph. Black Range (Monday) and
Argea (Wednesday)—latter was 100 to 1 against for Don-
caster Handicap, and shaped accordingly, and stable
backed Adriatic in preference to Black Range—were
veritable things from the clouds. They saved Moses the
trouble of paying out, thus enabling him to straightway
begin gathering in his next load of loot. Of three w.f.a.
races first three days, two were ruinous to some
backers, and the excessive odds (12 to 1 on) asked
by Ikey about the winner (Mountain King) of the third
(the St. Leger) debarred all but out-and-out gamblers
from investing. Despite Poseidon’s prodigious failure in
the Autumn Stakes (Saturday), he made Tartan look a
commoner in the Cumberland Stakes (Wednesday), and
the bulk of the crowd demonstrated as a Randwick mob
never demonstrated before—the members’ stand contri-
buted considerably to the kick. They were sore over
Poseidon’s very late withdrawal from the Cup, which his
run in the Cumberland Stakes persuaded them he might
have won, and they were more pained over the reversal
of form compared with Saturday’s display, but the differ-
ence was accounted for by that bump mentioned in The
Bulletin (23/4/’08). Someone made a goodly haul
through Poseidon’s success. Independent of smaller
investments, bets of £3OO to £lOO, £350 to £lOO, and, in
one case, £4OO to £lOO, were booked. Reported that one
P. Moses boasts of landing £9OOO over the meeting and
another £7OOO.
* # * *
Mountain King’s displays were poor compared
with the brilliant things done by him in the spring. He
hadn’t the vim of last year—a fact possibly due to that
“ musical”trouble (which The Bulletin referred to some
time ago, thereby getting itself disliked in some quarters)
becoming more pronounced. Welcome Trist’s threat in
the straight during the running of the St. Leger sug-
gested that had Peru been a starter he might have sorely
tried “ the King,” who, on the following Wednesday,
didn’t win the All Aged Stakes too easily, though the
margin was a comfortable one. Hence the racing of him
again the same afternoon in Cumberland Stakes looked
folly. After battling hard over a mile to get away, the
horse tired almost to the crawling point.
* * * *
Poor as was the A. J.C. first steeple, the second,
on Saturday, fell even flatter. Two of the three starters
flopped and came down With such weight that neither 1 of
them was equal to going after the £BO and £4O (second
and third money) which waited for anything that could
finish before the next race. As in Easter Monday’s
spectacle, and in face of an added load of 101 b., Kuala
Lumper, who started favorite, again proved, the safest
convey—and was alone over 2£ miles of the 3-mile course.
Thus 'did £l2O of the £4OO offered revert to the club,
making a total unexpected saving for the day of £770,
which The Bulletin would be glad to hear has been
handed over to some hospital. Time these jumping farces
were dropped and the money put to better uses. Last
year’s second ’chase was bad enough, when three of five
starters fell, but .this year’s record bangs Banagher. ,
Tlie slow pace at which A.J.C. Plate (3m.)
was run on Saturday saved the club’s coffers £650. A
special provision fixes the maximum time-limit for the
race at 6min. 45sec., and if that be exceeded, half first,
second, and third money is withheld, consequently
winner only received £5OO, second £lOO, and third £5O.
The three starters (Poseidon, Tartan, and Dyed Gar-
ments) covered the first two furlongs in little better than
a walk. Randwick never before saw anything so bad of
its kind,' and hooting was general, but without, result
worth mentioning, till the beginning of the last ljm.,
when the trio got going strongly and finished fast. Posei-
don (3 to 1 on) won easily, with Tartan just in front of
the other starter. Time (6min. 49£sec.) is the slowest
ever recorded in the race’s history. The previous worst
during past 20 years was The Harvester’s 6min. 17sec. in
1896. The cheering of Poseidon was in marked contrast
with the roar of boo-hoos that owner Denison heard on
Wednesday, and plainly showed how very closely the
average racegoer’s feelings are associated with his
pocket. #
Theoutstandingfeaturesof the Sydney Metropoli-
tan Rugby Union’s competition so far have been the defeat
of Glebe, the premier club of last year, in both matches,
and the recovery of Eastern Suburbs from the effects of
the disruption 'of the League. When Messenger went
over to the Northern Union division he took half, and
the stronger half, of the kingdom with him. And the
spectacle of the reconstructed club defeating the formid-
able Glebe in a pitched battle on Saturday last was an
amazing one. The Eastern Suburbs deserved to win.
Captain Murnin handled his men as few generals have
handled an army, shouting instructions which were
obeyed to the letter, and paralysing the premiers with an
overwhelming combination of speed and tactics. It was
the match of the day.
The game opened with 20 minutes of give and
take, in which much long kicking kept both forwards
practically standing still. With a sudden rush Glebe
threw the East upon its goal line, and made a great effort
to score. D’Alpuget refused to save the situation by
forcing, and recklessly ran the ball out, and out of a
series of scrums the Eastern forwards suddenly swept
away in a magnificent dribbling rush. Glebe men
were side-stepped, and the Eastern line, sweeping
along with a perfect face, kept the leather zig-
zagging towards the Glebe line at a great speed.
Behind, the backs dusted along, ready for the pass if the
charge should be blocked. It was stopped right on the
line after one of the best combined efforts ever seen on a
Sydney football ground. With a huge effort East got the
ball to the backs, and the passing work was very pretty,
the last man dashing over the line, and scoring amid
tremendous enthusiasm. The shot at goal failed. In play
again, the East tried to repeat the combined movement,
but Glebe fell on the forwards in a manner that was more
vimful than legal. Men who were only making for the
ball were hauled down, and the heavy Reds, by sheer
weight and brute forcfe, shovelled the Eastern men
steadily back. Glebe reached a spot within 10yds. of the
Eastern line, and piling all its force on the ball, tried
to lift the whole Eastern team back over the line bodily.
It was a blunder. The Eastern backs got going, and the
ball was kicked into touch at half-way. But it was only a
respite. Glebe again got possession, and surging forward,
burst through and scored, and Hickey, adding the extra
points, placed the premiers ahead by five to three. The
second half opened with a struggle on the Eastern
line, where the tackling of the defenders was deadly.
Wrenching the ball from the stormers, Murnin sent his
forwards off at a gallop. They cut the Glebes down and
ran over them, till within striking distance, when the
rush became a passing charge, and a faulty pass gave
Glebe an opportunity to force. Then came the spec-
tacular part of the game. Murnin used his forwards as a
screen from behind which his-backs charged repeatedly,
and although the machine only pierced the Glebe line
once, scoring three, for the rest of the game Glebe was
penned up in its own 25, and only escaped a regular
debacle by sheer good luck. AVhen the whistle blew both
teams were exhausted, and the premiers were defeated by
six to five.
* * * *
The only other match worth mention was that
between University and Newtown. The first half was
spoilt by an excess of individual play, which, though
brilliant, was not Rugby. In the second half the Uni
pulled itself together, and let loose some fine passing
charges which made it victorious by 11 to eight. Manly
failed to live up to its early promise against South Sydney,
and was walloped by 15 to nil; South broke up
its combination and smothered the backs. At Epping
the Balmain team turned up three-quarters of an
hour late, but recovered from its tiredness in a
remarkable manner when it did arrive, and ran over North
Sydney to the tune of nine to nil. Down at Rushcutter’s
Bay a dreary struggle between Sydney and Western
Suburbs ended in the defeat of the latter by nine to six.
* * *
The general result of the scrimmage is to leave
University, Sydney, Eastern Suburbs and South Sydney
dead level with four points each. Balmain, Newtown,
North Sydney, and St. George form the second rank with
two points each, and behind them drift Glebe, Western
Suburbs and Manly with the sign of the fowl on their
banners.
• * * *
The three first-grade League matches in Sydney
were anything but attractive. The men were mostly
out of condition, combination was lacking, and it
was obvious that the teams had not yet settled down to
the new rules. The best play of the day was shown by a
new recruit, Conlon of the Glebe. The one bright
piece of strategy was the desperate way he played
to secure a goal right behind the posts after
crossing the line well on the wing. From where he
crossed it was almost impossible to kick the leather
between the posts, so he passed to another player, whose
chance of taking it was dubious. But it came off, and
the goal was duly kicked. Some of the long-range kick-
ing was also good, but taken all round the displays were
very disappointing. If the League is to last it will have
to show better football than that of Saturday.
At the Agricultural Ground, South Sydney
inflicted a crushing defeat on Western Suburbs, the
figures being 42 to seven. The Newtown-Glebe struggle
at Wentworth Park was much better in every way, and
Glebe won by seven points to two. Balmain and North
Sydney played a very mediocre game,in which flashes of
bright individual play were like whiskies at a charity con-
cert. Altogether it was a dull, drab day. Up at Newcastle,
the All Blacks knocked the contents out of a Newcastle
team, crushing it by 37 points to eight. The Australian
Rule Leaguers are still doing practice matches, but the
real campaign will commence this coming Saturday.
With a view to annexing the early and prema-
ture worm, the ten Vic. Football Association clubs started
their season last Saturday, a Week sooner than usual.
The reptile proved less juicy and luscious than expected,
for the day was a choice sample of midwinter, and the
League crowds weren’t taking any improved Association
game of 17 players a side. The innovation seemed to make
little difference, anyhow, the scrambling ruck play
being the same as of old. It is early yet to expect
concerted, scientific play among men who are mostly
unacquainted with each other’s styles and customs;
nevertheless, far too many of them seem obsessed, with
theidea that the best player is the one who “ bogs in” most
strenuously. Several have already acquired the honor of
being addressed as “ Bogger Smith ” or Jones as a result of
this reckless habit of tossing themselves around ; later on
they may grasp the fact that the cool individual who saves
himself not only lasts longer, but gets more work out
of the ball, so to speak, than the one who rushes
all over the ground in a vain attempt to do
something, often spoiling his mates’ chances in the pro-
cess. As for the games, the Premiers, Williamstown,
ran over the regrettable “ Ports ” by 76 points to
nine. Prahran spiflicated the reorganised “ North ”
by 87 to 14. The amateurs of Essendon made hacks
of the ditto Brighton team —late of the Metropolitan
Association and last year’s runners-up to the mighty
’Varsity team—by 105 to 43. Footscray scored 61
to Preston’s 43—which result argues that either
’Scray isn’t what it was, or Preston is a good
deal more. And the promising Brunswick 17 tackled
the newly-promoted juniors, Northcote, and adminis-
tered a Wallopping to the tune of 121 points to
30. There were no casualties, neither was any umpire
treated to a course of road-metal or palings. These
irregularities occur later on when the players get “ wet,”
and the barrackers are dry with barracking. The League
will begin to “ bog” next Saturday.
The Rugby championship of the British Isles
went again this year to Wales, which beat England,
Ireland and Scotland—the last-named only by one point.
England beat Ireland, which beat Scotland, but all the
matches were close. In “ Soccer,” England and Scotland
have the .championship to themselves, the other two
always competing, but without a hope.
The Mephistophelean influence again. Two
Victorian Patrick Moseses and a jockey were in the thick
of “ nap ” and gloating over a fat “ kitty ” at the moment
of impact between the trains at Sunshine. Flames fed on
the car above and below' and fiercely swallowed surround-
inglumber, but the sinful trio escaped absolutely
unsinged. * #
Australia is not the only country in which the
referee conies in for a violent time occasionally. Re-
cently, in Paris, the well-known rider Jacquelin, having
finished in the semi-final of the Grand Prix de l’Union
Velocopedique de France, found himself disqualified on
the protest of Sydney’s one-time visitor, Henri Meyer, he
of the odd stockings. Jacquelin, overcome by the length
of the name of the affair he had just competed in, flew
right “off his block” at this last infliction. First he
called the judge “hard names”; then he seized him,
and shook him, knocked his hat off, and sus-
pended violent operations to give his vocabulary
another show. The judge beat a retreat, with
Jacquelin in full pursuit. The crowd joined in the hunt,
and w'hen the judge broke the record and escaped, the
mob came back and made a bonfire of the seats and
barriers in the track, and started a row that continued
till no less than 300 French equivalents for c< ppers
arrived and made a huge casualty list which they called
the restoration of peace and quiet.
Latest reports from the States tell that cyclist
Major Taylor is rapidly recovering his old form, and has
beaten J. Fogler, the man who beat Lawson just before
Lawson beat Taylor.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union will open its season
on Saturday, May 16, with a procession from Sydney
Domain through the city out to Centennial Park and
away to Ooogee, where the refreshments will be laid on.
As large prizes are to be offered for the best costumes and
most artistically ornamented machine, something special
in the way of a splash is anticipated.
•* * * *
Dick Arnst reappeared on the wheel at Temuka
(Canterbury, Maoriland) on Easter Monday. The handi-
capper smote him where he lived, making him concede a
series of starts up to 45yds. in a half-mile. This broke
Richard’s boiler ; when he caught his field he was so
pumped out that he couldn’t stay it out, and
finished fourth, howling to the men in front as
they streaked down the straight to wait for a
bloke. Dick always had a tendency to howl about some-
thing in his exciting finishes. When he streaked home
in the Sydney Thousand he was roaring “ I’ve won it !
I’ve won it 1 ” as he bounded over the line like a flash of
lightning going down a greasy steep place. Said that he
is returning to Australia shortly to row Pearce.
* * *
Hockey is going ahead around Sydney like
the great bounding kangaroo of the fairy tale.
Judging by the names they choose for their clubs,
the hockeyites have a sense of humor, or some-
thing just as good —as the chemist says. On
Saturday last the Corinthians met the Coronas, and
the Barbarians battled with the Sydneians, while, very
appropriately, the Bandits struggled with the Pilgrims.
Of the bunch, Corinthians are about the best, but the
whole six clubs are now high-class combinations, and
play a fine, fast game. In Saturday’s struggle the Coronas,
the baby' club, showed remarkable speed, but it was more
than counterbalanced by' the skill of the Corinthians, and
at half-time the scoring board was still empty. The
second half opened with a series of well-sustained attacks
by both sides, but the Corinthians were the rising tide,
and the infants were kept mostly on the defensive.
And a fine defence it was, the ball being banged out again
and again when a score seemed an absolute certainty. But
the skill of the older players asserted itself. Rushing the
leather down the wing they drew the defenders from the
goal, and like a flash it was centred and driven home.
Then Coronas began to go to pieces, condition failed, one
player retired injured, and the Corinthians came again
and again with short quick attacks which made
them winners hy five goals to nil. The Barbarians
beat the Sydneians by the same score after a tough
tussle, in which the beaten side showed good combina-
tion, but was outpaced. The Bandits downed the
Pilgrims by two goals tomne.
* * * *
Writer was under the impression that croquet
was as dead as the Family Herald sort of earl with the
long white hands; but the successful carrying out of a
championship tournament at Strathfield (Sydney), last
Saturday, seems to indicate that there are still people
who play the game and smash their toes when they get
excited—if anyone could get excited over croquet. A
large crowd of very' nice people looked on and applauded
in a pleasant, restrained and well-bred manner reminis-
cent of the order of Vere de Verb.
* * * •
N.S.W. Rowing Association made an ass of
itself by interfering in the Tasmanian rowing rumpus.
The bombastic Tas. Rowing Association, which does not
include half the clubs in Appleland, had played fast and
loose right through its negotiations with the Rowing
Union, first of all consenting to compromise, and then
declining to compromise unless the Union consented to
be eaten, bones and skin ; and also agreeing that two
rival crews should row off to see which should
represent the isle, and then backing out of the arrange-
ment and trying to enforce its disqualifications. A body
that cannot be relied on to keep any arrangements, and
which breaks its promises immediately it sees a
chance of scoring a point, is not worthy of con-
sideration. But the crusty old N.S.W. Rowing Ass,
which oarefully contrives to exclude manual workers
from its crews, could be relied upon to inter-
vene at the wrong moment, and prevent the disput-
>ng parties coming l to an arrangement between them-
selves. As it is, Tasmania will now send two crews to
Melbourne, and the Rowing Conference will be called upon
to decide which is to represent the Isle of Sleep. The
Conference will probably call upon them to row a race,
and let the winner represent the whole of Tasmania.
• * * »
George Whelch, the Akaroa (M.L.) sculler who
has been amateur sculling champion of his island for the
past two years, is now' out after boodle. He has a large
collection of silverware as the reward of 50 or 60 victories
as an amateur, but he wants a share of the gold which
Webb’s party has at command. As a sort of pre-
liminary flutter, Whelch tried to arrange a match with
Dick Arnst, but no reply was received from the ex-cyclist,
and the Akaroa people who are behind Whelch are now’
trying to make up their minds to put their man at the
champion without wasting any more time over the small
frV. There was some talk about a match betw-een Webb
and Arnst, but nothing has been heard of it for weeks,
and everything points to the probability of Webb having
to defend the title against Whelch. Very little has
been heard of Arnst since his return to Maoriland,
and he certainly has not been keeping in form on the
water. Whelch is a rattling good man—many l’engths
faster than any amateur in the Dominion. He “donkey-
licked” Webb, a few years ago, for the amateur cham-
pionship ; he is in the prime of athletic manhood ; his
daily toil as a waterman and fisherman keeps him “ hard ”
all the year round ; and, last but not least, Akaroa is
alive with good sports, and Whelch is in the happy posi-
tion of having stake-money at his command.
* * » *
Bamboo vaulting poles are not viewed with
favor in Southern Canterbury, the home of pole vaulting
in Maoriland, where quite a number of men can clear
10ft. 3in. as easily as they can kiss a good-looking girl.
Quite a number of the cracks have experienced broken
bones and bad shocks through the bamboos splintering
between the joints. The only way to make a bamboo
reasonably safe is to wind it with waxed thread from top
to bottom, and even then it is far from reliable. There’s
a small fortune for anybody who can sell a good, abso-
lutely unbreakable, and yet light vaulting pole in South
Canterbury, where the boys use the weapon for getting
across creeks when they take their walks abroad.
* * * *
Burns may not be the class of the old-time
fighters, but his claim to the title of the world’s heavy-
weight champion is ahead of any of theirs. He has
cleaned up in quick succession the American, the Aus-
tralian, the English and the Irish Champions—a record
never yet put up by any other stoush-merchant. By
latest advices he is going to follow Jim Jeffries’ example,
and take on again as many of his victims as offer, pro-
vided the offer rests on a sound cash basis.
THE ACHIEVING OF THE GLAD HAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hardened fight-fans fancy Bill’s latest method of
handling-out wallops. What they pay to see is blood and
hard slugging, regardless as to what causes the blood to
flow, or in what manner the slugging is administered.
These same fans would dig deeper into their pockets if
the management of a contest would guarantee 'a rough
and tumble fight which admits of biting, gouging, and
kicking.— N. Y. World, 18/8/'OB, in tardy appreciation of
Australia V Bill Squires.
William, we have misjudged you. Put it there,
And shake. The.U.S.A.
To him who scatters round teeth, blood and hair
Their billing devoirs pay.
In ’Frisco, when you fell to Tommy Burns,
We liked you muchly ; still
We glimpsed a weak spot in your ringside turns—
You did not fight to kill.
You never clinched and bit huge fragments from
An adversary’s frame ;
When pugs your placid forehead leaped upon
You failed to do the same.
Eye-gouging was a trick you seemed to shun ;
You never even kicked.
A boxer with such habits, here, is one
Full certain to be licked.
Of late you’ve changed—yet still are far too mild.
God’s country will not wax
Keal mad about you, Bill, till baresark, wild,
You win out with an axe !
N.S.W.

“D.” writes an obituary :
Obit unobtrusively, in a Soho (London) lodging house
(18/3/’08), Richard Benham, who was a figure in the world
15 years ago. He was the master swindler of his day.
Starting with nothing on earth, barring a unique gift for
lying, he raised £l5O (when only 23) from the bank
manager of his town by false representations, and pro-
ceeded to Brussels after certain “deeds.” By keeping
to a rigid system of living w'ell and paying no
one unless by “stumer” cheques, he found himself, six
years later, the highly-esteemed initiator, manager, and
owner of the Western Bank of London, Charing Cross.
This establishment exploded after six months, doing
damage to everyone in its vicinity except Benham.
About this period the latter was living in a £6OO per
annum mansion in Piccadilly ; and at the same time
running separate establishments at Brighton and Tun-
bridge Wells. He paid for necessaries (when he did pay)
by cheques drawn on bogus banks. The fact that at
Tunbridge Wells he borrowed £2OOO from a resident by
name Rolev, and established therewith a volunteer fire
brigade (neglecting to pay, however, for either the fire
engines or the firemen’s uniforms), indicates something of
the subtle humor of the man. Benham’s smash (it ran to
14 years’ hard labor) befell when the swindle he had
worked in connection with the London and General Bank
got abroad. As is usual with this class of fraud, the
fervor of his religious spirit was the admiration of every
old lady who happened across him.

Superintendent of Public Buildings Owen
Smyth, a wild and aggressive Irishman, has just
perpetrated the Hibernianism of establishing a
branch of the Society of St. George in Adelaide.
It is designed to stimulate Englishmen to forget
that they are Australians.
The Imperial Review, in a needlessly
greasy article on Horace Tozer, says the Queens-
land Agent-General “ hopes to return to his native
land.” Probably a good large section of this com-
munity hopes that the lion, member for the con-
stituency of Stinking Fish will think better of it,
and stay away for good. If he does return, there
should be a fairly large clod ready to be thrown at
him on sight, in memory of certain particularly
objectionable slanders he has uttered re Aus-
tralia’s democratic legislation.
Florence Eva Trevanion, a daughter of
Daniel Cooper, was complainant in a libel case in
the Bexhill (Eng.) police court the other week,
and incidentally the tale of the lady’s matrimonial
ventures was related by her solicitor. In 1882 she
wedded Hugh Arundell Trevanion, a cousin of
one of Britain’s insufficiently belted earls. The
marriage turned out a failure, and was dissolved
in 1888. Trevanion married again, and his second
wife died. Three months after the second wife’s
death, Daniel’s daughter, for the sake of their three
sons, became Trevanion’s wife a second time.
The second experiment was just as great a failure
as the first, and at the time of the libel case
divorce proceedings were again pending. It was
a stormy and confused narrative of matrimonial
mischance.

“PURSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division of the
High Court of Justice, dated the 21st January, 1907
made in the Matter of the ESTATE of RICHARD
CALVER CLARKE, deceased, Clarke v. Hutchinson, 1906
C. 3735.—An Inquiry is directed in the following terms'
namely : An inquiry what children there were of William
Press and Robert Press, the brothers of the testator’s
wife, and what children there were of John Clarke
Thomas Clarke, Henry Clarke, and Ann Read, the brothers
and sister of the testator, and what children there were of
Charles Notley, and which of all such children are living
and as to such of them as are dead, an inquiry when they
died, and whether they attained the age of 21 years, and
as to such of them as died before the 10th September,
1906, having attained the age of 21 years, whether
they left any and what children them surviving,
or if they left no such children who are their
legal personal representatives, and as to such of them
as may have died since the 10th September, 1906, who are
their legal personal representatives. Notice is Hereby
Given that the persons claiming to be children of the
above-named William Press, Robert Press, John Clarke,
Thomas Clarke, Henry Clarke, Ann Read, and Charles
Notley, and especially the persons mentioned in the
schedule hereto, or the persons claiming through them,
or to be their legal personal representatives respectively,
are personally, or by their solicitors, on or before the
First day of J tfly, 1908, to come in and prove their claims,
at the Chambers of Mr. Justice Parker, at Room 252, the
Royal Courts of Justice, Strand, London, England, or in
default thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from
the benefit of the said order in this action. Wednesday,
the 15th day of July, 1908, at eleven o’clock in the fore-
noon, at the said Chambers, is appointed for the hearing
and adjudicating upon the claims.
C. BURNEY, Master.
GRIBBLE, ODDIE, SINCLAIR, and JOHNSON, 38
Bedford-row, London, England, Agents for
Humphry Lynde, Great Yarmouth, England,
Plaintiff’s Solicitor.
The SCHEDULE before referred to.
CHILDREN of WILLIAM PRESS.
ADELINE PRESS, one of the children of the said Wil-
liam Press, was born on the 16th July, 1830, atSust.ad,
Norfolk. She married one Thomas Adams, and emigrated
to Australia. She was last heard of about 25 years ago
as living in Australia.
RICHARD PRESS, a son of the said William Press, was
born in April, 1834, at Sustead, Norfolk. He was a
sailor, and went to America in his youth. He was last
heard of on the 7th September, 1871, and was then resid-
ing in New Caledonia. It is not known whether he was
married.
CHILDREN of JOHN CLARKE.
HENRY CLARKE was born in or about 1796, atHoxne,
Suffolk, and was a miller at that place. He is believed to
have left the neighborhood before 1840. It is supposed he
died shortly after, and was never married.
CHILDREN of H ANNAH CLARKE, a daughter of the
above-named John Clarke, who is believed to have married
one Charles Jeffery, a shoemaker, of Homerton, London.
She died 18th November, 1872.
JULIA ANN JEFFERY was born on the 16th January,
1833, and was married to Edwin Theodore Harris on the
4th June, 1854, at Bethnal-green, Middlesex. She is be-
lieved to have died many years ago.
WILLIAM JEFFERY was baptised on the 9th October,
1836, at Homerton, Middlesex. It is not known whether
he is living or dead.
HANNAH MARIA JEFFERY was born on the 17th
February, 1839, at Morning-lane, Homerton, Middlesex.
She lived with a man named Scott, and is believed to be
now dead.
JESSIE JEFFERY was born about 1840, and married a
man named Chapman. She is believed to have died many
years ago. .
SUSAN CLARKE was born about 1800, and is believed
to have married a man named Wood, who was a leather-
cutter in the Old Kent-road, London. She is supposedto
have died many years ago. She left one son, named
RICHARD WOOD, who was born about 1828, and is
also supposed to have been long dead.
CHILDREN of THOMAS CLARKE.
HARRIET CLARKE was baptised 13th September,
1813, at Earsham, Norfolk. She is believed to have
married one John Shaw, and lived for a time at Great
Yarmouth, Norfolk. She and her husband afterwards
emigrated, it is believed, to Jamaica. It is not known
whether she is living or dead, or whether she left any
children. ~..
WILLIAM GREEN SMITH CLARKE was baptised wn
July, 1816, at Earsham, aforesaid. He is believed t
have married and emigrated to Mexico. It is not know
whether he is living or dead, or whether he left any
children.
CHILDREN of CHARLES NOTLEY.
CHARLES NOTLEY was the natural child of Theodora
Notley, who afterwards married John Clarke, mention
in the above inquiry. He was born about 1794, a
baptised on the 20th July, 1794, at Hoxne, Suffolk. 1
not known whether he was married or whether hen
any children.

The (Allegedly) Negligible Chow.
The extremely successful boycott of Japan by
China indicates a circumstance the anti-Defence
Australian hates to recognise—to wit, the Chow’s
dangerously earnest patriotism. A French
essayist discussed the problem recently as to
whether the loathing the Chink notoriously has
for “foreign barbarians” arises from effetely
ignorant prejudice, or from what he called “adis-
torted sentiment of nationalism,” and plumped
with a clothes-basketful of reasons for the second
theory. There now arises one “Chuey, grand
master of the Chinese Masonic Lodge of Australia
(numbering 10,000),” who takes a different
view. Said he, blandly, to a Sydney Telegraph
young man (16/4/’08): “ Some of your papers say
that China is to have a big army and navy. That
will be in hundreds of years. I know the Chinese.
I travel among them, and I know their feelings,
and I am quite sure that they will never want to
fight. They will never be aggressive.” And so
on—the gist of his contention being that the Chow
is naturally so peaceful and shockingly inoffensive
that nothing on earth will ever induce him to be
rude to Australia, and that it is therefore quite
superfluous on the Commonwealth’s part to go
arming itself and preparing for trouble which will
never arise. It is an argument that does not
impress The Bulletin worth a cent. China
has stated its intention of mulcting Japan
of £60.000,000 worth of trade before lifting the
present boycott. It will probably carry out
that threat; or at least a great part
of it. That will entail a big national sacrifice ; a
money sacrifice —the same brand of sacrifice pre-
cisely as is entailed on Germany or the United
Kingdom or America in fleet-building. It may
be said that the Chow will spill his money in his
country’s cause, and yet halt at spilling his blood.
History proves otherwise. The Chow is unusually
careless in the matter of his gore. These yellow
men, thousands of whom voluntarily die by
proxy, and who are wont to jest with the
public executioner two or three heads away
from them, have shown a like indifference
to death on many battle-fields. Their habits
and morals are vile, but their stolid courage is in-
disputable. Chuey and the anti-Defence person
in general would have rational Australians believe
that these people with whom national sentiment
is a fetich (to quote Chuey: “The individual
Chinese only stop in Australia long enough to make
money, and then they go back to the land of their
birth ”), who treat death with flippancy, and who
regard money as nothing in the scale with national
self-respect, are going to voluntarily keep them-
selves impotent for hundreds of years to come.
The dreadful thinness of the yarn places it in a
commanding position in the freak gallery which
contains the Petriana mendacity, the Six Hatters
lie, and such-like emaciated concoctions.
From Shem’s Country.
A letter from a British port in Asia :
Within view of the Monkey, and surrounded by his
yellow cousins, I want to talk WHITE AUSTRALIA. I
have always been a believer in the policy, and actual
contact with Asiatics in their own haunts has made me
rabid. I teach in a school which boasts about 300 of them
and their bastard cousins, the Eurasians.
The distant mountain is picturesque. It is only those
who are struggling up the sides who see the precipices
they walk beside, the chasm that yawns, or the boulder
that has slain. At a distance you cannot realise the situ-
ation you would be called upon to face, nor the conditions
arising from having among you, on an equal footing',
hordes of Asiatics. One or two of the spawn may, to
some tastes, appear picturesque—spectacular; but to
be in the midst of a whelming yellow or brown
flood, rolling its waves against the bulwarks
of our civilisation, flooding our public highways,
swarming in our public conveyances, threatening our
homes—that is the condition which the men and women
of our race should investigate and realise, before they
devote themselves to that strange philanthropy which is
so tender of the colored brother and sister, and so crimi-
nally ignorant of, and careless about, the terrible dan-
gers that threaten the women and children of our own
race. THERE IS NO COMMUNITY WHERE THE
ASIATICS EXIST IN ANY NUMBERS THAT THEY
HAVE NOT MADE THEMSELVES OFFENSIVE TO THE
WHITES.
Behind the adamantine resolve that the Australian
people shall for ever be a white people is an instinct that
few have analysed—the instinct for the preservation of
species. Preservation of species is one of the strongest
laws of nature. The lion does not mate with the tiger.
Race aversion is simply the unconscious recognition of
this creational difference, and the normal attitude to-
wards a mingling of the two races should be one of in-
stinctive animal revolt. Because some have told the
brown man, “You are the equal of the white man,” the
object most desired by him is the forbidden, the un-
attainable white woman. It is time those people knew
better. Let them hear the warning while it is uttered in
a still small voice. “The white woman is the coveted
desire of the brown man ; the despoiling of the white
woman is his chosen vengeance.”
The all-pervasiveness of the Eurasian, not by ones or
twos, but by the thousand—the Eurasian with his equality
insolence, his odiferous person, and his criminal ten-
dencies—is a factor for danger and discomfort that must
be held in check by the most far-seeing intelligence.
Right to associate with the white is the goal to which the
Asiatic strives. It is for this very reason that many a
Jap woman holds herself more honored in being the mis-
tress of some white man than in being the honest wife of
a good colored man. For the same reason the bastard
Eurasian, instead of being taught to be ashamed of his
whiter skin, which is proof of his disgraceful origin that
all the world may read, is proud of the white blood
which is there only through infamy and vileness. The
Eurasian child of infamy looks down with scorn upon the
pure-blooded Asiatic.
Miscegenation is a crime against two races. The
Eurasian is certainly no improvement on the white race.
Nor is he any improvement on the Asiatic of the best
type. Nature marks him for extermination by making
him physiologically inferior to both races, with far less
endurance and vitality, according to statistics, more
susceptible to disease especially tuberculosis and
insanity.
♦
Natal, which long ago adopted the coolie immi-
gration policy which the Bruce Smith party
recommends as the means whereby Australia is to
be saved, is stirring heavily in its sleep. The
voter there can do comparatively little, merely as
a voter, on account of an amazing system of gerry-
mandering, whereby the comparatively progressive
town of Durban has one member for each 1499
voters, while Alfred County, which returns a
Tory Minister, has one for every 195 voters. But
about half the voters have signed a petition which
is calculated to make the Moor Cabinet think
that something has hit it fairly hard, and they say
that they will find some way of ending the present
political fake. Some of the grievances which the
petition sets forth are as follows :
(a) The land is in the hands of the few, and largely
uncultivated and unproductive.
(b) The incidence of taxation is manifestly and
grievously unjust.
(c) Under a policy of drift there has arisen an absolute
confusion in the relations of the white and black popula-
tion, which is yearly intensifying.
(d) Asiatic immigration has become a serious danger
to the Colony. The Asiatic population now consider-
ably out-numbers the white population, and is rapidly
increasing.
(e) There has been gross extravagance in our State ex-
penditure, rendering our financial position one of grave
embarrassment, and the future of our Colony a cause
of great disquietude, if not alarm.
(f) Our white population is rapidly decreasing.
The situation suggests an old scriptural remark
slightly altered—what shall it profit a white man
if he gains 100,000 cheap niggers and loses himself ?

"THE CHILDREN'S FOOD.”
“D.J.” complains :
Sydney Sunday Times printed this slab of painful
foolishness (26/4/08)
|> if Winston Churchill’s name were something else, he
would have no place among the world’s prominent per-
It surely constitutes about the limit in the way of dirt-
cheap gibes. As a fact, Churchill is, apart from his name
oranv other adventitious circumstance, (1) a born war-
correspondent. The Morning Post boomed while his
S’ African letters where appearing. Rival sheets also had
their Debretted employees; but these cut no ice
whatever— for the simple reason that they couldn’t
write. Churchill*—the Churchill of to-day—is (2) a
recognised historical authority on the Victorian period,
and, to boot, a masterly handler of fine English prose.
His biography of his father has been accepted by critics
in a dozen languages, as a work ranking near Morley’s
“Gladstone.” He is (3) one of the most effective, if not
the most effective, of debaters on his side in the House of
Commons. As one result, although starting with the
Joint handicaps of youth and extreme personal unpopu-
larity, he made good so astoundingly in his Under-
yeoretaryship as to wholly overshadow his chief, Lord
Elgin ; and to render his elevation to Cabinet rank a
oertnintv in any reconstructed Ministry. He is (4)
admittedly the best platform man with a crowd-
rough or smooth —barring a couple or three of the Nation-
alist members, and classing Chamberlain as out of
the running— in the United Kingdom ; and (5) a
travel, novel, social and political writer of no mean
order. To suggest that a man of this mental calibre
depends on his name for his fame is surely un-
worthy nonsense. It is the class of jeer the Tory gutter
press of England ceased hurling at him years and years
ago- .

THE BANK OF ADELAIDE
FORTY-SECOND ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS TO THE SHAREHOLDERS.
CHE Directors have pleasure in submitting herewith for fche information of Shareholders the
Balance-sheet of the Bank as on 30th March, 1908; also Statements of Profit and Loss Account
and the Reserve Fund on that date.
The balance to credit of Profit and Loss is , ... ... ...£59,014 6 0
Which it is proposed to appropriate as follows :
To Reserve Fund (making it £305,000) £20.000 0 0
Dividend at the rate of 9 per cent, per annum ... 18,000 0 0
38,000 0 0
Leaving a balance of ... ...£21,014 6 0
to be carried forward.
An Interim Dividend at the rate of 9 per cent, per annum was paid in November last.
During the year from 31st March, 1907, Owen and Freeling Agencies have been converted into
Branches, and Agencies have been opened at Edwardstown, Morgan, Parrakie, and Clarendon.
A desire having been expressed by some of the Shareholders at the last Annual Meeting that the
Board should give consideration to the establishment of an Officers’ Provident Fund, a scheme for
that purpose has been drawn up, and will be submitted to the Shareholders at a Special Meeting to be
held at the close of the business of the General Annual Meeting.
The General Annual Meeting will be held at No. 25 Waymouth-street, Adelaide, on Wednes-
day, 6th May, 1908, at 11.30 a.m., at which the Shareholders will be called upon to elect Two Direc-
tors in place of James Harvey, Esq., J.P., and the Honorable Sir Jenkm Coles, K.C.M.G., M.P.,
who retire by rotation, but are eligible for re-election, and offer themselves accordingly. The Share-
holders will also have to elect Two Auditors for the ensuing year, and fix the remuneration of the
retiring Auditors.
By order of the Board of Directors,
JOHN SHIELS, Manager.

and is instantaneous start, top, and fly back. The
Chronograph is only in use when wanted—the
ordinary works keep the time of day. It is a fully
jewelled movement, non-magnetic, direct quick train,
compensation balance, polished steel regulator, and
perfectly finished in every detail. It is wound
and set without opening the case, and the

TO KEEP YOU SICK!
rjIHE first impulse of persons who find that they are ailing in any way is to
JL plunge into a chemist’s and purchase a box of pills or a bottle of some patent
medicine. . . Failing to get more than temporary relief from this they then
consult a doctor, and he writes a prescription which more than likely calls for the
same medicine, only in a different form from what the patient has already taken.
The same result, generally. Next, the patient comes to the conclusion that this
doctor didn’t diagnose the case right, and then goes to another, with the same
result. And so on.
Oup modern medical system is a foolish system. We practically pay
our doctors to keep us ill. Their prosperity depends not on our health,
BUT ON OUR SICKNESS! . . . To-day very little is known of medicine
and its use. The wisest doctor can do but little more than clear out
the system or accelerate the action of the heart. When he has done these
things he has reached the end. MEDICINE CANNOT MAKE STRENGTH.
Now, just study yourself for a moment. The failure or breaking down of any
organic function means that it has not strength to do its work. If there was any
nourishment in medicine it might bring about a healthy change, but you know there
is not. Drugs, medicines, stimulants, narcotics and antidotes are POISONS, not
food. Food is needed to build up new energy. Electricity is nerve food, nerve life.
No pain or debility can exist in a body charged with Electric Life; you can have
no Weakness, no Inactive Parts, no Rheumatism, Indi-
gestion, Constipation or Kidney Trouble, if you fill your
body with it, because the life generated by this grand force gives health and
strength to every organ, and
Dr. MCLAUGHLIN S ELECTRIC BEET
is the newest and most successful method of applying it. It is a series of small batteries which you wear about your waist.
** worßs while you sheep, and generates its power constantly. It is worn as comfortably as your clothing,
adjusted in a minute, and made as strong or mild as you like by the pressure of a finger upon the regulator. With it
you can carry the current direct to the spot affected. You can get the full current at your ankles, feet, arms, shoulders,
head or any other part of your body. It has a Special Attachment for men. We have fixed a price for it which puts it
within the reach of everyone. Its price is as low as 5c.l in many cases.

“ Mate ” : Enthusiasm for the colored
alien (B. 12/3/’08) is not confined to the British
female. The pious .of both sexes are badly
afflicted—unless they live right up against a
color question as in the Southern United States.
I was in one ship on this coast that carried a large
and flabby nigger cook. He was an object of very
special attention to a number of reverend
brothers—deacons and such. Don’t know the
denomination, but they seemed to run to grey
whiskers, a cheerful pessimism, and many mis-
applied pious expressions. They feted this
nigger at their tabernacles in various ports,
where he warmed their hearts with prayerful but
hysterical bellowings. They gave teas and suppers
with edifying embellishments, and invited black
brother. He was slobbered over by their wives
and daughters at great length. He used to dis-
cuss their charms with a weak-minded mess-room
boy in a style that would have made them one big
blush for the rest of their lives—had they known.
He was fired out as being too dirty for endurance,
and for weeks after his wild, black exit our sus-
ceptible mate used to put on a clean collar and
his best smile to try and make an impression on
the fair ones who came to inquire for Sambo.
“ J.D.R.” : Paragraph 6 of the memo-
randum concerning the proposed defence organisa-
tion says : “ It is proposed that all N.C.O’s and
officers shall pass through the ranks.” Hooray!
The corps to which I gave my valuable services
was officered, to a great extent, by bank clerks,
who would no sooner purchase their pretty uni’-
forms than they would receive notice from the
bank to rush off to some remote branch, so that
the corps was never sure how many officers it pos-
sessed, or who they were. This occurred time
after time, yet no effort was ever made to fill the
vacancies with non-com. officers who were situ-
ated permanently in the place, and had been 15
years in the service. Another Augustus would
be brought in, and so the thing went on. The
consummation of Prime Minister Deakin’s scheme
should have the effect of bringing the genuine,
natural Soldier out on top.

BRONCHITIS
CURE.
The Famous Remedy For
Coughs, Bronchitis, Pneumonia,
Pleurisy, Asthma & Consumption
BRONCHITIS and PNEUMONIA
Cured by
HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
After other Treatment had Failed.
Mr. W. G. Hearne. Dear Sir, —From a strict sense
of duty I feel that I should publish the following state-
ment, so that others may know what to do when the
awful fact is evident that a life is in danger of being
lost:—ln September, 1906, my little girl, aged at that
time 3 years, contracted Measles, and in the following
October was attacked by Bronchitis, Pneumonia and
Congestion of the Lungs. She was attended by a
legally qualified doctor of high standing, but his treat-
ment was not successful in arresting the progress of the
illness. On Saturday, the 21st October, 1906, he said
that her life was in danger—that there was very little
hope for her. For eight days and nights she had been
prostrated by Cough, Pain and Fever, and was lying
like a statue, unconscious. At this stage I was per-
suaded by a friend to obtain Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure,
with its auxiliary medicine for the Fever and Conges-
tion of the Lungs, as directed in the Catalogue of
Medicines which accompanies each bottle of the Bron-
chitis Cure. I gave the medicine as directed, and there
was an improvement from the first dose of Hearne’s
Bronchitis Cure. The improvement continued after
each dose of the medicine. In a week she was perfectly
free from the Pneumonia, Congestion, Cough, Pain and
Fever, and was well, except that she was still weak. In
a fortnight she was quite recovered, and is now in
splendid health, and stronger than ever. Any person
asking for information about this grand medicine can
be supplied by me, or by any of my neighbours who have
witnessed its wonderful effects. It absolutely snatched
my child from an early grave.—Yours gratefully,
_ D. GARDINER,
Police Station, Geelong East, Feb. 5, 1908.

PNEUMONIA and PLEURISY
cured by
HEARNE'S BRONCHITIS CURE
After other Treatment had Failed.
I, Prudence M‘Kee, of Carr-street, South Geelong, in
view of the importance of a person making it quite clear
what treatment was successful in curing a serious and
complicated case when the medicine, directions and treat-
ment of a legally qualified doctor had failed, state as
follows
My son, Henry M‘Kee, then aged 8 years, had been
attended by a legally qualified doctor, who pronounced
him to be suffering from Pneumonia, Pleurisy and a
stoppage of the passing of Urine. Under the doctor’s
treatment the child gradually got worse, and the doctor
pronounced the case hopeless. He told me that the
child could not live. At this stage I obtained from
Mr. W. G. Hearne, Chemist, of Geelong, a bottle of
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure, and gave it to the child, ac-
cording to the directions which accompany each bottle
of it. The child improved after the second dose of
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure. He continued to improve
each day from each dose of Hearne’s Medicine alone, and
within three days he was free from the Cough, Pneu-
monia and the Pleurisy, and the Urine was passing satis-
factorily. He was out of bed at the end of a week,
completely recovered, and he is now in perfect health.
PRUDENCE M’KEE,
Carr-street, South Geelong, Feb. 6, 1908.
CONSUMPTION.
Too 111 to Leave His Bed.
A Complete Cure.
Mr. W. G. Hearne.
Dear Sir, —I am writing to tell yeu about the wonder-
ful cure your medicine has effected in my case. About
three years ago I began to cough. At first the cough
was not severe, but it gradual,,, got worse, and I became
very weak and troubled with night sweats, pain in my
chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On several oc-
casions there was blood in the expectorated matter. I
had been treated by a doctor who pronounced my case to
be consumption, and various other treatments had been
tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage that I
heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for a
course of the medicine. When it arrived, I was too ill
to leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually imDroved. I am glad to say that the two
lots of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure,
for which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR,
Westminster Bridge-road, S.E., London.
BRONCHITIS and PLEURISY.
A Severe Case Cured by Two Bottles of
Hearne'S Bronchitis Cure.
After Other Treatment had Failed.
Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong.
Dear Sir, —Some months ago, in Sydney, I suffered
from a severe attack of influenza, and was confined to
my room for about a week, at the end of which time,
feeling somewhat better, I got up and tried to transact
my business as usual. But I got up too soon, for the
very next day I had a relapse, and suffered tortures from
what the doctor told me was pleurisy and bronchitis.
The pain from the former in my chest and shoulders
was frightful, and for four long weeks I was confined to
my bed under the care of a well-known Sydney doctor,
and all the time his medicine gave me but temporary
relief. The landlady of the hotel (the Cleveland), where
I resided, told me of a medicine—Hearne’s Bronchitis
Cure—from Victoria, which had cured her of a bad at-
tack of bronchitis and pains in the chest, and begged
of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks and gratitude
to you, tell you that, after the second bottle, my cough
had ceased; but what is more astonishing, the pains from
pleurisy entirely left me, and in about a week I was able
to attend to my duties as usual.—Yours faithfully,
, J. BRAHAM.
Melbourne “Punch” Cfifice, Melbourne.
ASTHMA-A 17 YEARS CASE.
Previous Treatment Failed.
Cured by Three Bottles,
Mr. Alex. J. Anderson, of Oak Park, Charleville,
Queensland, wrote:—“After suffering from asthma for
seventeen years, and having been under a great many
different treatments without benefit, I was induced to try
Hearne’s Medicine for Asthma. After taking three
bottles of this medicine I quite got rid of the asthma,
and since then, which was the beginning of 1883 —fifteen
years ago—l have not had the slightest return of it.
The medicine quite cured me, and I have much pleasure
in recommending it.”
Speaking in February, 1908, he states:—“l am keeping
very well. Never have the slightest return of the asthma.”
“ Hearne’s Medicine cured me of Asthma, from which
T had been suffering for twenty-five years, during which
time I had used almost every patent medicine on the
market—including asthma inhalations—without getting a
cure. It was 8 years ago that the cure was effected
by Hearne’s Medicine, and I now feel stronger than I
have felt for years—in fact. I f'-'-l r-lendid.”
C. WISEMAN.
Meredith, Victoria.
Beware of Imitations! The great success of HEARNE’S Bronchitis Cure has induced a number of*
unprincipled persons to make imitations, each calling his medicine “ Bronchitis Cure,” with the object of deceiving
the unsuspecting, and so getting a sale for an imitation which has none of the beneficial effects that HEARNE’S
Bronchitis Cure has. Consequently it has become necessary to draw your attention to this fact, and to request you
in your own interests to be particular to ask for HEARNE’S, ana see tnat you get it.

HEADACHE
A Prostrated Postmaster’s
Prompt and Perfect Cure.
ALIi headaches are traceable either to stomach
weakness, which lets the stomach become
“ run-down,” or to liver disorder, which
deranges bile secretion. To be a sufferer from
headache for a space of only half-an-hour is
very often a terrible experience. Most people
are content with the belief that a headache is
only a headache, and a thing when it comes
to be borne with as much patience and for-
bearance as the severity of the case may call
for. Bile Beans banish headaches, as they do
all other symptoms of digestive disorder,
by correcting the liver and stomach in a
natural manner by natural means, for
Bile Beans are compounded solely from
Nature’s finest vegetable remedies. Bile
Beans will cure a sick headache in a day,
and a regular course will permanently
remove the cause, banishing sick head-
aches from your life altogether.
“ I am never without a supply of Bile Beans in
my house,” says Mr. John Bath, Postmaster, of
Queenton, Queensland, “for they are a splendid
family medicine. Some time ago I retired to rest
one night feeling quite well, but was awakened in
the early morning with a fearful headache. My
head felt as though it had been crushed in a vice,
and I could not bear it to touch the pillow, being
forced to get up and sit in an armchair. Having a supply of Bile Beans in the house, I
took three of them, and was quickly relieved of the agonising pain. In the morning I was
my old self again, and able to attend to my duties as usual, which I am sure I would
have been unable to do but for Bile Beans. I have recommended Bile Beans to others,
and for the past four years they have been installed as my family medicine.”

“Ye betther go aisy now,” cautions O’Shea.
“Ifth’boss”
His remonstrance is cut short by an indignant
“Whaffor?” And he turns to meet the immovable
countenance of the Chinese foreman.
“Whaffor?” he again demands, now of the
woman, who brazenly replaces the stolen property
and contemptuously replies:
“ Oh, a few bits of third-rate kauri—wot a’ell
of a row you’re makin’ about it, ole plait-’ead.”
“Whaffor?” demands the Chinese of O’Shea.
“Oi don’t knaw nothin’. Oi’m not fitted with
oyes behint. Why don’t ye emphloy a tally-mahn ?
It ud be chaper in th’ ind.”
But the Chinese has penetrated the yard. The
lady, a picture of outraged honesty, gives varied
and conflicting accounts of the plunder.
“Isn’t me wurrd good enough?” she exclaims
shrilly. “This chap give me th’ timber! It’s
’im wants rousin’ on, not me—a wumman. Well,
here it is straight: Me old bloke bought the stuff
a month ago ! ! ”
She lies shamelessly, indifferent to the fact that
her torrent of fiction deceives no one. Then the
back door opens, and a Chinese with a face like
a blotched and withered water-melon shuffles
through it.
The first heathen hastens to explain the trouble.
He of the melon-face advances and clutches the
woman’s arm :
“No goot—no goot, you ! ”
A torrent of unpublishable abuse follows —
abuse in which the words “polis,” “ plison,”
mingle quaintly with the names of strange gods—
abuse so blasphemous that O’Shea furtively crosses
himself, and the eyes of the morose black horse
exude tears.
The Chinese continues to descend with violence
upon his “wife.” Then he swoops low, and
catching up a half-caste baby, till now a silent
spectator of the scene, crowds it, bawling, upon
her, turns her face gatewards, raises his skinny
claw in a dramatic curse, and bundles her into the
lane.
“Shteady now !” remonstrates O’Shea.
“Shuttee up!” snarls the Chow. “She my
plopetty.”
Then he falls upon the boards of contention,
huddles them pell-mell into Lee Sing’s yard, and
locks himself into his own.
The woman, when she tires of calling insults
through the crack in the fence, picks the hair from
her eyes and sails majestically round to the front
entrance. This, open for business, offers no bar
to her ingress. She enters with triumph in her
eye, and the watermelon’s high-handed action is
rendered null and void.
“She’s a strong joint, isn’t she?” remarks a
passing girl to O’Shea, indicating slightingly the
reinstated one.
O’Shea has no answer ready, and the girl,
young and stylish in the millinery of Upper
Bourke-street, passes on to visit Mrs. Ah Goon,
whose offspring, the slant-eyed Albert and Vic-
toria Ah Goon, receive their aunt’s caresses in the
stoical manner inherited from “papa.”
O’Shea is curious regarding her, and the fore-
man, who has eyed her with a reflected air of
proprietorship, volunteers that she is the affianced
of Ah Sing, Lee Sing’s kinsman, and that “ she
live ’ere plesently ; mally Ah Sing bimeby.”
O’Shea is understood to doubt it. The China-
man is surprised.
“No mally Ah Sing?” he queries. “ P’laps
mally Wah Fat —allee same. Ah Sing find plenty
more gel. He lot money. He lot order for
wa’dlobe. He go China—yes—b.meby. Me no
gell ” (he becomes coy). “ Too poll ! ”
O’Shea makes no reply, and the Chinaman
goes on :
“ She mally Chinaman all li. She wive soon,
samee siste’ !” he adds, with conviction, pointing
fraternally to the European countenance of Mrs.
Ah Goon.
Further interest in the mingling of the races is
postponed by the approach of two well-tailored
men, whose air of silent authority causes the
citizens uneasiness.
They peer into doorways and over fences, and
gaze inquisitorially at inscrutable makers of rotten
washstands and broken-winded sofas destined for
the suburban time-payment stores.
O’Shea’s conscience, seldom without a sense of
guilt, has qualms also. He surmises them to be
connected with the Law, and liis thoughts hark
back repentantly to a recent episode in his career
in which he, a moonless night, a sack of potatoes,
and a silent, well-greased wheelbarrow were the
actors.
“Are they inshpector-rs ?” asks O’Shea, trem-
blingly. John’s evidence is not corroborative :
“ I de-no !” he answers, cheerfully.
“ Are they d’s ? ”
“ I de-no 1 ”
“ Ever seen ’em before ? ”
“ I de-no ! ”
“ Anny of yez wanted fer annythin’ ? ”
“ I de-no 1 ”
“Fer lottery tickets,- fer fan-tan, er marking
theearrds?”
“ I de-no ! ”
O’Shea gives up. From every window in
Punch’s Lane a shaven head projects, watching
the departing intruders. The latter turn to re-
view the scene. Every Chinese poll turns swiftly
as on one neck, and gazes indifferently in the
opposite direction.
They are rewarded, for up Little Bourke-street,
watch-housewards, comes a straggling procession.
Bare-legged guttersnipes head it, walking back-
wards and tumbling in the manner of acrobats.
Loafers fringe it, dogs bark at it, and blue-bloused
Chinese deride it.
The nucleus of the group is formed by a perspir-
ing policeman hauling along a battered daughter
of Little Bourke-street alleys. The “ trap ”is
having trouble with his case. Her objections to
the lockup are loud and very strenuous, and at every
other step she sheds some portion of her raiment.
The ease with which she accomplishes this sug-
gests to one of the bounding vanguard the making
of a “ book.”
“Wot’ll yer bet sue loses’em all be the time
she’s in ther jug ? ” he asks of a Catherine-wheel
turning beside him.
“ All wot ? ” inquires the wheel.
“’Er duds —clobber.”
An old derelict in the marine trade affords
Punch’s its next entertainment. He buys bottles,
and, lacking hard cash, pays for his purchases in
song. So obliging is he that when “the ladies”
happen to be out of bottles he pays, in the same
coin, for the promise of future bottles. Gems
from the comic operas follow one another in quick
succession, but “The Heart Bowed Down” sad-
dens O’Shea, and reduces him to sentimental
despair. Then the bottle merchant tries his
powers of invention. He narrates a tale of such
harrowing pathos that O’Shea goes to Lee Sing’s
office, intent on borrowing a small coin from that
dignitary himself with which to appease the
ravages hunger has made ’neath the draughty
waistcoat of the decayed artist.
When O'Shea returns empty-handed from his
errand the artist is discernible in the distance,
lie stays not for explanation. He wears O’Shea’s
coat. He has O’Shea’s tobacco in his pocket, and
he sprints for crowded streets and safety.
And as O’Shea lifts up his voice in malediction
an unimpassioned Chinese voice behind him says:
“ Whaffor ?”
VIC.
WEEROONA.
A Love Ilyll.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GUS used to tell people he only worked
so as to wear out his old clothes.
(His dad never was seen in anything else.)
Gus entertained all the First Flight girls he
could stand, and who would stand him. They
knew he had plenty of money and didn't need to
toil. He told them so.
Gus first met Lola at a Dance—as distinguished
from a Darnce. When Lola and he had finished
noting atmospheric phenomena, they found they
also agreed on other points. Gus repeated ancient
history. Inside evens, Gus had told Lola more of
the things he hadn’t done (but which he would have
liked to do, and which he said he had done) than he
had any girl during the entire preceding fortnight.
Lola’s dad was president of the Anti-Everything
League and the local Progress Association, and
wore a 62in. vest. She had command of the
64 h.p., the brougham, and also the private han-
som, but preferred to ride in trams, and, in
between times, she walked. Gus was so fond of
walking.
Gus’s great anxiety was how to give her a good
time on 30s. per week (plus spiffs). He took to
repudiating his board as a matter of habit.
Though far from proud of pa, Gus would now
and then lower himself about this time so far as
to accept a small grant in aid whenever dad got
work at the tannery. And yet the adored of Lola
was on the rocks not seldom.
Sometimes the happy couple would go up stream
for a quiet afternoon. After explaining to Lola
how difficult it was to get good tenants, and that
the price of debentures was below par, he would
tell her what she was to have, nevertheless, when
the time came. Then they would select a green
spot and rest in the shade, keeping close together
lest something might come between them. Lola
would do the castle-building act, while Gus figured
out mentally how many times he would have to
walk to work till next salary day.
Gus had a credulous tailor, and was well sup-
plied with tweeds and serges. After a while he
started forgetting to give customers their change,
and the mean head rang a marked coin into him.
Gus got fired. He saw Lola, and did the George
Washington turn. He was a liar, and admitted
it modestly. Lola took the news like a lamb.
She was a sensible girl. Dad allowed her
£5OO a year for clothes and things, and she was
the only issue. She could afford to be generous.
Before saying the word she considered the situa-
tion. She was of age, and could marry at once if
she wanted to. She didn’t. Lola shared her for-
tune in the spring with another youth who had a
real income. Gus now spends his time dodging the
credulous tailor, and the church money feels as
lonely as ever in his hip pocket.
O. B. S.
Yap.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The German-Dnteh cable terminates at the island of Yap
in the South Pacific.— News Item.
SWEPT were the mantling miles aside,
A Spirit touched my dreaming eyes,
And mighty Yap arose in pride
Her scattered sons to mobilise.
They hustled from the ends of earth
To gather in their mother’s lap ;
And fill the land that gave them birth—
The wond’rous, thund’rous isle of Yap.
Reverberating from that shore
Rise woeful bowls from every beach ;
The Spell-binders in fury roar
With none to listen to their speech.
The petrels and the oysters care
For oratory not one scrap ;
There are no daily papers there,
There are no Hansard men in Yap.
But all outsiders keep away ;
The bold head-hunter steers his craft
To port, when awful noises play
Upon the breeze that chases aft;
The mariner who fears no foe
Marks off that island on the map,
And shudders when he has to go
Within a hundred miles of Yap.
I ventured close and saw a sight
That made my very blood run cold;
Upon a scarp that he did smite
In punctuation, Judkins bold
Made charges vague ; while, standing near,
Each with his face an open gap.
Were Woodfull, Worrall and Tregear
Vibrating hard the air of Yap.
Above a beach the breezes blew
The steady voice of Johnny Wren,
Proclaiming loud he was the true
And only friend of working men ;
I heard from Johnson’s shrill sad shriek
How Federation was a trap ;
While on a distant mountain peak
Neild stilled volcanic fires in Yap.
Carruthers yelled and so did Wade
In frenzy re Canberra’s site ;
Joe Cook in buzz-saw accents frayed
The palm leaves on a distant height;
Bruce Smith in pompous diction talked
About our brave ally, the Jap ;
While practising his “ Oxford ” stalked
Small Kelly on the Isle of Yap.
It was, indeed, a sight accursed ;
It sent my blood all curdling through
My shaking veins—but still the worst
Is that the picture isn’t true.
Not yet Australia’s air is clear
Of insect pests—by some mishap
Australia’s enemies are here
And not marooned on sunny Yap.
N.S.W.
H. GLUBB.
